Divine Attributes
beginner
6 min
Classification of Divine Attributes
The author defines sifat al-ma'ani and classifies attributes into existential, negative, and psychological.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians classify divine attributes into existential, negative, and psychological categories, and the distinction between eternal and contingent attributes.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are either existential, negative, or psychological.

This passage from al-Kharida establishes the foundational classification of God's attributes, known as sifat al-ma'ani (attributes of meanings). The author defines these as existential attributes subsisting in God's essence, then presents a threefold division that organizes all divine attributes and clarifies the difference between attributes of God and created attributes. Understanding this classification is essential for avoiding anthropomorphism and maintaining tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read and translate
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read its literal English translation. Let the author's words establish the foundation.
Notice the technical terms: صفات المعاني, الصفات الوجودية, etc.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage presents three kinds of attributes. Find each one and note the logical condition that defines it.
The classification is based on existence and necessity.
3
Clarify negative attributes
Pay attention to the correction about negative attributes. They do not mean God lacks attributes, but negate what is unbecoming.
This prevents misunderstanding.
4
Distinguish eternal vs. temporal
Note the final distinction: eternal attributes are not called accidents, while temporal ones are.
This guards against comparing God's attributes to created ones.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيجب عليك معرفة الصفات المسماة بالمعاني؛ لأن كل واحدة منها معنى قائم بذاته تعالى. ومرادهم بصفات المعاني الصفات الوجودية؛ أي التي لها وجود في نفسها، قديمة كانت أو حادثة؛ كعلمه وقدرته تعالى، وكعلمنا وقدرتنا، والبياض والسواد. والحاصل: أن الصفات إن كانت وجودية سميت صفات معان، وإن لم تكن وجودية، فإن كان مدلولها عدم أمر لا يليق سميت سلبية، وإن لم يكن مدلولها عدما، فإن كانت واجبة للذات ما دامت الذات غير معللة بعلة سميت صفة نفسية وحالا نفسية؛ كالوجود. وليس المراد بكونها سلبية أنها مسلوبة عن الله ومنفية عنه، بل المراد بكونها سلبية أن كل واحدة منها سلبت أمرا لا يليق به جل وعز. وهذا التعريف للمعاني من حيث هي كانت لقديم أو حادث، وحينئذ فالفرق بين صفات القديم والحادث أن صفات القديم قديمة، ولا تسمى أعراضا، وصفات الحادث حادثة وتسمى أعراضا.
It is incumbent upon you to know the attributes called 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani), because each one of them is a meaning subsisting in His Essence, exalted is He. And what they mean by 'attributes of meanings' is the existential attributes (al-sifat al-wujudiyya): that is, those which have existence in themselves, whether eternal or temporally originated; like His knowledge and power, exalted is He, and like our knowledge and our power, and whiteness and blackness. The upshot: that attributes, if they are existential, are called 'attributes of meanings'; and if they are not existential, then if their denotation is the absence of a matter unbecoming, they are called 'negative attributes' (sifāt salbiyya); and if their denotation is not an absence, then if they are necessary for the essence as long as the essence exists, not caused by a cause, they are called 'psychological attribute' (sifa nafsiyya) and 'psychological state' (ḥāl nafsī); like existence. And what is meant by their being negative is not that they are negated from God and denied of Him, but rather what is meant by their being negative is that each one of them negates a matter unbecoming of Him, mighty and majestic. And this definition of 'attributes of meanings' applies whether they are for the eternal or the temporally originated. Accordingly, the difference between the attributes of the Eternal and the temporally originated is that the attributes of the Eternal are eternal and are not called 'accidents' (a'rāḍ), while the attributes of the temporally originated are temporally originated and are called 'accidents'.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the divine attributes. It begins by stating the importance of knowing the attributes called 'sifat al-ma'ani' (attributes of meanings), explaining that each is a meaning (ma'na) subsisting in God's essence. The author then clarifies that 'sifat al-ma'ani' refers to existential attributes (sifat wujudiyya)—those having real existence, whether eternal or contingent—giving examples from both God (knowledge, power) and creatures (knowledge, power, whiteness, blackness). Next, a threefold classification is given: (1) existential attributes are called sifat ma'ani; (2) if not existential, and their denotation is the absence of something unbecoming, they are called negative attributes (sifat salbiyya); (3) if not existential and not negative, but necessary for the essence as long as it exists, not caused, they are called psychological attributes (sifat nafsiyya) or psychological state (ḥāl nafsī), with existence as an example. The author then corrects a potential misunderstanding: negative attributes do not mean that God is stripped of attributes, but rather each such attribute negates an unbecoming quality (e.g., not being subject to origination). Finally, the definition of 'sifat ma'ani' applies to both eternal and contingent attributes, but with a crucial distinction: eternal attributes are eternal and not called 'accidents' (a'rad), whereas contingent attributes are temporally originated and are called accidents. This guards against anthropomorphic or univocal conceptions of God's attributes.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
الصفات الوجودية
الصفات السلبية
الصفات النفسية
الحال النفسي
الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negative attributes mean that God has no attributes or is stripped of attributes.
Correction: Negative attributes negate what is unbecoming of God, not the attributes themselves.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how are the three categories of attributes distinguished?
A) Existential, negative, and psychological based on existence and necessity.
B) Essential, negative, and relational based on relation to the essence.
C) Eternal, temporal, and accidental based on duration.
D) Essential, accidental, and mental based on mental existence.

Key Takeaway
The three categories—existential, negative, and psychological—cover all divine attributes and prevent both anthropomorphism and stripping God of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is powerful,' do you implicitly compare His power to yours, or do you truly believe His power is eternal and not an accident? How does this distinction change the way you rely on Him?
?
If 'negative attributes' merely negate what is unworthy, not strip God of attributes, then what specific unbecoming qualities must you actively deny in your heart when you contemplate God? Are there any subtle assumptions about God you need to uproot today?
?
The passage distinguishes between eternal attributes (not accidents) and created attributes (accidents). In your daily worship, do you ever treat God's attributes as if they were changeable or limited like human traits? What one step can you take to ensure your understanding remains pure?

Divine Attributes

Defining the Attribute of Meaning (al-Ṣifah al-Maʿnawiyyah)
Sanusi defines the attribute of meaning and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what makes an attribute an 'attribute of meaning' and how it differs from self-attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes of meaning are derived from inner causes (maʿānī) and are necessary for the essence due to those causes.

Sanusi begins by distinguishing attributes of meaning from other types. He provides a precise criterion: an attribute is called 'meaning-related' if it is caused by an internal meaning (like knowledge or power) and is necessary for the essence as long as that meaning exists. This lesson unpacks that definition with clear examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage from 'وغير صفات المعاني' to 'نسبة إلى المعاني'. Focus on the words 'معللة بعلة' and 'واجبةً للذات'.
The phrase 'ما دامت عدَّتُها' is technical; the commentary explains it.
2
Identify the examples
The passage gives two examples: 'العالمية' and 'القادرية'. Notice how these are relative to the meanings (knowledge and power).
3
Grasp the logical relationship
Understand that the essence is knowing because it has knowledge; the attribute of being knowing is derived from the meaning (knowledge). Thus, the attribute is 'meaning-related'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغير صفات المعاني وكالتَّحيُّز للجرم وقبوله للأعراض، وإن كانت معللة بعلة بأن كانت واجبةً للذات ما دامت عدَّتُها سميت معنوية ; كالعالمية والقادرية; أي : كون الذَّات المتّصفة بالعلم عالمة، وكونُ الذَّات المتّصفة بالقدرة قادرة، نسبة إلى المعاني، وهي ( سبعة للرَّائي) أي : الناظر المتأمل، ثمَّ فسَّرها بقوله : العلم (أي: عِلْمُه) وما عطف عليه (المحيط بالأشياء) كلّها، واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها، فليس مراده بالأشياء الموجودات فقط كما هو المتعارف عندهم .
And other than the attributes of meanings, and such as spatial occupancy (al-taḥayyuz) for the body and its acceptance of accidents. And if it is caused by a cause, in that it is necessary for the essence as long as its [the cause's] number [exists], it is called an attribute of meaning (ma'nawiyyah); such as 'the attribute of being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyyah) and 'the attribute of being powerful' (al-qādiriyyah); i.e., the essence described by knowledge being knowing, and the essence described by power being powerful, relative to the meanings (al-maʿānī). And they are seven for the theologian (al-rāʾī), i.e., the contemplative observer. Then he explained them by his saying: knowledge (i.e., His knowledge) and what is conjoined to it (encompassing all things), their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones. So he does not mean by 'things' only existents, as is customary among them.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of meaning (al-ṣifah al-maʿnawiyyah) and begins its enumeration. The commentator first clarifies that the example 'spatial occupancy for the body' (al-taḥayyuz li-l-jirm) is an example of a self-attribute. Then he transitions to attributes of meanings: if an attribute is caused by a cause (muʿallala bi-ʿillah)—i.e., it is necessary for the essence as long as that cause exists—then it is called a meaning-attribute (maʿnawiyyah). The examples given are 'being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyyah) and 'being powerful' (al-qādiriyyah), which are attributed to the essence because the essence possesses inner meanings (maʿānī) like knowledge and power. These attributes are 'relative to the meanings' (nisbah ilā al-maʿānī), meaning they are derived from those internal meanings. The author then states that these attributes are seven for 'the theologian' (al-rāʾī), i.e., the one who reflects. He begins to list them by saying: 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm), i.e., His knowledge, and what is conjoined to it (the other six). Then he emphasizes that knowledge encompasses all things—both necessary (wājib), possible (jāʾiz), and impossible (mustaḥīl)—so it is not restricted to existents only, as some may assume.

Key Terms
صفة معنوية
معاني
عالمية
قادرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think attributes of meanings are separate from the essence, but they are relative attributes derived from inner meanings.
Correction: Attributes of meaning are not separate; they are relative states of the essence caused by underlying meanings.

Checkpoint
What condition must an attribute meet to be called an attribute of meaning?
A) It is a self-attribute
B) It is caused by a cause and necessary for the essence
C) It is not caused by anything
D) It is the same as the essence

Key Takeaway
An attribute of meaning is derived from an inner cause (maʿnā) and is necessary for the essence due to that cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of your actions is a necessary attribute flowing from His essence, not an arbitrary decision, how does that change your view of His judgment? Will you still try to hide your sin or rush to repentance?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you truly believe that God's power (al-qudra) is inherent to Him and never diminishes? How does that belief manifest in your dua and reliance on Him?
?
If 'being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyyah) is a direct result of God's perfect knowledge, then He knows you completely. Does this comfort you or terrify you? What needs to change in your heart to make it comfort?

The Seven Attributes of Meaning and the Scope of Knowledge
Sanusi enumerates that the attributes of meaning are seven and explains the first one, knowledge, with its comprehensive scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know that the attributes of meaning are seven for the theologian, and that the first one—knowledge—encompasses all things, including necessary, possible, and impossible.

Core Idea
Knowledge encompasses not just existents but also the possible and impossible.

After defining the attribute of meaning, Sanusi states that there are seven such attributes for the theologian. He then begins to list them, starting with knowledge, and emphasizes that divine knowledge encompasses all things—not only what exists, but also what is possible and what is impossible. This lesson focuses on that enumeration and the comprehensive scope of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Note the number seven
Read the phrase 'وهي سبعة للرائي'. This tells us that the attributes of meaning are seven for the one who reflects.
The number seven is a well-known doctrine for Ash'ari theologians.
2
Identify the first attribute
The author then explains that he meant by 'knowledge' and what is conjoined to it. So the first attribute is knowledge.
The others will be mentioned later.
3
Understand the scope of knowledge
The passage says knowledge encompasses 'all things, their necessary, possible, and impossible'. This expands the typical definition.
This is a key Ash'ari teaching that knowledge is not limited to actual existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغير صفات المعاني وكالتَّحيُّز للجرم وقبوله للأعراض، وإن كانت معللة بعلة بأن كانت واجبةً للذات ما دامت عدَّتُها سميت معنوية ; كالعالمية والقادرية; أي : كون الذَّات المتّصفة بالعلم عالمة، وكونُ الذَّات المتّصفة بالقدرة قادرة، نسبة إلى المعاني، وهي ( سبعة للرَّائي) أي : الناظر المتأمل، ثمَّ فسَّرها بقوله : العلم (أي: عِلْمُه) وما عطف عليه (المحيط بالأشياء) كلّها، واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها، فليس مراده بالأشياء الموجودات فقط كما هو المتعارف عندهم .
And other than the attributes of meanings, and such as spatial occupancy (al-taḥayyuz) for the body and its acceptance of accidents. And if it is caused by a cause, in that it is necessary for the essence as long as its [the cause's] number [exists], it is called an attribute of meaning (ma'nawiyyah); such as 'the attribute of being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyyah) and 'the attribute of being powerful' (al-qādiriyyah); i.e., the essence described by knowledge being knowing, and the essence described by power being powerful, relative to the meanings (al-maʿānī). And they are seven for the theologian (al-rāʾī), i.e., the contemplative observer. Then he explained them by his saying: knowledge (i.e., His knowledge) and what is conjoined to it (encompassing all things), their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones. So he does not mean by 'things' only existents, as is customary among them.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of meaning (al-ṣifah al-maʿnawiyyah) and begins its enumeration. The commentator first clarifies that the example 'spatial occupancy for the body' (al-taḥayyuz li-l-jirm) is an example of a self-attribute. Then he transitions to attributes of meanings: if an attribute is caused by a cause (muʿallala bi-ʿillah)—i.e., it is necessary for the essence as long as that cause exists—then it is called a meaning-attribute (maʿnawiyyah). The examples given are 'being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyyah) and 'being powerful' (al-qādiriyyah), which are attributed to the essence because the essence possesses inner meanings (maʿānī) like knowledge and power. These attributes are 'relative to the meanings' (nisbah ilā al-maʿānī), meaning they are derived from those internal meanings. The author then states that these attributes are seven for 'the theologian' (al-rāʾī), i.e., the one who reflects. He begins to list them by saying: 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm), i.e., His knowledge, and what is conjoined to it (the other six). Then he emphasizes that knowledge encompasses all things—both necessary (wājib), possible (jāʾiz), and impossible (mustaḥīl)—so it is not restricted to existents only, as some may assume.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's knowledge only pertains to what exists.
Correction: This passage clarifies that knowledge encompasses all things, including possible and impossible.

B) Only necessary things

Allah's knowledge encompasses all things—necessary, possible, and impossible—not just existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah knows even the impossible thoughts that never cross your mind, what excuse do you have for the sins you commit in secret? How will you change your inner life starting today?
?
You claim to believe that Allah's knowledge encompasses everything. Yet do you truly live as if every intention, every fleeting thought, is seen by Him? What one hidden habit will you confront today because of this truth?
?
The lesson says Allah knows even the impossible—things that can never happen. How does that challenge your tendency to hide from God? Will you repent openly from a sin you thought was too small for His notice?

Causal Concomitance in Divine Attributes
Sanusi explains the example of spatial occupancy and redefines 'cause' as logical concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'cause' in the context of attributes of meanings refers to logical concomitance, not efficient causation, and how this applies to the example of spatial occupancy.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'cause' means 'concomitant' — the attribute is necessarily tied to the essence.

This passage of the Kharidat clarifies a crucial conceptual point: when we say that an essence is 'knowing by knowledge,' the causal language does not imply that knowledge produces knowing as an efficient cause. Rather, it means that being knowing is necessarily concomitant with having knowledge. Understanding this distinction is essential for accurate reasoning about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the terms jirm, tahayyuz, illah, malzum, and lazim in the passage. Notice how each is defined and how they relate to each other.
These are technical kalam terms that have specific meanings.
3
Understand the shift in meaning of 'cause'
The passage explicitly redefines 'cause' (al-illah) as the concomitant (al-malzūm) and 'effect' (al-maʿlūl) as the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim). Reflect on why this redefinition is important for avoiding confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وكالتَّحيُّز للجرم : المراد بالجرم ما قام بذاته، سواء كان جسماً أو جوهراً فرداً، والمراد بتحيزه أخذه قدراً من الفراغ، وفي تمثيل الشارح بالتحيز إشارة لما قلناه من أنَّ هذا في الصفة النفسية مطلقاً قديمة وحادثة. قوله : (أي: كون الذَّات المتّصفة بالعلم عالمة) : أي : فتكون عالمة معللة بالعلم ; أي: ملازمة له، فالمراد بالعلة الملزوم، والمراد بالمعلول اللازم. قوله : (نسبة إلى المعاني : مرتبط بقوله : (سمِّيت معنويَّة) . قوله : (وما عطف عليه دفع به ما يقال : إنَّ العلم وحده ليس تفسيراً للمعاني كلها . قوله : (واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر، تقديره : الشَّيء هو الموجود، فيقتضي قصر تعلُّق العلم على الموجودات، مع أنَّه يتعلق بالمعدومات أيضاً.
His saying: ('and such as spatial occupancy for the body'): what is meant by 'body' (jirm) is anything that subsists by itself, whether it is a body or an indivisible atom. And what is meant by its spatial occupancy (taḥayyuz) is its taking up a portion of space. In the author's example of spatial occupancy there is an indication to what we said, that this in the self-attribute applies absolutely, both eternal and originated. His saying: ('i.e., the essence described by knowledge being knowing'): i.e., then it becomes 'knowing' caused (muʿallala) by knowledge, i.e., concomitant with it. So what is meant by 'cause' (al-ʿillah) is the concomitant (al-malzūm), and what is meant by 'effect' (al-maʿlūl) is the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim). His saying: ('relative to the meanings') is connected to his saying: ('it is called meaning-attribute'). His saying: ('and what is conjoined to it') is to repudiate what is said: that knowledge alone is not an explanation of all the meanings. His saying: ('their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones') is an answer to an implied question, the estimation: 'thing' is the existent, which would require restricting the object of knowledge to existents, while it also relates to non-existents.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage provides several clarifications on the attributes of meanings. First, the commentator explains the example 'spatial occupancy for the body' (al-taḥayyuz li-l-jirm) which was mentioned earlier. He clarifies that 'body' (jirm) means anything that subsists by itself, whether a body or a single atom, and 'spatial occupancy' means occupying a portion of space. This example illustrates that self-attributes apply universally to both eternal and originated entities. Then he addresses the phrase 'the essence described by knowledge being knowing'. He explains that 'knowing' is caused (muʿallala) by knowledge, but 'cause' here means the concomitant (al-malzūm) and 'effect' means the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim). In other words, the attribute of being knowing is necessarily concomitant with the essence that possesses knowledge—not that knowledge efficiently produces the attribute. Next, he notes that the phrase 'relative to the meanings' (nisbah ilā al-maʿānī) connects to why it is called 'meaning-attribute'. Then he addresses a potential objection: if only knowledge is mentioned, it might imply that the other attributes are not included. The answer is that the phrase 'and what is conjoined to it' (wa-mā ʿuṭifa ʿalayhi) indicates the other attributes are also included. Finally, he explains that the phrase 'their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones' responds to an assumed objection: since 'thing' often means 'existent', one might think knowledge only pertains to existents. But the author explicitly includes the necessary, possible, and impossible, showing that knowledge encompasses non-existents as well.

Key Terms
جرم
تحيز
علة
ملزوم
لازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'cause' (علة) in 'معللة بالعلم' means efficient cause.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'cause' here means the concomitant (ملزوم) and 'effect' means the necessary concomitant (لازم), indicating a relationship of logical necessity, not efficient causation.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'العلة الملزوم' and 'المعلول اللازم'?
A) The cause is the effect, and the effect is the cause
B) The cause is the concomitant, and the effect is the necessary concomitant
C) The cause is the essence, and the effect is the attribute
D) The cause is the attribute, and the effect is the essence

Key Takeaway
In the attributes of meanings, the 'cause' is the essence with the attribute, and the 'effect' is the concomitant description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see events in your life—a job loss, an illness, a blessing—do you instinctively attribute them to secondary causes (luck, people, circumstances) or do you see them as directly concomitant with Allah's essence? What needs to change in your heart to shift from seeing efficient causes to seeing divine concomitance?
?
Sanusi redefines 'cause' as a logical tie to the essence. Think of a moment you felt your faith was disconnected from your daily reality. Was it because you treated Allah's attributes as distant efficient causes rather than as ever-present concomitant truths? How can you train your soul to live in constant awareness of this nearness?

The Scope of Divine Knowledge Includes Non-Existents
Sanusi clarifies that knowledge includes non-existents via the three rational categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine knowledge encompasses not only existents but also non-existents, including the possible, necessary, and impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge covers all that is possible, necessary, and impossible — including non-existents.

A potential misunderstanding arises when the word 'thing' (shay') is equated with 'existent' (mawjud). If that were the case, then knowledge would only pertain to existents. Sanusi clarifies that the attributes of meanings include the necessary, possible, and impossible among their objects, showing that divine knowledge extends to non-existents as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: an implied question, then the answer.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
The implied objection is that 'thing' means 'existent', which would limit knowledge to existents. Find the phrase that states this.
The estimation is given in the translation.
3
Understand the refutation
The author's addition of 'their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones' shows that knowledge includes non-existents. Reflect on why these three categories cover non-existents.

Source Text

قوله : (وكالتَّحيُّز للجرم : المراد بالجرم ما قام بذاته، سواء كان جسماً أو جوهراً فرداً، والمراد بتحيزه أخذه قدراً من الفراغ، وفي تمثيل الشارح بالتحيز إشارة لما قلناه من أنَّ هذا في الصفة النفسية مطلقاً قديمة وحادثة. قوله : (أي: كون الذَّات المتّصفة بالعلم عالمة) : أي : فتكون عالمة معللة بالعلم ; أي: ملازمة له، فالمراد بالعلة الملزوم، والمراد بالمعلول اللازم. قوله : (نسبة إلى المعاني : مرتبط بقوله : (سمِّيت معنويَّة) . قوله : (وما عطف عليه دفع به ما يقال : إنَّ العلم وحده ليس تفسيراً للمعاني كلها . قوله : (واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر، تقديره : الشَّيء هو الموجود، فيقتضي قصر تعلُّق العلم على الموجودات، مع أنَّه يتعلق بالمعدومات أيضاً.
His saying: ('and such as spatial occupancy for the body'): what is meant by 'body' (jirm) is anything that subsists by itself, whether it is a body or an indivisible atom. And what is meant by its spatial occupancy (taḥayyuz) is its taking up a portion of space. In the author's example of spatial occupancy there is an indication to what we said, that this in the self-attribute applies absolutely, both eternal and originated. His saying: ('i.e., the essence described by knowledge being knowing'): i.e., then it becomes 'knowing' caused (muʿallala) by knowledge, i.e., concomitant with it. So what is meant by 'cause' (al-ʿillah) is the concomitant (al-malzūm), and what is meant by 'effect' (al-maʿlūl) is the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim). His saying: ('relative to the meanings') is connected to his saying: ('it is called meaning-attribute'). His saying: ('and what is conjoined to it') is to repudiate what is said: that knowledge alone is not an explanation of all the meanings. His saying: ('their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones') is an answer to an implied question, the estimation: 'thing' is the existent, which would require restricting the object of knowledge to existents, while it also relates to non-existents.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage provides several clarifications on the attributes of meanings. First, the commentator explains the example 'spatial occupancy for the body' (al-taḥayyuz li-l-jirm) which was mentioned earlier. He clarifies that 'body' (jirm) means anything that subsists by itself, whether a body or a single atom, and 'spatial occupancy' means occupying a portion of space. This example illustrates that self-attributes apply universally to both eternal and originated entities. Then he addresses the phrase 'the essence described by knowledge being knowing'. He explains that 'knowing' is caused (muʿallala) by knowledge, but 'cause' here means the concomitant (al-malzūm) and 'effect' means the necessary concomitant (al-lāzim). In other words, the attribute of being knowing is necessarily concomitant with the essence that possesses knowledge—not that knowledge efficiently produces the attribute. Next, he notes that the phrase 'relative to the meanings' (nisbah ilā al-maʿānī) connects to why it is called 'meaning-attribute'. Then he addresses a potential objection: if only knowledge is mentioned, it might imply that the other attributes are not included. The answer is that the phrase 'and what is conjoined to it' (wa-mā ʿuṭifa ʿalayhi) indicates the other attributes are also included. Finally, he explains that the phrase 'their necessary ones, their possible ones, and their impossible ones' responds to an assumed objection: since 'thing' often means 'existent', one might think knowledge only pertains to existents. But the author explicitly includes the necessary, possible, and impossible, showing that knowledge encompasses non-existents as well.

جائز
مستحيل

Misconception: Knowledge only pertains to existing things.
Correction: The passage shows that knowledge includes non-existents because the necessary, possible, and impossible are all objects of knowledge, and the latter two are non-existent.

Divine knowledge is universal: it covers all that is logically necessary, possible, and impossible, thereby including non-existents.

Distinguishing Attributes of Meanings and Meaningful Attributes
Al-Sawi defines صفات المعاني and المعنوية with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes of meanings and meaningful attributes, and the condition that makes an attribute meaningful.

Core Idea
Meaningful attributes are caused and necessary for the essence.

Al-Sawi begins by defining two types of attributes: those that are 'attributes of meanings' like spatial occupancy, and those that are 'meaningful' when they are caused and necessary for the essence. This distinction is foundational for understanding the seven attributes of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise definitions and examples.
2
Identify the defining criterion
Find the condition that makes an attribute of meanings become meaningful. Look for the phrase 'معللة بعلة' and 'واجبة للذات'.
3
Analyze the examples
Consider the examples العالمية and القادرية. How do they illustrate the relation between the essence and the meaning?
These are relational attributes, not separate entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات المعاني وكالتَّحيُّز للجرم وقبوله للأعراض، وإن كانت معللة بعلة بأن كانت واجبةً للذات ما دامت عدَّتُها سميت معنوية ؛ كالعالمية والقادرية؛ أي : كون الذَّات المتّصفة بالعلم عالمة، وكونُ الذَّات المتّصفة بالقدرة قادرة، نسبة إلى المعاني، وهي ( سبعة للرَّائي) أي : الناظر المتأمل، ثمَّ فسَّرها بقوله : العلم (أي: عِلْمُه) وما عطف عليه (المحيط بالأشياء) كلّها، واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها، فليس مراده بالأشياء الموجودات فقط كما هو المتعارف عندهم .
قوله : (وكالتَّحيُّز للجرم : المراد بالجرم ما قام بذاته، سواء كان جسماً أو جوهراً فرداً، والمراد بتحيزه أخذه قدراً من الفراغ، وفي تمثيل الشارح بالتحيز إشارة لما قلناه من أنَّ هذا في الصفة النفسية مطلقاً قديمة وحادثة.
قوله : (أي: كون الذَّات المتّصفة بالعلم عالمة) : أي : فتكون عالمة معللة بالعلم ؛ أي: ملازمة له، فالمراد بالعلة الملزوم، والمراد بالمعلول اللازم.
قوله : (نسبة إلى المعاني : مرتبط بقوله : (سمِّيت معنويَّة) .
The attributes of meanings, such as spatial occupancy ([التَّحيُّز]) for the body ([الجرم]) and its acceptance of accidents, and if they are caused by a cause, being necessary for the essence as long as they exist, they are called meaningful ([معنوية]) like being all-knowing ([العالمية]) and being all-powerful ([القادرية]); that is, the essence qualified with knowledge being knowing, and the essence qualified with power being powerful, relative to the meanings ([المعاني]). And they are seven for the observer ([الرَّائي]), i.e., the contemplative viewer. Then he explained them by saying: Knowledge ([العلم]), i.e., His knowledge, and what is conjoined with it, encompassing all things, their necessary, possible, and impossible, so He does not mean by things only existent things as is customary among them. His statement: (And like spatial occupancy for the body): what is meant by body ([الجرم]) is what subsists by itself, whether it is a body or an individual substance, and what is meant by its occupancy is its taking up a measure of space. In the commentator's example of occupancy there is an indication of what we said: that this applies to the essential attribute ([الصفة النفسية]) absolutely, whether eternal or originated. His statement: (that is, the essence qualified with knowledge being knowing): that is, it is knowing caused by knowledge, i.e., concomitant with it, so what is meant by cause is the necessary condition, and what is meant by effect is the necessary consequent. His statement: (relative to the meanings) is connected to His statement: (called meaningful).
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the definition of the attributes of meanings (صفات المعاني) and the meaningful attributes (المعنوية). The commentator (Al-Sawi) begins by identifying examples: spatial occupancy (التحيز) for a body and its acceptance of accidents are attributes of meanings. When these attributes are caused (معللة بعلة) and necessary for the essence, they are called meaningful (معنوية). Examples are العالمية (being all-knowing) and القادرية (being all-powerful), which are the essence's being knowing due to knowledge and being powerful due to power. These are relative to the meanings (المعاني) which are the internal attributes. The text then states that there are seven such attributes for the contemplative observer. The commentator then explains the first attribute, العلم (knowledge), as encompassing all things—necessary, possible, impossible—not only existent things. He then comments on specific phrases: 'التحيز للجرم' clarifies that 'جرم' means anything subsisting by itself (body or atom), and its occupancy is taking up space. This illustrates that the essential attribute (النفسية) applies to both eternal and originated things. Then he explains 'كون الذات متصفة بالعلم عالمة' as the essence being knowing, which is necessitated (معللة) by knowledge, meaning it is inseparable. Finally, he notes that 'نسبة إلى المعاني' relates to the term 'معنوية'. The passage follows the logical order: first defining the category, then exemplifying, then opening the enumeration of the seven attributes.

معنوية
العالمية
القادرية
التحيز
الجرم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Meaningful attributes are additional entities separate from the essence.
Correction: They are relational descriptions of the essence, caused by internal attributes.

Checkpoint

A) It must be spatial occupancy
B) It must be caused and necessary for the essence
C) It must be eternal
D) It must be known through revelation

Key Takeaway
Meaningful attributes are caused and necessary for the essence, unlike mere attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God's attributes like 'all-knowing' are caused by His knowledge and are necessary for His essence. When you say 'Allah knows everything', do you truly believe that His knowledge is essential and not an addition? How does that affect your trust in His knowledge when you face a difficult decision? Commit to reminding yourself today of one situation where you need to rely on His essential knowledge.
?
The passage mentions that meaningful attributes are 'واجبة للذات' (necessary for the essence). Does your belief about God's attributes reflect that they are inseparable from Him? Are there moments when you act as if God temporarily lacked an attribute (e.g., neglect, weakness)? Repent from such thoughts and affirm in your heart that His attributes are ever-present and perfect.
?
Consider the attribute 'القادرية' (all-powerful). The passage says it is relative to power. Do you truly internalize that God's power is not separate from Him but essential? When you witness something difficult in your life, do you doubt His power? Resolve to recite 'الله قادر' with the understanding that it is His very essence, and let that strengthen your conviction.

The First Meaningful Attribute: Knowledge
Al-Sawi explains the first meaningful attribute, knowledge, and its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the explanation of the first meaningful attribute, knowledge (العلم), its scope, and the relationship between the essence and knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge encompasses all things: necessary, possible, and impossible.

After mentioning seven attributes, Al-Sawi begins the enumeration with knowledge (العلم). He emphasizes that it encompasses all things, including necessary, possible, and impossible, correcting the common view that it only covers existents. The commentary also clarifies the example of spatial occupancy and the relationship of causality between the essence and attributes.

Guided Path
1

The Objection on 'Things' in God's Knowledge and Its Answer
Al-Sawi answers the objection that 'things' limits knowledge to existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the term 'thing' (الشيء) in the definition of God's knowledge must be understood broadly to include both existents and non-existents.

Core Idea
God's knowledge covers all things, existent and non-existent.

This short passage tackles a subtle but important objection: if 'things' means only existent objects, then God's knowledge would be limited to what exists. Al-Sawi shows that the term is used in a wider sense, preserving the universality of divine knowledge. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify the implied objection: that 'thing' conventionally means existent, which would restrict God's knowledge.
The objection is not stated outright but is implied.
2
Study the answer
Notice how Al-Sawi distinguishes between the conventional term and the broader usage that includes non-existents.
This distinction is key to resolving the objection.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this matters: God's knowledge of possibilities and impossibilities is affirmed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وما عطف عليه دفع به ما يقال : إنَّ العلم وحده ليس تفسيراً للمعاني كلها.
قوله : (واجبها وجائزها ومستحيلها جواب عن سؤال مقدَّر، تقديره : الشَّيء هو الموجود، فيقتضي قصر تعلُّق العلم على الموجودات، مع أنَّه يتعلق بالمعدومات أيضاً.
فأجاب : بانه ليس المراد بالشيء المصطلح عليه، بل المراد به الأمر الصادق بالموجود والمعدوم
His statement: (and what is conjoined with it) refutes by it what is said: that knowledge alone is not an explanation of all the meanings. His statement: (its necessary, possible, and impossible) is an answer to an implied question, the import of which is: the thing ([الشيء]) is the existent, so it necessitates restricting the relation of knowledge to existents, while it relates also to non-existents. So he answered: that what is meant by 'thing' is not the conventional term, but what is meant is the term that applies truly to both existent and non-existent.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The commentator (Al-Sawi) first explains that the phrase 'and what is conjoined with it' in the definition of knowledge is meant to refute the claim that knowledge alone (without the conjoined attributes) does not explain all the meanings (the seven attributes). Then he addresses an implied objection: if 'things' (الأشياء) is taken to mean only existent things, then God's knowledge would be limited to existents, whereas it also encompasses non-existents (المعدومات). The answer is that the term 'thing' in this context is not the conventional philosophical term (which refers only to existents), but rather a broader term that applies truly (صادق) to both existent and non-existent. This preserves the comprehensive scope of God's knowledge. The argument proceeds logically: first stating the potential objection, then giving the counter-answer.

Correction: In this context, 'thing' is used in a broader sense that includes non-existents (المعدومات) to preserve the universality of God's knowledge.

D) To contradict the definition of knowledge

Key Takeaway

Defining God's Knowledge: Eternal Attribute and Timeless Language
Al-Sawi presents and defends a definition of God's knowledge, addressing linguistic objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's knowledge as an eternal attribute and how scholars defend it against objections about temporal language.

Core Idea
Defining God's knowledge requires language purified of any temporal implication.

In this passage, Al-Sawi presents the soundest definition of God's attribute of knowledge and immediately addresses a subtle linguistic objection. The very terms we use to describe God must be examined for any trace of createdness. This lesson unpacks how scholars handled the tension between defining God's knowledge and avoiding unworthy implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and the controversy
Start by reading the Arabic definition and the discussion on whether knowledge can be defined. Note the two arguments against defining it: its obviousness and the difficulty of an uncontested definition.
The definition 'صفة أزلية ينكشف بها الموجودات' is the one being defended.
2
Analyze the objection and answer
Focus on the objection that the present tense verb 'ينكشف' suggests temporality. Read the rebuttal that verbs in definitions are abstracted from time. Reflect on why this matters for divine attributes.
The objection arises from the literal reading of the Arabic present tense.
3
Examine the deeper criticism of 'انكشاف'
Al-Sawi notes that even the term 'انكشاف' (disclosure) is problematic because it implies a change from obscurity to clarity. Consider why this is unfitting for God.
This shows the extreme care taken to avoid any imperfection in describing Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو صفة أزلية ينكشف بها الموجودات والمعلومات
قوله : (صفة أزلية ... الخ ) : اعلم : ان الناس المختلفوا ، في العلم هل يحد أو لا؟ فقال بعضهم : إنه لا يحد؛ لظهوره انه كاشف لغيره، فهو غني أن يظهره ،غيره ولعسره إذ لم يُحد بحدّ إلا نوزع فيه، والقائلون بحده لهم فيه تعاريف كثيرة وأكثرها مدخول ، وأصحها قولنا : هو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات تعلق إحاطة
قوله : (ينكشف: المراد بالانكشاف التمييز والاتضاح.
إن قلت : التَّعبير بـ ( ينكشف) يوهم حدوث الانكشاف؛ لأن المضارع يدلُّ على الحال والاستقبال وهو لا يناسب علم الله تعالى.
أجيب بأن الأفعال الواقعة في التعاريف مجرَّدة عن الزمان، ولا دلالة لها عليه، فكأنه قيل : صفة يحصل بها انكشاف ما تعلقت به كذا قيل
وأنت خبير بأن الفعل وإن كان الملاحظ منه المصدر وهو الانكشاف إلَّا أنَّ التَّعبير بالانكشاف هنا غير لائق من جهة أنَّه انفعال يوهم حدوث إيضاح بعد خفاء .
قوله : (الموجودات والمعدومات) : دخل فيه العلم نفسه، فيعلم بعلمه ،علمه كما يعلم به ذاته وسائر صفاته ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ صفة ليست من صفات
And it is an eternal attribute by which existents and known things are disclosed. His statement: (eternal attribute...): Know that people have differed over whether knowledge can be defined or not. Some said it cannot be defined because of its obviousness, as it discloses other things, so it is self-sufficient to be disclosed by other than itself, and because of its difficulty, as it has not been defined by a definition except that it has been contested. Those who hold that it can be defined have many definitions for it, most of which are flawed, and the most correct is our saying: it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence (Exalted is He) that relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible with an encompassing relation. His statement: (is disclosed): what is meant by disclosure is distinction and clarification. If you say: the expression 'is disclosed' suggests temporality of the disclosure, because the present tense indicates present or future time, and that does not befit God's knowledge. The answer is that verbs occurring in definitions are abstracted from time, and they have no indication of it, so it is as if it was said: an attribute by which disclosure of what it relates to occurs, as was said. And you are aware that the verb, even though what is derived from it is the infinitive, which is disclosure, the expression 'disclosure' here is inappropriate because it is a passive state suggesting the occurrence of clarification after obscurity. His statement: (the existents and non-existents): includes knowledge itself, so He knows His knowledge by His knowledge, just as He knows His essence and all His attributes by it, because every attribute is not among the attributes...
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the definition of God's attribute of knowledge. It begins with a proposed definition: 'It is an eternal attribute by which existents and known things are disclosed.' The commentator (Al-Sawi) then discusses the controversy over whether knowledge can be defined at all. Some argue it cannot because it is self-evident and any definition is contestable. Others propose definitions, but most are flawed. The soundest definition is: 'an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence that relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible with an encompassing relation.' The commentator then addresses an objection: the present tense verb 'ينكشف' might imply temporality, which is unfitting for God. The answer is that verbs in definitions are timeless (مجرَّدة عن الزمان), indicating no specific time. However, the commentator notes that even the term 'انكشاف' (disclosure) itself is problematic because it suggests a passive change from obscurity to clarity, which does not befit God. Finally, he notes that 'الموجودات والمعدومات' in the definition includes knowledge itself, so God knows His own knowledge, essence, and all attributes. This is a nuanced discussion of the definition of a divine attribute, showing the care taken to avoid unworthy implications.

الانكشاف
التمييز
الاتضاح
المضارع
التعاريف
إحاطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the present tense verb 'ينكشف' in the definition of God's knowledge implies that knowledge occurs in time.
Correction: Verbs in definitions are timeless (مجرَّدة عن الزمان) and indicate no specific time. However, even the term 'disclosure' is problematic as it suggests a passive change.

Checkpoint

A) Because the verb indicates future time
B) Because verbs in definitions are timeless and carry no temporal implication
C) Because knowledge only applies to existents
D) Because the definition is not intended to be accurate

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is an eternal attribute defined as encompassing all things, and any language suggesting temporality or change must be carefully reinterpreted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a du'a for something in the future, do you truly believe that Allah's knowledge already encompasses that event without any change in Him? How does that affect your certainty that He will respond as He knows best?
?
The author criticizes the word 'disclosure' because it implies that God's knowledge was once obscure. Honestly examine your heart: do you ever, even unintentionally, imagine that God 'finds out' new things as they happen? What does that reveal about your understanding of His eternal knowledge?
?
Our casual speech often describes Allah in temporal terms (e.g., 'Allah saw your good deed'). Based on this lesson, commit to one specific phrase you will correct to avoid implying change in Allah. What will you say instead, and how will you train yourself?

Divine Knowledge Encompasses Itself and All Things
Commentator explains that divine knowledge encompasses all existents, non-existents, and itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine knowledge includes not only all existents and non-existents but also itself, and that God's knowledge is a perfect, contradiction-free discovery.

Core Idea
God's knowledge knows itself — there is no gap between knower and known.

In classical Kalam, divine knowledge is defined as encompassing all things, including non-existents. But a subtle question arises: does this include knowledge itself? The commentator explains that yes, God knows His own knowledge by His knowledge, just as He knows His essence and attributes. This lesson unpacks that reflexive knowing and why it is unique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the commentator's own wording come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; pay attention to the phrase 'دخل فيه العلم نفسه'.
2
Identify the three claims
The passage makes three interconnected claims: (1) the phrase 'existents and non-existents' includes knowledge itself, (2) God knows His knowledge by it just as He knows His essence and attributes, (3) each attribute is a discovery that does not admit contradiction.
Try to see how claim 3 supports claim 1 and 2.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: Why is it important that God knows His own knowledge? How does this relate to the perfection of divine knowledge? The commentary will help clarify the technical terms.
The word 'انكشاف' (discovery) highlights that knowledge is a direct unveiling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الموجودات والمعدومات) : دخل فيه العلم نفسه، فيعلم بعلمه علمه كما يعلم به ذاته وسائر صفاته ؛ لأنَّ كلَّ صفة ليست من صفات سكات المعاني على ما هي عليه انكشافاً لا يحتمل النقيض بوجه.
His saying: (the existents and the non-existents): It includes knowledge itself, for He knows His knowledge by His knowledge just as He knows His essence and all His attributes; because every attribute is not from the attributes of [?] of meanings as they are, a discovery that does not admit contradiction in any way.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase 'existents and non-existents' in the definition of God's knowledge. The commentator points out that this includes God's own knowledge: He knows His knowledge by His knowledge, just as He knows His essence and all His attributes. This is because all attributes are known by God's knowledge. The passage ends with a clause describing that knowledge as a discovery that does not admit contradiction at all, emphasizing its perfection. The unclear 'سكات المعاني' likely refers to 'all attributes of meanings' or a similar technical term in kalam.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God cannot know His own knowledge because that would imply a reflexive loop that is impossible for a simple being.
Correction: Kalam affirms that God's knowledge is not like human knowledge; it is a single, simple, and perfect attribute. Knowing itself does not imply multiplicity or deficiency. Rather, it is part of His comprehensive knowledge of all things, including His essence and attributes.

Checkpoint

A) Knowledge itself is included among the known objects of God's knowledge.
B) Knowledge is the same as existence.
C) Non-existents are not known by God.
D) Only existents are included.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is all-encompassing: it includes every existent, every non-existent, and even itself, all known in a single, perfect, contradiction-free act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge is a 'discovery that does not admit contradiction'. In what areas of your life do you still entertain doubts about His promises, and what would it take for you to be as certain as God's knowledge is certain?
?
If God knows every thought you have ever had, including those you hide from others, how does that reality change the way you think about your private moments today?
?
God's knowledge includes all non-existents—every potential event that never happens. How does this truth affect your trust in His decree, especially regarding fears about what might have been?

The Attribute of Life: Definition and Proof
The commentator defines life and proves it via a logical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attribute of life is defined as an eternal attribute that makes knowledge and will possible, and how it is proven through a logical argument.

Core Idea
God's life is the foundation for His knowledge and will.

The commentator begins by defining the attribute of life as an eternal attribute that makes knowledge and will possible. He then provides a logical argument: since God possesses attributes like knowledge and will, which can only subsist in a living being, God must have life. This passage also clarifies that God's life is unlike created life, which depends on spirit.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the source passage and its literal translation to grasp the commentator's words directly.
Notice the concise style of the matn.
2
Understand the definition
Note that life is defined as an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and will. 'Eternal' excludes temporal life.
3
Follow the logical proof
The commentator presents a syllogism: God has attributes of meanings and mental attributes; whatever has such attributes must be living; therefore God has life.
This proof is rational, not merely revelatory.
4
Contrast with temporal life
Notice the clarification that God's life is not associated with spirit, unlike created life which depends on spirit.
This distinguishes divine life from human life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

و (حياته تعالى، وهي: صفة أزليَّة توجب صحة العلم والإرادة.
قوله : (أزليَّة) : خرجت الحادثة.
وقوله : (توجب صحة العلم والإرادة أي وباقي صفات المعاني والمعنوية، وذلك بأن تقول : الله متصف بالصفات المعاني والمعنوية، وكلُّ من كان كذلك تجب له الحياة، ينتج : الله يجب له الحياة، إذ لا يتصوَّر قيامها بغير حيّ، وحياة الله لا يروح ؛ بخلاف حياة الحادث فإنَّها بالرُّوح.

And that is by you saying: God is described with the attributes of meanings and mental attributes, and everything that is so requires life. The result: God must have life, for it is inconceivable that they subsist without a living being. And God's life does not have spirit; unlike temporal life which is by spirit.

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of life (al-hayat). The commentator states that life is an eternal attribute that makes knowledge and will possible. He explains that 'eternal' excludes temporal life. The attribute of life is necessary because God has attributes of meanings (like knowledge, will) and mental attributes; anything that possesses such attributes must be living, as they cannot inhere in a non-living being. The commentator also notes that God's life is not associated with a spirit (ruh), unlike created life which depends on spirit.

Divine Power and the Azali-Qadim Distinction
Commentary defines power and clarifies a terminological distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of power and the distinction between the terms 'eternal' (azali) and 'ancient' (qadim) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Power is eternal, but not all that is eternal is God—terminology matters.

This passage from the commentary on al-Kharida defines one of God's essential attributes: power (qudra). It first gives a linguistic and technical definition, then addresses a subtle terminological choice: why does the commentator say 'eternal' (azaliyya) rather than 'ancient' (qadima)? The answer reveals an important distinction in how these terms are used in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: definition of power and then the note on why 'azaliyya' is used instead of 'qadima'.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Grasp the definition of power
Understand the linguistic meaning (strength) and the technical definition (referred to the commentator).
The technical definition is not spelled out here, but is assumed from the original sharh.
3
Explore the 'azali' vs 'qadim' distinction
Consider the two reasons: either they are synonyms, or 'azali' is more general, covering essence, attributes, existents, and non-existents, while 'qadim' is specific to the Necessary Existent.
This nuance prevents confusion when discussing God's attributes.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this distinction matter? It shows the precision of kalam terminology in describing God without attributing temporality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقدرة): هي لغة: القوة، واصطلاحاً ما قاله الشارح.
قوله: (أزليَّة) : لم يقل قديمة، إما بناء على أنَّ القديم والأزليّ مترادفان، أو على أنَّ الأزليَّ أعم من القديم؛ لأنَّه يشمل الذات والصفات، والمعدوم والموجود، وتخصيص القديم بالذات الواجب الوجود.
His saying: (and power): it is linguistically: strength, and terminologically what the commentator said.
His saying: (eternal): He did not say ancient, either because 'ancient' and 'eternal' are synonymous, or because 'eternal' is more general than 'ancient'; for it includes essence and attributes, and the non-existent and existent, while 'ancient' is specific to the necessarily existent essence.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of power (qudra). The commentator gives the linguistic meaning (strength) and refers to the technical definition given by the commentator of the original text. He then explains why the definition uses 'eternal' (azaliyya) instead of 'ancient' (qadima). Two possibilities are given: either the terms are synonymous, or 'eternal' is more general, covering both the essence and attributes, as well as non-existents and existents, whereas 'ancient' is often reserved for God's essence (the Necessary Existent).

Correction: This passage shows that while they may be synonymous, 'azali' can be broader, including attributes and non-existents, whereas 'qadim' is often reserved for the divine essence alone.

The attribute of power is defined as strength, and its description as 'eternal' (azali) rather than 'ancient' (qadim) carries theological nuance either as a synonym or as a broader term.

?

The Divine Attribute of Life: Definition and Distinction
Al-Sawi defines the divine attribute of life and explains its necessity and distinction from created life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of life and how it differs from created life.

Core Idea
God's life is eternal, uncaused, and the foundation of His knowledge and will.

In this lesson, we explore the divine attribute of life as defined by classical Islamic theology. Al-Sawi explains that life is an eternal attribute that makes knowledge and will possible, and he distinguishes it from temporal, spirit-dependent life. Understanding this attribute is crucial for grasping how God's other attributes subsist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the key terms.
2
Identify the components of the definition
The definition has three parts: it is eternal, it necessitates knowledge and will, and it does not admit contradiction from the knower's perspective.
The phrase 'does not admit contradiction' refers to the knower's certainty, not others' doubt.
3
Examine the logical argument
The author provides a syllogism: God possesses the attributes of meaning; such attributes require a living being; therefore God must be living.
This connects life to other attributes.
4
Contrast with created life
Created life depends on a spirit, but God's life is not like that. It is self-subsisting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحياة و (حياته تعالى، وهي: صفة أزليَّة توجب صحة العلم والإرادة. قوله : (لا يحتمل النقيض بوجه) : أي : لا يحسب الذهن ولا يحسب الخارج عند العالم، أما عند غيره فلا ، إذ كثيراً ما يعلم الإنسان شيئاً ويتردد فيه غيره أو ينقيه . قوله : (أزليَّة) : خرجت الحادثة. وقوله : (توجب صحة العلم والإرادة أي وباقي صفات المعاني والمعنوية، وذلك بأن تقول : الله متصف بالصفات المعاني والمعنوية، وكلُّ من كان كذلك تجب له الحياة، ينتج : الله يجب له الحياة، إذ لا يتصوَّر قيامها بغير حيّ، وحياة الله لا يروح ؛ بخلاف حياة الحادث فإنَّها بالرُّوح.
And (His life, exalted is He, is an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and will. His statement: 'does not admit of contradiction in any respect' means: neither in the mind nor in external reality from the perspective of the knower; as for others, then no, for often a person knows something while another hesitates about it or denies it. His statement: 'eternal' excludes temporal [life]. And His statement: 'necessitates the validity of knowledge and will' i.e., and the rest of the attributes of meaning and meaning-derived. That is by saying: God is described with the attributes of meaning and meaning-derived, and whoever is such, life is necessary for him. The conclusion: life is necessary for God, for it is inconceivable that they subsist in other than a living being. And the life of God does not have a spirit, unlike the life of the temporal being which is by a spirit.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of life (hayat). The author states that it is an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge and will. He then explains that the definition implies that God's knowledge does not admit of contradiction in any respect, either in the mind or in external reality, from the perspective of the knower. However, others may have doubts. The term 'eternal' (azaliyya) excludes created life. Furthermore, life is necessary for God because He possesses the attributes of meaning and meaning-derived, and such attributes can only subsist in a living being. Finally, unlike created life which depends on a spirit, God's life is not dependent on a spirit.

Divine power is an eternal attribute that enables God to effect possible things.

قوله: (وقدرة): هي لغة: القوة، واصطلاحاً ما قاله الشارح. قوله: (أزليَّة) : لم يقل ،قديمة، إما بناء على أنَّ القديم والأزليّ مترادفان، أو على أنَّ الأزليَّ أعم من القديم؛ لأنَّه يشمل الذات والصفات، والمعدوم والموجود، وتخصيص القديم بالذات الواجب الوجود. قوله : (يتأتى بها إيجاد الممكن : دخل فيه أفعالنا الاختياريَّة، ففيه ردُّ على المعتزلة القائلين : بأنَّ العبد يخلق أفعال نفسه الاختياريَّة. وقوله : (وإعدامه) هذا هو المشهور، وقيل: لا تتعلق بالإعدام، بل إذا أراد الله إعدام شيء أمسك عنه المدد والتعاريف في صفات الباري جل وعلا ليست حدوداً حقيقيَّة وإنَّما هي رسوم؛ لأنَّه لا يعلم كنه ذاته وصفاته إلا هو. واعلم : أنَّ أعدامنا الأزليَّة لا تتعلَّق بها القدرة ولا الإرادة اتّفاقاً : لوجوبها، وأمَّا أعدامنا فيما لا يزال السَّابقة على وجودنا، ووجودنا بعد عدمنا، واستمرار وجودنا وأعدامنا بعد وجودنا، وإيجادنا يوم القيامة فمن تعلقات القدرة والإرادة.
His statement: (and power) is linguistically strength, and terminologically what the commentator said. His statement: (eternal): he did not say 'ancient (qadim)' either on the basis that 'ancient' and 'eternal' are synonymous, or that 'eternal' is more general than 'ancient'; because it includes the essence and attributes, the non-existent and the existent, while specifying 'ancient' for the essence of the Necessary Existent. His statement: (by it the existence of the possible is effected): it includes our voluntary actions, so in it is a refutation of the Mu'tazila who say that the servant creates his own voluntary actions. And His statement: (and its non-existence) this is the well-known [view], and it was said: it does not relate to non-existence, rather when God wills the non-existence of something He withholds from it continuance. And the definitions concerning the attributes of the Creator, glorified and exalted, are not real definitions (hudud haqiqiyya) but rather are descriptive (rusum), because no one knows the reality of His essence and attributes except Him. And know: our eternal non-existences are not related to power nor will by consensus, due to their necessity. As for our non-existences in the future preceding our existence, and our existence after our non-existence, and the continuation of our existence and our non-existence after our existence, and our creation on the Day of Resurrection, these are among the objects of power and will.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of power (qudra). The author explains that power is an eternal attribute by which God effects the existence and non-existence of possible things. He first clarifies why the term 'eternal' (azali) is used instead of 'ancient' (qadim), noting they may be synonymous or that 'eternal' is more general. Power includes all voluntary human actions, which refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions. Regarding non-existence, the well-known view is that power relates to both existence and non-existence, though some limit it to existence only, saying non-existence occurs by withholding continuance. The author then remarks that definitions of divine attributes are only descriptive (rusum), not real definitions (hudud haqiqiyya), because God's essence is unknowable. Finally, he clarifies the scope of power and will: eternal non-existence (e.g., non-existence before creation) is not subject to them by consensus because it is necessary, but temporal non-existences (those preceding or following existence) are within their scope.

Key Terms
القدرة
أزليَّة
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'azali' and 'qadim' are completely different in meaning with no overlap.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows they may be synonymous, or 'azali' is more general. The difference is not absolute.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does al-Sawi use 'azali' instead of 'qadim'?
A) Because 'azali' is a Quranic term while 'qadim' is not
B) Either because they are synonymous or because 'azali' is more general
C) Because 'qadim' implies createdness
D) Because 'azali' refers only to attributes

Key Takeaway
The term 'eternal' (azali) is used to describe divine power either as synonymous with 'ancient' or as more comprehensive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a single word choice in describing Allah carries deep theological weight. When you speak about Allah in daily conversation, do you choose your words carefully or casually? Will you commit to learning the precise meanings of every attribute you mention?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'azali' and 'qadim', emphasizing that 'qadim' is reserved for Allah's essence. Have you ever used the term 'ancient' or 'old' in reference to Allah without reflecting on its proper theological usage? What will you do to be more precise?
?
You have just read that divine power is eternal. Reflect on what it means that Allah's power is without beginning and encompasses all possible things. How does this truth change the way you view your own limitations and the things you worry about? Will you consciously trust in Allah's power today?

The Scope of Divine Power: Existence, Non-Existence, and Refutation of the Mu'tazila
Al-Sawi explains that power effects existence and non-existence, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine power brings about both existence and non-existence of possible things, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions.

Core Idea
Divine power encompasses both existence and non-existence, including human voluntary actions.

Al-Sawi now expands on the function of divine power. It is not limited to creating existence but also to bringing about non-existence. This includes all voluntary human actions, which directly challenges the Mu'tazilite doctrine of human self-generation of actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement about power effecting existence and non-existence
Read carefully the phrase 'يتأتى بها إيجاد الممكن وإعدامه' and note that both existence and non-existence are mentioned.
2
Note the inclusion of voluntary actions
Al-Sawi explicitly includes 'our voluntary actions' under 'إيجاد الممكن'. Understand that this means all human actions are created by God.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
The inclusion of voluntary actions is a direct refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own actions. Reflect on why this is significant.
The Mu'tazila argued that humans have free will and create their own actions; Al-Sawi asserts that God's power encompasses all actions.

Divine Attributes Beyond Real Definitions
Al-Sawi states that definitions of divine attributes are only descriptive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that definitions of divine attributes are only descriptive (rusum), not real definitions (hudud haqiqiyya), because God's essence is unknowable.

Core Idea
Human definitions of divine attributes are mere descriptions, not comprehensive realities.

Al-Sawi inserts an important epistemological note: our attempts to define divine attributes are only descriptive outlines. We cannot grasp the true essence of God's attributes; only He knows them fully.

Guided Path
1
Read the remark about definitions
Read the sentence stating that definitions of divine attributes are not real definitions but mere descriptions.
2
Understand the reason
Al-Sawi gives the reason: no one knows the reality of God's essence and attributes except Him. Reflect on the implication for theological language.
3
Apply the principle
Consider how this principle affects the way we talk about all divine attributes. Why is it important to acknowledge the limitation?

Distinguishing Eternal Non-Existence from Temporal Non-Existence
Al-Sawi distinguishes eternal vs temporal non-existence regarding power and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that eternal non-existence (non-existence before creation) is not subject to divine power and will, whereas temporal non-existences are.

Core Idea
Only temporal non-existence falls under divine power, not the necessary non-existence of eternity.

Al-Sawi concludes with a crucial clarification: not all non-existence is the same. The absolute eternal non-existence of possible things before their creation is not an object of power or will by consensus. However, non-existences that occur within time—before existence, after existence, or in the Resurrection—are indeed under God's power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction
Read the passage that begins with 'واعلم' and identifies two types of non-existence: eternal and temporal.
2
Identify the categories of temporal non-existence
List the four categories mentioned: non-existence before existence, existence after non-existence, continuation of existence and non-existence after existence, and creation on the Day of Resurrection.
3
Understand why eternal non-existence is excluded
Al-Sawi says it is excluded by consensus due to its necessity. Reflect on the difference between necessary and contingent non-existence.

Source Text

Are there moments in your day when you forget this? What habit can you form to remind yourself that every breath is a miracle of divine preservation?

The Divine Attribute of Will: Sunni Doctrine and Refutation of Deviant Views
Al-Sawi defines will, states the Sunni view, and refutes opinions of Jubba'i, Karramiyya, Dirar, and Najjar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni doctrine of the divine attribute of will as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, and you will be able to distinguish it from four deviant views.

Core Idea
God's will is an eternal attribute, not a temporal event or mere negation.

The divine attribute of will is a central topic in Sunni theology. Al-Sawi presents the correct Sunni position and then contrasts it with four erroneous views held by various theological sects. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for safeguarding one's creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the Sunni definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the Sunni view is stated first as the truth. The term 'صفة زائدة' is key.
The commentator (al-Sawi) refers to his own commentary on al-Kharida.
2
Examine each deviant view
The passage lists four deviant views: al-Jubba'i, al-Karramiyya, Dirar, and al-Najjar. For each, note how they differ from the Sunni position.
Al-Jubba'i says will is superadded but not in the essence; al-Karramiyya say it is temporal; Dirar says it is a relative description; al-Najjar says it is negative.
3
Reflect on the truth
The passage concludes that the truth is the Sunni view. Ask yourself why the other views are insufficient or problematic.

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of will (irada). It begins with the linguistic meaning (intention) and terminological definition according to the commentator. The author states that the Sunni position is that will is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. He then enumerates four deviant views: al-Jubba'i (a Mu'tazilite) says will is an attribute superadded to the essence but subsisting without a locus (i.e., not in the essence); al-Karramiyya say it is a temporal, created attribute subsisting in the essence; Dirar ibn Amr says it is the mere fact that God is not coerced or forgetful (i.e., a relative or negative description); al-Najjar says it is a negative attribute (i.e., not being ignorant or unwilling). The author concludes that the truth is the Sunni view as stated by the commentator.

Divine Will: Creation, Annihilation, and Refuting the Mu'tazila
Al-Sawi defines divine will and its scope, refuting Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine will as an eternal attribute that effects both creation and annihilation, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own voluntary acts.

Core Idea
Divine will includes both existence and non-existence, denying human self-creation.

Al-Sawi begins by explaining the function of divine will: it brings about the existence and annihilation of possible things. This includes our voluntary actions, directly refuting the Mu'tazilite doctrine that humans create their own deeds. He also notes that definitions of God's attributes are mere descriptions, as His essence is unknowable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall meaning, then read the translation. Focus on the definition of will and its scope.
Notice how the passage starts with the function of will before giving its definition.
2
Identify the refutation of Mu'tazila
Find the sentence that includes our voluntary acts and understand why this refutes the Mu'tazilite view. The key phrase is 'دخل فيه أفعالنا الاختيارية'.
The Mu'tazilites held that humans create their own actions, which contradicts the all-encompassing divine will.
3
Understand the nature of definitions
Al-Sawi states that definitions of God's attributes are not real definitions but descriptions. Reflect on why this is important for avoiding anthropomorphism.

واعلم : أنَّ أعدامنا الأزليَّة لا تتعلَّق بها القدرة ولا الإرادة اتّفاقاً : لوجوبها، وأمَّا أعدامنا فيما لا يزال السَّابقة على وجودنا، ووجودنا بعد عدمنا، واستمرار وجودنا وأعدامنا بعد وجودنا، وإيجادنا يوم القيامة فمن تعلقات القدرة والإرادة.
قوله : (وإرادة) : هي لغة: القصد، واصطلاحاً : ما قاله الشارح، وهذا مذهب أهل السُّنَّة، وعند الجبائي : هي صفة زائدة على الذات قائمة لا بمحلّ، وعند الكراميَّة : صفة حادثة قائمة بالذات، وعند ضرار : نفس كون الفاعل ليس بمكره ولا ساه، وعند النجار : صفة سلبية هي . والحقُّ : مذهب أهل السُّنَّة الذي ذكره الشارح .
