By it, the creation of the possible and its annihilation are effected. And (will) is: an eternal attribute... Hashiyat Al-Sawi on Sharh Al-Kharidat. Will. His saying: 'By it, the creation of the possible' includes our voluntary acts, and therein is a refutation of the Mu'tazilites who say that the servant creates his own voluntary acts. And his saying: 'and its annihilation' – this is the well-known [view], and it is said: it does not pertain to annihilation, but if God wills the annihilation of something, He withholds from it support. Definitions concerning the attributes of the Exalted and Glorious are not real definitions but only descriptions, because no one knows the essence of His Essence and His attributes except Him. Know that our eternal non-existences are agreed to be not subject to power or will, because they are necessary. As for our future non-existences that precede our existence, our existence after our non-existence, the continuation of our existence and our non-existences after our existence, and our creation on the Day of Resurrection, they are among the objects of power and will. His saying: (will) linguistically: intention; terminologically: what the commentator said – and this is the school of the Ahl al-Sunnah. According to al-Juba'i: it is an attribute additional to the Essence subsisting not in a substrate. According to the Karamiyya: it is a temporal attribute subsisting in the Essence. According to Dirar: it is the very fact that the doer is not coerced nor forgetful. According to al-Najjar: it is a negative attribute. And the truth is the school of the Ahl al-Sunnah which the commentator mentioned. (1) In the printed edition 'كل' instead of 'ممكن'.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage from al-Sawi's commentary defines the divine attribute of will and clarifies its scope. It begins by quoting the original text: 'By it (the will), the creation of the possible and its annihilation are effected.' The commentary explains that this includes our voluntary acts, thus refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that humans create their own voluntary actions. It then discusses whether will pertains to annihilation; the well-known view is that it does, while some say that annihilation occurs by withholding sustainment. The definitions of God's attributes are not real definitions but mere descriptions, as His essence is unknowable. Then al-Sawi clarifies that eternal non-existences (things that never existed) are not objects of power or will by agreement, because they are necessary non-existents. However, future non-existences before creation, existence after non-existence, continued existence, non-existence after existence, and resurrection are objects of power and will. Finally, the passage presents the terminological definition of will: linguistically 'intention', and technically as stated by the commentator (the author of the Sharh), which is the Ash'ari view. It then summarises the views of other schools: al-Juba'i (Mu'tazilite) says will is an attribute subsisting not in a substrate; the Karamiyya say it is temporal; Dirar says it is the absence of coercion; al-Najjar says it is a negative attribute. The truth is that of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Key Terms
إرادة
إيجاد
إعدام
صفة أزليَّة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that divine will only creates but does not annihilate, or that humans create their own actions independently of God's will.
Correction: The passage asserts that divine will effects both creation and annihilation, and it refutes the Mu'tazilite view by including human voluntary acts under God's creative will.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what is included in the phrase 'creation of the possible' that refutes the Mu'tazilites?
A) The creation of the heavens and the earth
B) Our voluntary acts
C) The annihilation of things
D) The eternal attributes

Key Takeaway
Divine will creates and annihilates all things, including human voluntary acts, refuting any claim of independent human creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single action you take—even your most private, conscious choice—is created by Allah's will? How does that belief change the way you view your own responsibility and your need for Allah's help in every moment?
?
When you sin, do you tend to blame your own independent 'free will' or do you recognize that even that sin occurs within Allah's will? How should this understanding affect your repentance and your trust in Allah's mercy?
?
If your voluntary acts are not self-created but are effected by Allah's will, then what does that mean for your sense of pride in your good deeds? How can you cultivate gratitude and humility in light of this truth?

Divine Will's Relation to Non-Existents and the Schools' Views
Al-Sawi discusses will's relation to non-existents and various schools' views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between eternal non-existents (which are necessarily nonexistent and not subject to will) and future non-existents (which are), and the different theological definitions of the nature of divine will.

Core Idea
Divine will applies to future non-existents but not to eternal ones; the correct definition is that of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Al-Sawi then addresses a critical nuance: does divine will pertain to non-existents? He distinguishes between eternal non-existents (which are necessary and thus not willed) and future non-existents (which are willed). He also presents the views of different schools on the nature of will, affirming the Ash'ari position as the truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on non-existents
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the distinction between eternal non-existents and future non-existents. Note the phrase 'لوجوبها' for eternal non-existents.
Eternal non-existents are those that never were and never will be; they are necessarily nonexistent.
2
Identify the list of willed things
Find the list of items that are objects of power and will: future non-existences, existence after non-existence, continuation, non-existence after existence, and resurrection.
This shows the comprehensive scope of divine will.
3
Compare the schools' definitions
Read the different definitions: the Ash'ari view, al-Juba'i's, Karamiyya, Dirar, and al-Najjar. Understand how each differs from the Ahl al-Sunnah position.
The truth is the school of Ahl al-Sunnah as stated by the commentator.

Rational Proof for the Four Essential Attributes
Al-Sawi proves four divine attributes by the impossibility of their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Sawi uses the impossibility of the opposite to prove that God must possess knowledge, life, power, and will.

Core Idea
God must possess these four attributes because their opposites prevent creation of the perfect world.

In this passage, al-Sawi presents a concise rational argument for four essential divine attributes. He builds his proof on the fact that the world exists in perfect order, and that the creator of such a world cannot be characterized by ignorance, death, incapacity, or lack of intent. As you read, watch how he uses a conditional chain to establish necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument flow.
Notice the conditional structure: 'إذا لم يتصف ... لاتصف'.
2
Identify the four attributes and their opposites
The passage lists four attributes and their opposites. Find them and note the logical relationship.
The opposites are جهل, موت, عجز, عدم قصد.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the reasoning: if God lacked any attribute, He would have the opposite, which would make creation impossible. But creation exists, so God must have the attributes.
This is a proof from the impossibility of the opposite.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical question
The passage ends with a rhetorical question 'كيف والعالَمُ موجود على أتم النّظام؟' which drives home the conclusion. Consider its force.
This connects observation of the world to theological necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه من وجود أو عَدَم، ومقدار و زمان ومكان وجهة.
إذ لو لم يتّصف بواحدة من هذه الصفات الأربعة لاتّصفَ بأضدادها، من جهل وموت وعَجْز وعَدَم قصد إلى شيء، والمتَصفُ بأضدادها لا يمكنه أن يخلق شيئاً من العالم البديع الإتقان، كيف والعالَمُ موجود على أتم النّظام؟ وسيأتي لهذا مزيد بيان.
It (the will) specifies the possible by some of what is admissible for it: existence or non-existence, measure, time, place, and direction. For if He were not characterized by one of these four attributes, He would be characterized by their opposites: ignorance, death, incapacity, and absence of intent towards anything. And the one characterized by their opposites cannot create anything of the meticulously perfected world. How, when the world exists in the most perfect order? And there will come for this further clarification.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the necessity of four essential divine attributes: knowledge, life, power, and will. The author begins by noting that the divine will specifies possibles in six ways: existence, non-existence, measure, time, place, and direction. Then he argues that if God lacked any of these four attributes, He would necessarily possess their opposites: ignorance (opposite of knowledge), death (opposite of life), incapacity (opposite of power), and lack of intent (opposite of will). But the one who possesses these opposites cannot be the creator of the world, which exhibits perfect order and craftsmanship. Therefore, since the world exists in such perfect order, God must possess these four attributes. The proof is a classic argument from the impossibility of the opposite.

موت
عجز
عدم قصد
العالم البديع الإتقان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God could create the world without knowledge or will, as a random act.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that without will and knowledge, the creator would be ignorant and purposeless, unable to produce the intricate order we see.

C) Will (lack of intent), Speech (silence), Sight (blindness), Hearing (deafness)
D) Existence (non-existence), Eternity (temporality), Oneness (multiplicity), Power (incapacity)

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi proves God's knowledge, life, power, and will by showing that their opposites would make creation of the perfect world impossible, and since the world is perfect, God must have these attributes.

The Four Attributes of the Possible
Sawi proves that every contingent thing must be specified by one of four attributes from the order of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the perfection of the world necessitates that every possible contingent thing is specified by one of four attributes, and why this implies a divine specifying will.

Core Idea
The world's perfection forces a choice: the possible is either specified by an attribute or stuck with its opposite.

In this lesson, we examine a compact rational proof from Imam al-Sawi. He argues that every possible contingent thing must be characterized by at least one of four attributes: existence/non-existence, quantity, time, and place/direction. Otherwise, it would be characterized by their opposites—ignorance, death, inability, and lack of purpose—and such a being could never produce the perfectly ordered universe we see. This argument establishes that the possible must be specified, pointing to the necessity of divine will as the specifying principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the concise style and the logical flow.
The Arabic uses a conditional structure starting with 'idh law lam...'.
2
Identify the four attributes
List the four categories mentioned: existence/non-existence, quantity, time, and place/direction. Notice that the author groups them as 'these four attributes'.
The opposites are listed as ignorance, death, inability, and lack of purpose.
3
Trace the conditional argument
Follow the logic: If not characterized by one of the four, then characterized by their opposites. This is a dichotomous choice.
This is a reductio ad absurdum structure.
4
Connect to the world's perfection
A being with these opposites cannot create the perfectly ordered world. Since the world is perfect, the possible must be specified by one of the four attributes.
The rhetorical question 'how, while the world exists in the most perfect order?' emphasizes the conclusion.
5
Understand the implication
The specification implies a specifying agent, which is the divine will. This proof shows the necessity of will as an attribute of God.
The author promises further clarification, indicating this is a concise presentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه من وجود أو عدم، ومقدار و زمان ومكان وجهة. إذ لو لم يتّصف بواحدة من هذه الصفات الأربعة لاتّصفَ بأضدادها، من جهل وموت وعَجْز وعَدَم قصد إلى شيء، والمتَصفُ بأضدادها لا يمكنه أن يخلق شيئاً من العالم البديع الإتقان، كيف والعالَمُ موجود على أتمّ النّظام؟ وسيأتي لهذا مزيد بيان.
The possible is specified by some of what is permissible for it: existence or non-existence, quantity, time, place, and direction. For if it were not characterized by one of these four attributes, it would be characterized by their opposites: ignorance, death, inability, and lack of purpose towards anything. And the one characterized by these opposites cannot create anything of the wonderfully precise world. How, while the world exists in the most perfect order? And there will come more clarification on this.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the necessity of divine will (irada) by showing that the possible thing (mumkin) must be specified by one of four attributes. The author begins by stating the attributes: existence/non-existence, quantity, time, place/direction. He then argues that if the possible thing were not characterized by one of these four, it would necessarily be characterized by their opposites: ignorance, death, inability, and lack of purpose. However, a thing with these opposites cannot be the creator of the wonderfully precise and well-ordered world. Since the world does exist in perfect order, the possible thing must indeed be specified by one of the attributes, implying a specifying agent—divine will. The proof is thus from the order of the world to the necessity of specification. The author ends by mentioning that more clarification will follow, indicating that this is a concise argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that the four attributes are properties of God rather than characteristics of the possible contingent thing.
Correction: The four attributes (existence/non-existence, quantity, time, place/direction) are qualities that apply to the possible created thing, not to God. God is not subject to these limitations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if the possible thing lacked all four attributes?
A) It would become necessary.
B) It would be characterized by their opposites: ignorance, death, inability, and lack of purpose.
C) It would cease to exist.
D) It would be independent of a Creator.

Key Takeaway
The world's perfect order necessitates that every possible contingent thing is specified by one of four attributes; otherwise it would be flawed and incapable of creating such a world.

?
When you face hardship, do you recognize it as part of God's perfect specification for you, or do you see it as random and meaningless? How can you train your heart to trust the wisdom behind His choices?
?
The passage warns that without divine specification, a thing is characterized by ignorance and lack of purpose. In what areas of your life do you operate with 'lack of purpose'—going through motions without intention? What one step can you take today to align your actions with God's will?

Everything That Occurs is Willed by God: Will vs. Command
Sawi explains that all existent things are willed by God, even if contrary to His command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all existent things are willed by God, even those contrary to His command, and how this differs from divine command.

Core Idea
Everything that happens is willed by God, whether He commanded it or not.

Sawi clarifies a crucial theological distinction: divine will (irada) and divine command (amr) are not synonymous. Everything that occurs—the faith of believers and the disbelief of disbelievers alike—is willed by God, even when it contradicts His command. This passage lays the foundation for understanding the four categories of events in relation to God's will and command.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the grammatical structure: 'كل شيء كائن' is subject, 'أراده' is predicate.
The passage uses a concise style.
2
Identify the principle
The passage states that every existent thing is willed by God. Find the evidence: 'فلا يقع في ملكه تعالى إلا ما يريد'.
3
Distinguish between will and command
The passage contrasts events that are commanded (e.g., faith of believers) with those that are forbidden but still occur (e.g., disbelief of Abu Jahl). Both are willed by God.
This guards against confusing God's will with His command.
4
Understand the implication
If something does not occur, it is not willed to occur, whether it was commanded or not. This yields four divisions.
The divisions are mentioned but not detailed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل شيء كائن أي موجود من الجواهر والأعراض وهذا مبتدأ وجملة قوله أراده أي أراد وجوده خبره فلا يقع في ملكه تعالى إلا ما يريد وهذا إذا كان الكائن قد أمر الله به كإيمان أبي بكر [؟] وكذا إيمان بقية المؤمنين بل وإن يكن بضده أي بضد ذلك الكائن قد أمرا بألف الإطلاق والضمير يعود عليه تعالى أي وإن كان ذلك الكائن قد أمر الله تعالى بضده ككفر أبي جهل لعنه الله وكذا كفر بقية الكافرين فإنه كائن وقد أمر الله بضده وهو الإيمان ونهى عنه ومع ذلك هو مراد له تعالى بدليل وقوعه والحاصل أن كل كائن أي واقع فهو مراد له تعالى سواء أمر به أو لا ومفهومه أن ما لم يكن فهو غير مراد الوقوع سواء أمر به كالإيمان من أبي جهل أو لم يأمر به كالكفر من المؤمنين فالأقسام أربعة كما يأتي.
And every thing that occurs, i.e., exists from substances and accidents, and this is a subject (mubtada') and the sentence 'He willed it' i.e., He willed its existence is its predicate. So nothing occurs in His kingdom except what He wills. And this is when the occurring thing has been commanded by God, like the faith of Abu Bakr [?], and similarly the faith of the rest of the believers. Rather, 'even if it is contrary to it', i.e., contrary to that occurring thing, He has commanded - with the alif of absolute release - and the pronoun refers to Him, i.e., even if that occurring thing, God has commanded its opposite, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl (may God curse him), and similarly the disbelief of the rest of the disbelievers. For it occurs, and God has commanded its opposite, which is faith, and forbade it, yet it is willed by Him due to the evidence of its occurrence. The conclusion: that every occurring thing, i.e., event, is willed by Him, whether He commanded it or not. And its implication: that whatever does not occur is not willed to occur, whether it was commanded - like faith from Abu Jahl - or not commanded - like disbelief from believers. So the divisions are four, as will come.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between divine will (irada) and divine command (amr). The author begins by stating a principle: everything that occurs—whether substances or accidents—is willed by God. The Arabic phrasing emphasizes that the predicate of 'everything that occurs' is 'He willed it.' Thus, no event falls outside divine will. The author then distinguishes between events that are commanded by God (like the faith of believers) and those that are not commanded but still occur (like the disbelief of Abu Jahl). Even when an event is contrary to God's command, it is still willed by God because it happens. This demonstrates that divine will encompasses all occurrences, regardless of divine command. The implication is that events that do not occur are not willed to occur, whether they were commanded (e.g., faith from Abu Jahl, which was commanded but did not happen) or not commanded (e.g., disbelief from believers, which was not commanded and did not happen). The author notes that this yields four divisions, which will be explained later. Thus, the passage guards against confusing God's will with His command.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That whatever God commands must be willed by Him, and whatever He wills must be commanded.
Correction: The passage shows that God commands faith but does not will it from disbelievers, and He wills disbelief from disbelievers though He forbids it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between divine will and the occurrence of events?
A) Only events that are commanded by God occur.
B) All events that occur are willed by God, even if they are contrary to His command.
C) God wills only those events that He commands.
D) Willed events are only those that are not commanded.

Key Takeaway
All events that occur are willed by God, whether commanded or not; what does not occur is not willed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In your heart, do you accept that His will is not measured by your moral expectations?
?
If God's command (what He wants from you) and His will (what He decrees) can diverge, where does that leave your own responsibility? Will you examine your life to see if you are fulfilling His command even when you cannot understand His decree, or do you let the difficulty of qadar excuse you from obeying?
?
The passage says 'nothing occurs in His kingdom except what He wills.' This includes your own choices and trials. Can you honestly thank God for every situation He wills for you—both the faith He granted you and the hardships you did not choose? If not, what specific area of your life reveals a struggle to trust that His will is perfectly wise?

Everything that Occurs is Willed by God: The Four Divisions
Al-Sawi explains that everything occurring is willed by God, with examples and four divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that everything that occurs in the universe is willed by God, regardless of whether He commands it, and you will be introduced to the four logical divisions that arise from this principle.

Core Idea
God's will encompasses all occurring things, even those contrary to His commands.

In this passage, al-Sawi explains a foundational principle of Sunni theology: nothing happens in God's dominion except by His will. He then illustrates this with examples from the lives of Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl, showing that even acts of disbelief occur by God's will, though He commands the opposite. This leads to a classification of four logical divisions that help clarify the relationship between God's will and His commands.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the grammatical structure where 'everything' is the subject and 'He willed it' is the predicate.
The passage uses a contrast between commanded and occurring.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage asserts that nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills. This is the central thesis.
3
Understand the distinction between will and command
Examine the examples: faith of Abu Bakr (commanded and occurs) vs. disbelief of Abu Jahl (not commanded but still occurs by God's will). This shows that will and command are not identical.
4
Recognize the implication for four divisions
The passage states that from this principle, four divisions follow: things that are commanded and occur, commanded and do not occur, not commanded and occur, and not commanded and do not occur. Try to classify the examples given.
The full enumeration is not given here but is implied.
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it affirms God's complete sovereignty while preserving the reality of human accountability (since commands are separate from will).
This lesson sets the stage for further discussion of qadar (divine decree).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلُّ شيء كائن) أي : موجود من الجواهر والأعراض، وهذا : مبتدأ ، وجملة قوله : ((أراده) أي : أراد وجوده خبره . فلا يقع في ملكه تعالى إلا ما يريد وهذا إذا كان الكائن قد أمر الله به؛ كإيمان أبي بكر الله ، وكذا إيمان بقيَّة المؤمنين، بل (وإن يكُنْ بضدّه)، أي: بضدّ ذلك الكائن قد أَمَرَا) - بألف الإطلاق - والضمير يعود عليه تعالى؛ أي: وإن كان ذلك الكائن قد أمر الله تعالى بضده؛ ككفر أبي جهل لعنه الله ، وكذا كفر بقيَّة الكافرين، فإنَّه كائن وقد أمر الله بضده ؛ وهو الإيمان، ونهى عنه ومع ذلك هو مراد له تعالى بدليل وقوعه. والحاصل : أنَّ كلَّ كائن أي ،واقع فهو مراد له تعالى، سواء أمر به أو لا، ومفهومه : أنَّ ما لم يكن فهو غير مراد الوقوع، سواء أمر به ؛ كالإيمان من أبي جهل، أو لم يأمر به؛ كالكفر من المؤمنين، فالأقسام أربعة كما يأتي .
('And everything that is') meaning: existing from substances and accidents. This is a mubtada', and the sentence 'He willed it' meaning: He willed its existence is its khabar. So nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. This is when that which occurs has been commanded by God, like the faith of Abu Bakr (رضي الله عنه), and likewise the faith of the rest of the believers. Rather, ('even if it be contrary to it'), i.e., contrary to that which occurs, He has commanded it with the alif of absolute form, and the pronoun refers to Him (تعالى); meaning: even if that which occurs God (تعالى) has commanded its opposite, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl (لعنه الله), and likewise the disbelief of the rest of the disbelievers, for it occurs and God has commanded its opposite, which is faith, and prohibited it, yet with that it is willed by Him (تعالى) by evidence of its occurrence. The conclusion: that every occurring thing, i.e., actual, is willed by Him (تعالى), whether commanded or not. Its implication is that what does not occur is not willed to occur, whether commanded, like faith from Abu Jahl, or not commanded, like disbelief from the believers. So the divisions are four as will come.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the statement 'and everything that is' from the main text: it means everything existing, whether substance or accident. Then it clarifies the grammatical structure: 'everything' is the subject, and the sentence 'He willed it' is the predicate. The key point is that nothing occurs in God's dominion except what He wills. However, the author makes a distinction: this principle holds both when the occurring thing has been commanded by God, like the faith of Abu Bakr, and even when it is contrary to what He commanded. For example, the disbelief of Abu Jahl occurs, even though God commanded its opposite (faith) and prohibited it. Yet it is still willed by God, because it actually happens. The conclusion is that everything that occurs is willed by God, whether commanded or not. Conversely, anything that does not occur is not willed to occur, whether it was commanded (like faith from Abu Jahl) or not commanded (like disbelief from believers). This yields four logical divisions: (1) commanded and occurs (obedience), (2) commanded and does not occur (disobedience that was commanded but not willed?), (3) not commanded and occurs (like disbelief from disbelievers? Actually, it is willed but not commanded), (4) not commanded and does not occur. The text says the divisions are four as will come later.

Key Terms

الإرادة
الأمر
الوقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Whatever occurs must be commanded by God.
Correction: God wills things that He does not command, such as disbelief. Will and command are distinct.

Checkpoint

A) Everything that occurs is willed by God only if He commands it.
B) Everything that occurs is willed by God, whether commanded or not.
C) Only good things are willed by God.
D) God wills only what He commands.

Key Takeaway
Everything that happens in existence is willed by God, whether it conforms to His commands or not.

The passage uses the example of Abu Jahl's disbelief being willed by God despite God commanding faith. When you pray for someone's guidance, do you truly submit to the fact that God may will otherwise? How can you pray with more sincerity, acknowledging God's sovereign will over all outcomes?

Distinguishing Divine Will from Command and Refuting the Mu'tazila
Al-Sawi distinguishes divine will from command and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's will (irāda) and His command (amr) are distinct attributes, and why the Mu'tazilite position that they are identical is incorrect.

Core Idea
Will and command are not the same; Allah can will what He does not command.

This lesson introduces a fundamental distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between what Allah wills and what He commands. Al-Sawi explains that will is an attribute of specification, while command relates to inner speech. The Mu'tazilites, by equating the two, concluded that Allah does not will evil because He does not command it—a view that Al-Sawi refutes using both rational and textual proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
Read the opening lines where Al-Sawi states that intention (qaṣd) means will, and it is not command nor does it entail command. Note the example of Abu Bakr's faith where both combine, and the possibility of each occurring alone.
2
Understand the definitions
Will is an attribute that specifies a possible thing with some permissible state; command is a matter of inner speech, like prohibition. These definitions show they are different.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite argument
Read the section describing the Mu'tazilite claim: since Allah does not command indecency, and will is identical to command, He cannot will evil. They argue that willing evil would be ugly, so most human actions are not willed or created by Allah.
This leads to a denial of Allah's omnipotence over all events.
4
Understand Al-Sawi's refutation
He denies the unity of will and command, citing the hadith 'Whatever Allah wills happens...'. He clarifies that ugliness is in acquisition and description, not in creation or willing. The final sentence states that reason and revelation testify to the corruption of the Mu'tazilite view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت ذلك (فالقَصْدُ) يعني : الإرادة، (غير الأمر) بالشَّيء، بل : ولا يستلزمه، كما أنه لا يستلزمها ، لما علمتَ أنَّهما قد يجتمعان في شيء كإيمان أبي بكر، وقد ينفردان، وذلك لأن الإرادة صفة تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، والأمر يرجع للكلام النفسي كالنهي . (فاطرح) أي : اترك (المرا) وهو : الجدال والنزاع الباطل من أَنَّه المعتزلة الذاهبين إلى أنه تعالى يقع في ملكه ما لا يريد، بناء على اتحاد الإرادة ،والأمر ، وهو تعالى لا يأمر بالفحشاء، فلا يريد القبائح كالكفر والمعاصي، وإلا لزم أنه يأمر بها ، وهو باطل، وحينئذ فهو تعالى لم يرد من الفاسق إلا إيمانه وطاعته لا كفره ومعصيته . قالوا : ولأنَّ إرادة القبائح قبيحة كخلقه وإيجاده، فعندهم أكثر ما يقع من أفعال العباد ليس بإرادة الله ولا بخَلْقِه وإيجاده، وإنَّما هو بمراد العبد وإيجاده ؛ وهو شنيع . قوله : (لما علمت أنهما قد يجتمعان في شيء) : أي : فبينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه يجتمعان في مادة، وينفرد كلُّ واحد في مادة. قوله كإيمان أبي بكر أي وسائر المؤمنين قوله : (بناء على اتحاد الإرادة والأمر ) : هذا قول بعض المعتزلة، وقال بعضهم : إنَّهما غيران، إلَّا أنَّ تعلُّق الإرادة تابع للأمر. قوله : (وحينئذ فهو تعالى . . . إلخ) : هذا من جملة كلام المعتزلة . قوله : (وهو شنيع) : أي : لأنه يلزم وقوع شيء في الكائنات قهراً عليه، فيلزمه إثبات العجز، تعالى الله ذلك . عن هذا، ونحن نمنع اتحاد الإرادة والأمر بدليل «ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، والقبيح إنَّما هو كسب القبائح والاتصافُ بها لا خَلْقُها وإرادتها، وبالجملة : ما ذهبوا إليه يشهد بفساده العقل والنقل . (فقد عَلِمْتَ من قولنا : وكل شيء كائن أراده ... إلخ) منطوقاً ومفهوماً ، أربعاً أقساماً) عطف بيان لأربع (في الكائنات) أي : ذات كائنة. جمع كائنة ؛ القسم الأول : مأمور به ومراد ؛ كإيمان أبي بكر ؛ الثاني : عكسه؛ كالكفر منه الثالث : مأمور غير مراد ؛ كالإيمان من أبي جهل، الرابع: عکسه ککفره (فاحفظ) هذا المقاما) فإنَّه قد زلّت فيه أقدام المعتزلة، ومعرفته واعتقاده على الوجه المتقدّم هو مذهب أهل السُّنَّة من سَلف الأمة وخَلَفِهم . قوله : (بدليل: «ما شاء الله كان . . . » إلخ ) : هذا لفظ حديث ورد عن رسول الله . قوله : (منطوقاً) : أي : وهو أنَّ ما شاءه وقع وإن لم يأمر به. وقوله : (ومفهوماً) : أي : وهو أنَّ ما لم يشأه لم يقع وإن أمر . قوله: (مأمور به ومراد . . . إلخ): عدل الشارح له عن التَّقسيم عنه.
And when you know that, (the intention) means: the will, (is not the command) of the thing, nay, nor does it entail it, just as it does not entail it, because you know that they may both combine in something like the faith of Abu Bakr, and each may occur alone, and that is because will is an attribute that specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it, while command refers to the inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) like prohibition. (So discard) i.e., abandon (disputation), which is vain argument and strife, from that the Mu'tazila who go to that He, exalted, occurs in His kingdom what He does not will, based on the unity of will and command, and He, exalted, does not command indecency, so He does not will ugly things like disbelief and sins, otherwise it would necessitate that He commands them, and that is false, and then He, exalted, did not will from the sinner except his faith and obedience, not his disbelief and disobedience. They said: And because willing ugly things is ugly like creating and bringing them into existence, so according to them most of what occurs from the actions of servants is not by God's will nor by His creation and bringing into existence, but rather it is by the servant's will and his bringing into existence; and that is reprehensible. His statement: (because you know that they may both combine in something): i.e., so between them is general and specific from one aspect; they combine in one matter, and each is separate in one matter. His statement: like the faith of Abu Bakr, i.e., and the rest of the believers. His statement: (based on the unity of will and command): this is the saying of some of the Mu'tazila, and some of them said: they are two different things, but the attachment of will is dependent on command. His statement: (and then He, exalted... etc.): this is part of the speech of the Mu'tazila. His statement: (and that is reprehensible): i.e., because it necessitates the occurrence of something in the universe forcibly upon Him, so it necessitates establishing incapacity—exalted is God above that. Against this, we deny the unity of will and command by the proof: “Whatever God wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen,” and ugliness is only the acquisition of ugly things and being described by them, not their creation and willing them. In short, what they have gone to is testified to its corruption by reason and revelation. (So you have known from our saying: “And every thing that happens He wills... etc.”) by explicit wording (mantūq) and inferred meaning (mafhūm), (four sections) as an explanatory apposition to “four” (in the things that occur) i.e., existing entities, plural of existing. First section: commanded and willed; like the faith of Abu Bakr. Second: its opposite; like his disbelief. Third: commanded not willed; like the faith of Abu Jahl. Fourth: its opposite like his disbelief. (So preserve this position) for the feet of the Mu'tazila have slipped in it, and knowing it and believing it in the aforementioned manner is the way of the Ahl al-Sunna from the early and later generations of the community. His statement: (by the proof: “Whatever God wills happens... etc.”): this is the wording of a hadith reported from the Messenger of God. His statement: (by explicit wording): i.e., it is that what He wills occurs even if He does not command it. And his statement: (and inferred meaning): i.e., it is that what He does not will does not occur even if He commands it. His statement: (commanded and willed... etc.): the commentator revised the classification from what he had.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal exposition on the distinction between divine will (irāda) and command (amr), a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view that conflates them, and a classification of things in existence. The author begins by stating that intention (al-qaṣd) means will, and it is not the same as command, nor does one entail the other. They can combine (as in the faith of Abu Bakr) or occur separately. The reason is that will is an attribute that specifies a possible thing with some of its permissible states, while command is a matter of inner speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) like prohibition. The author then instructs to discard vain disputation, referring to the Mu'tazilites who claim that God's will and command are identical, and since God does not command indecency, He does not will ugly acts like disbelief. They argue that willing ugliness would be ugly itself, so most human actions are not willed or created by God but by the servant. The author labels this as reprehensible because it implies something occurs in the universe forcibly upon God, necessitating incapacity. The author then refutes by denying the unity of will and command, citing the hadith 'Whatever God wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen,' and clarifies that ugliness lies in acquisition and description, not in creation or willing. Then the author presents a fourfold division learned from the statement 'every thing that happens He wills': (1) commanded and willed (e.g., Abu Bakr's faith), (2) its opposite (commanded and not willed? No, the second is 'its opposite' meaning not commanded and not willed? Actually the text says 'second: its opposite; like his disbelief' meaning Abu Bakr's disbelief? That would be not commanded and not willed? But the classification is: (1) commanded and willed, (2) not commanded and not willed (the opposite of the first), (3) commanded but not willed (like the faith of Abu Jahl, which God commands but does not will because He knows he won't believe), (4) not commanded but willed (like the disbelief of Abu Jahl, which God wills but does not command). The commentary clarifies that this classification is the position of Ahl al-Sunna. The author concludes by advising to preserve this position as the correct Sunni doctrine.

Key Terms
إرادة (irāda: will)
أمر (amr: command)
كلام نفسي (kalām nafsī: inner speech)
المراء (al-mirā': disputation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something, He must also command it, or that God cannot will evil because He does not command it.
Correction: God's will and command are distinct; He may will something without commanding it, and command something without willing it (e.g., He commands faith from Abu Jahl but does not will it).

Checkpoint
According to Al-Sawi, why is the Mu'tazilite position that God does not will evil because He does not command it incorrect?
A) Because will and command are identical
B) Because will and command are distinct, and God may will what He does not command
C) Because God commands all things that happen
D) Because evil does not exist

Key Takeaway
Allah's will and command are not identical; His will encompasses all that occurs, while His command prescribes what is good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that everything happens by Allah's will. But when you commit a sin, do you truly accept that Allah willed it to occur—even though He hates it and does not command it? How does that acceptance affect your view of your own responsibility and your need for repentance? What would it mean to you if you believed Allah did not will that sin? Would that make your accountability lighter, or heavier?
?
Consider the Mu'tazilite argument: 'If God willed ugliness, He would be ugly.' You now know this is false because ugliness is in acquisition, not creation. But in your own life, when you see evil or suffering, do you ever indirectly attribute that evil to Allah in your heart? How might this lesson correct that subtle assumption? What practical step can you take today to realign your heart with the truth that Allah's will is perfect, even when events are painful?
?
The hadith says: 'Whatever Allah wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen.' Do you live as if this is true for every detail of your life—including your failures, your family, your health, and your deen? Or do you sometimes act as if some things escape His will? What area of your life do you most struggle to submit to this reality? Write down one concrete way you will consciously affirm Allah's will in that area this week.

The Fourfold Classification of Things in Existence
Al-Sawi presents the fourfold classification of things in existence based on divine will and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of things in existence based on whether they are commanded and/or willed by Allah.

Core Idea
Everything in existence falls into one of four categories: commanded and willed, commanded but not willed, willed but not commanded, or neither.

Building on the distinction between will and command, Al-Sawi presents a fourfold division of all existing things (al-kā'ināt). This classification, derived from the statement 'Every thing that happens He wills,' clarifies how divine will and command interact in reality. Understanding this is key to the Sunni position on predestination and free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourfold division
Read the passage listing the four sections: 1) commanded and willed, 2) not commanded and not willed, 3) commanded not willed, 4) willed not commanded. Note the examples for each with Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl.
2
Understand the derivation
This division is derived from the statement 'Every thing that happens He wills' by its explicit wording (mantūq) and implied meaning (mafhūm). Understand how these two aspects produce four categories.
The explicit wording gives the first two categories, and the implied meaning gives the last two.
3
Reflect on the significance
Al-Sawi advises to preserve this position as the Sunni doctrine, noting that the Mu'tazila slipped here. Consider why this classification is crucial for affirming God's omnipotence and justice.

(فقد عَلِمْتَ من قولنا : وكل شيء كائن أراده ... إلخ) منطوقاً ومفهوماً ، أربعاً أقساماً) عطف بيان لأربع (في الكائنات) أي : ذات كائنة. جمع كائنة ؛ القسم الأول : مأمور به ومراد ؛ كإيمان أبي بكر ؛ الثاني : عكسه؛ كالكفر منه الثالث : مأمور غير مراد ؛ كالإيمان من أبي جهل، الرابع: عکسه ککفره (فاحفظ) هذا المقاما) فإنَّه قد زلّت فيه أقدام المعتزلة، ومعرفته واعتقاده على الوجه المتقدّم هو مذهب أهل السُّنَّة من سَلف الأمة وخَلَفِهم . قوله : (بدليل: «ما شاء الله كان . . . » إلخ ) : هذا لفظ حديث ورد عن رسول الله . قوله : (منطوقاً) : أي : وهو أنَّ ما شاءه وقع وإن لم يأمر به. وقوله : (ومفهوماً) : أي : وهو أنَّ ما لم يشأه لم يقع وإن أمر . قوله: (مأمور به ومراد . . . إلخ): عدل الشارح له عن التَّقسيم عنه.
And when you know that, (the intention) means: the will, (is not the command) of the thing, nay, nor does it entail it, just as it does not entail it, because you know that they may both combine in something like the faith of Abu Bakr, and each may occur alone, and that is because will is an attribute that specifies the possible with some of what is permissible for it, while command refers to the inner speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) like prohibition. (So discard) i.e., abandon (disputation), which is vain argument and strife, from that the Mu'tazila who go to that He, exalted, occurs in His kingdom what He does not will, based on the unity of will and command, and He, exalted, does not command indecency, so He does not will ugly things like disbelief and sins, otherwise it would necessitate that He commands them, and that is false, and then He, exalted, did not will from the sinner except his faith and obedience, not his disbelief and disobedience. They said: And because willing ugly things is ugly like creating and bringing them into existence, so according to them most of what occurs from the actions of servants is not by God's will nor by His creation and bringing into existence, but rather it is by the servant's will and his bringing into existence; and that is reprehensible. His statement: (because you know that they may both combine in something): i.e., so between them is general and specific from one aspect; they combine in one matter, and each is separate in one matter. His statement: like the faith of Abu Bakr, i.e., and the rest of the believers. His statement: (based on the unity of will and command): this is the saying of some of the Mu'tazila, and some of them said: they are two different things, but the attachment of will is dependent on command. His statement: (and then He, exalted... etc.): this is part of the speech of the Mu'tazila. His statement: (and that is reprehensible): i.e., because it necessitates the occurrence of something in the universe forcibly upon Him, so it necessitates establishing incapacity—exalted is God above that. Against this, we deny the unity of will and command by the proof: “Whatever God wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen,” and ugliness is only the acquisition of ugly things and being described by them, not their creation and willing them. (So you have known from our saying: “And every thing that happens He wills... etc.”) by explicit wording (mantūq) and inferred meaning (mafhūm), (four sections) as an explanatory apposition to “four” (in the things that occur) i.e., existing entities, plural of existing. First section: commanded and willed; like the faith of Abu Bakr. Second: its opposite; like his disbelief. Third: commanded not willed; like the faith of Abu Jahl. Fourth: its opposite like his disbelief. (So preserve this position) for the feet of the Mu'tazila have slipped in it, and knowing it and believing it in the aforementioned manner is the way of the Ahl al-Sunna from the early and later generations of the community. His statement: (by the proof: “Whatever God wills happens... etc.”): this is the wording of a hadith reported from the Messenger of God. His statement: (by explicit wording): i.e., it is that what He wills occurs even if He does not command it. And his statement: (and inferred meaning): i.e., it is that what He does not will does not occur even if He commands it. His statement: (commanded and willed... etc.): the commentator revised the classification from what he had.

This classification depends on distinguishing Allah's will from His command. In your own life, where have you mistakenly thought that if something is good and commanded, it must always happen—or if it does not happen, it must not be from Allah? How can you live with the tension of obeying commands even when outcomes differ, trusting that Allah's will is perfect?

Where in your understanding of Islam do you risk a similar confusion—expecting that your obedience guarantees worldly success or that suffering means Allah's displeasure? The Fourfold Division of Things by Command and Will
Al-Sawi proves distinction between will and command and classifies four categories of things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between Allah's will and command, and the four categories of things based on their relation to both.

Core Idea
Allah may command what He does not will, and will what He does not command.

In this passage, al-Sawi addresses a key point of Sunni theology: the relationship between divine will and command. He provides a proof from a well-known hadith and then classifies all created things into four logical categories based on whether they are commanded and/or willed by Allah. This classification clarifies a common misunderstanding and defends Sunni orthodoxy against Mu'tazilite views.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Sawi uses a very compressed style but the examples clarify the categories.
2
Understand the proof
Identify the hadith used as proof and how it distinguishes will from command. Note the two aspects: direct wording (mantuq) and implication (mafhum).
The hadith is 'Whatever Allah wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen.'
3
Learn the four categories
Go through each of the four categories with their examples: (1) commanded and willed (Abu Bakr's faith), (2) not commanded and not willed (Abu Bakr's disbelief), (3) commanded but not willed (Abu Jahl's faith), (4) not commanded but willed (Abu Jahl's disbelief). Ask yourself why each example fits.
These categories show that command and will can diverge.
4
Recognize the Sunni position
Note that al-Sawi warns against the Mu'tazila error and emphasizes that this classification is the school of Ahl al-Sunna. Reflect on why this distinction matters for understanding divine justice and human responsibility.
The Mu'tazila tended to equate command with will, leading to problematic conclusions about evil.
5
Review the commentator's adjustment
Read the final part about why the commentator rephrased the division. Understand that the division properly applies to the objects (commanded and willed) not to the attributes themselves.
This nuance prevents misunderstanding about metaphorical language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا، ونحن نمنع اتحاد الإرادة والأمر بدليل «ما شاء الله كان، وما لم يشأ لم يكن ، والقبيح إنَّما هو كسب القبائح والاتصافُ بها لا خَلْقُها وإرادتها، وبالجملة : ما ذهبوا إليه يشهد بفساده العقل والنقل . (فقد عَلِمْتَ من قولنا : وكل شيء كائن أراده ... إلخ) منطوقاً ومفهوماً ، أربعاً أقساماً) عطف بيان لأربع (في الكائنات) أي : ذات كائنة. جمع كائنة ؛ القسم الأول : مأمور به ومراد ؛ كإيمان أبي بكر ؛ الثاني : عكسه؛ كالكفر منه الثالث : مأمور غير مراد ؛ كالإيمان من أبي جهل، الرابع: عکسه ککفره (فاحفظ) هذا المقاما) فإنَّه قد زلّت فيه أقدام المعتزلة، ومعرفته واعتقاده على الوجه المتقدّم هو مذهب أهل السُّنَّة من سَلف الأمة وخلَفِهم . قوله : (بدليل: «ما شاء الله كان . . . » إلخ ) : هذا لفظ حديث ورد عن رسول الله . قوله : (منطوقاً) : أي : وهو أنَّ ما شاءه وقع وإن لم يأمر به. وقوله : (ومفهوماً) : أي : وهو أنَّ ما لم يشأه لم يقع وإن أمر . قوله: (مأمور به ومراد . . . إلخ): عدل الشارح له عن التَّقسيم المشهور، وهو قولهم : (فقد يأمر ويريد . . إلخ)؛ لما فيه من التَّجوز؛ فإنَّ التقسيم للمتعلّق وهو المأمور به والمراد، لا للأمر والإرادة.
This is, and we deny the union of will and command by the evidence: 'Whatever Allah wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen,' and evil is only the acquisition of ugly acts and being described by them, not their creation and willing them. In summary, what they have gone to is witnessed by intellect and transmission to be corrupt. (You have known from our statement: 'And everything that occurs He willed... etc.') by direct wording (mantuqan) and implication (mafhoman), four divisions – an appositional clarification of 'four' – (in the created things) meaning: ever-existing entities, plural of 'existing'. The first division: commanded and willed, like the faith of Abu Bakr; the second: its opposite, like the disbelief from him; the third: commanded but not willed, like the faith of Abu Jahl; the fourth: its opposite, like his disbelief. (So memorize this station) for the feet of the Mu'tazila have slipped in it, and knowing it and believing it in the aforementioned manner is the school of the People of the Sunnah from the predecessors of the community and its successors. His statement: 'By the evidence: 'Whatever Allah wills... etc.'' this is the wording of a hadith reported from the Messenger of Allah. His statement: 'By direct wording (mantuqan)' i.e., that what He wills occurs even if He did not command it. And his statement: 'And by implication (mafhoman)' i.e., that what He does not will does not occur even if He commands it. His statement: 'Commanded and willed... etc.': the commentator (al-sharih) changed from the well-known division, which is their statement: 'He may command and will... etc.' because of the metaphor in it; for the division pertains to the object of command and will, not to command and will themselves.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification. Al-Sawi begins by denying that divine will and command are identical, citing the hadith 'Whatever Allah wills happens, and whatever He does not will does not happen.' He notes that evil acts are acquired by humans but not created or willed by Allah in the same way as good. He then presents a fourfold division of things in the created world, based on their relation to command and will: (1) commanded and willed (e.g., the faith of Abu Bakr), (2) not commanded and not willed (its opposite, e.g., the disbelief of Abu Bakr – though this seems contradictory, it refers to disbelief that was not his but hypothetically), (3) commanded but not willed (e.g., the faith of Abu Jahl, which Allah commands but does not will because Abu Jahl does not believe), (4) not commanded but willed (e.g., the disbelief of Abu Jahl, which Allah wills as a created act but does not command). He then warns that the Mu'tazila stumbled here, and this division is the Sunni position. Then he explains the terms 'mantuqan' (direct wording) and 'mafhoman' (implication): direct wording means that what Allah wills occurs even if He did not command it; implication means that what He does not will does not occur even if He commands it. Finally, he explains why the commentator (al-sharih) changed the wording from the well-known division ('He may command and will, etc.') – because the division properly applies to the objects (the commanded and the willed), not to the acts of commanding and willing themselves.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر
المأمور به
المراد
الكائنات
المعتزلة
منطوق
مفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Command and will are identical; whatever Allah commands He also wills to occur from the commanded person.
Correction: The passage shows that command and will can diverge: Allah may command something (like Abu Jahl's faith) but not will it to actually occur from that person, and He may will something (like Abu Jahl's disbelief) without commanding it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what example is given for something that is commanded but not willed?
A) The faith of Abu Bakr
B) The disbelief of Abu Jahl
C) The faith of Abu Jahl
D) The disbelief of Abu Bakr

Key Takeaway
The fourfold division of things based on command and will is essential for understanding Sunni theology and refuting the Mu'tazila.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your faith is either commanded and willed like Abu Bakr's, or commanded but not willed like Abu Jahl's. Which one is it, and what evidence from your life supports that?
?
If Allah does not will your sin, yet it still occurs, does that make you more or less responsible? How does this truth change the way you approach repentance?
?
When you face a trial you did not choose, do you submit to Allah's will even when He did not command it? How can you train your heart to see His hand in both command and will?

Defining Divine Speech as an Eternal Self-Subsisting Attribute
Al-Sawi defines divine speech and refutes the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech (kalam) is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not composed of letters or sound, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not created letters and sounds.

This lesson focuses on the first part of Al-Sawi's definition of divine speech. He establishes that speech is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute, and explains why the term 'self-subsisting' is used to counter the Mu'tazila who deny such an attribute. He also clarifies why both 'letter' and 'sound' are negated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source once, then the translation. Pay attention to the definition and the two explanatory comments.
Notice the reason for using 'nafsiyya'.
2
Identify the three key claims
Find the claims: speech is an attribute of meanings; it is eternal and self-subsisting; it is not letters or sound.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
Note why 'nafsiyya' is mentioned: to refute the Mu'tazila who say God's speech is created.
This is a key doctrinal point.
4
Grasp the negation of letter and sound
Understand the logical reason: negating the specific (letter) does not negate the general (sound), so both are explicitly denied.
Sound is defined as compression of air.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامس صفات المعاني (كلامه تعالى، وهو: صفة أزلية نفسية، ليست بحرف ولا صوت تدل على جميع المعلومات . قوله : (نفسيَّة) : أي : قائمة بالنَّفس ؛ أي: الذات، وعبّر عنها بـ(نفسيَّة) دون سائر الصفات؛ ردّاً على المعتزلة القائلين: ليس الله كلام نفسي، بل معنى كونه متكلّماً خلق الكلام. قوله : (ليست بحرف ولا صوت الحرف أخصُّ من الصوت، ولمَّا كان لا يلزم من نفي الأخص نفي الأعم ؛ ذكر الأعم بعده، وإنَّما كان الصَّوت أعم من الحرف؛ لأنَّ الكيفية الحاصلة عند انضغاط الهواء وانحباسه بشيء صوت، سواء انحبس في مخرج من مخارج الحروف، ويقال له حرف وصوت أو في غير ذلك، ويقال للكيفية الحاصلة حينئذ : صوت فقط . واعلم أنَّ كلام الله تعالى يطلق بالاشتراك على الحسي والنَّفسي، الذي هو الصفة القديمة، فهو حقيقة عرفيَّة في كلّ، فالحسي : ما كان بحرف وصوت ومدلوله بعض مدلول الكلام النَّفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى والنَّفسي : ما ليس بحرف ولا صوت، ولا يوصف بتقديم ولا تأخير، ولا تقسيم ولا بداية ولا نهاية، وهو قديم ليس بمخلوق، فالكتب السَّماوية دالة على بعض مدلول الكلام النَّفسي، ولا يحيط بكلِّ مدلوله إِلَّا هو ؛ لأنَّ مدلول الكلام النَّفسيّ الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات تفصيلاً .
And the fifth of the attributes of meanings is His speech (kalam), and it is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute, not composed of letter nor sound, indicating all known things. His statement: 'Self-subsisting (nafsiyya): i.e., subsisting in the self (nafs); i.e., the essence, and He expressed it as 'self-subsisting' apart from the other attributes as a refutation of the Mu'tazila who say: 'Allah does not have a self-subsisting speech, but rather the meaning of His being 'speaking' is that He creates speech.' His statement: 'Not composed of letter nor sound: The letter is more specific than the sound, and since the negation of the more specific does not necessitate the negation of the more general, He mentioned the more general after it. Sound is more general than letter because the quality that occurs when air is compressed and confined by something is sound, whether it is confined at an articulation point of the letters – in which case it is called both letter and sound – or in other than that, in which case the quality occurring is called sound only. And know that the speech of Allah (may He be exalted) is used homonymously for the sensory and the self-subsisting (nafsi), which is the eternal attribute, so it is a customary reality in each. The sensory is that which consists of letter and sound, and its indication is part of the indication of the eternal self-subsisting speech subsisting in His essence. The self-subsisting is that which is not composed of letter nor sound, and is not described with precedence nor delay, nor with division nor beginning nor end, and it is eternal, not created. The heavenly scriptures indicate some of the meaning of the self-subsisting speech, and none encompasses all of its meaning except Him, because the meaning of the self-subsisting speech is the necessary, impossible, and possible things in detail.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of divine speech (kalam) and refutes the Mu'tazila. It begins by stating that speech is the fifth of the 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani). It is defined as an eternal (azaliyya), self-subsisting (nafsiyya) attribute – meaning it subsists in God's essence – and it is not composed of letters or sounds; rather it indicates all known things. The author then explains why the term 'self-subsisting' (nafsiyya) is used: to refute the Mu'tazila, who deny that God has a self-subsisting speech and instead claim that God's 'speaking' means He creates speech in some substrate. Next, the author clarifies why both 'letter' and 'sound' are negated: letter is more specific than sound; negating the specific does not imply negating the general, so the general is mentioned after. Sound is more general because it arises from compression of air, whether at a letter's point of articulation (then it is both letter and sound) or elsewhere (then it is sound only). Finally, the author explains that the term 'speech of Allah' is used homonymously for two things: sensory speech (consisting of letters and sounds, like the Qur'an) and the self-subsisting attribute (the eternal speech in God's essence). Sensory speech indicates only part of the meaning of the self-subsisting speech. The self-subsisting speech is not described by time (precedence/delay), division, beginning, or end; it is eternal and not created. The heavenly scriptures (Torah, Gospel, Qur'an) indicate some of that meaning, but only Allah encompasses all of it, because the self-subsisting speech includes detailed knowledge of all necessary, impossible, and possible things.

Key Terms
كلام
صفة نفسية
أزلية
حرف
صوت
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's speech is composed of letters and sounds because scriptures are written.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine speech itself is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds; scriptures are sensory expressions that indicate that attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does the author use the term 'nafsiyya' for the attribute of speech?
A) To emphasize that speech is not a letter or sound
B) To refute the Mu'tazila who deny self-subsisting speech
C) To indicate that speech is eternal
D) To show that speech indicates all known things

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and it is not created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you truly believe you are engaging with an eternal attribute of God, or do you treat it as a mere book? How does this belief change the reverence and urgency with which you approach its commands?
?
The Mu'tazila denied God's eternal speech because they could not accept letters and sounds subsisting in God. Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah because it didn't fit your rational expectations? What would it take for you to submit fully to revealed truths even when they surpass your comprehension?
?
If Allah's speech is eternal and not created, then His command to you is as timeless as He is. In what area of your life are you delaying obedience to a clear command from the Qur'an? What would change if you truly believed that command was spoken directly to you from eternity?

Sensory vs. Self-Subsisting Divine Speech
Al-Sawi distinguishes sensory and self-subsisting divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensory speech (scriptures) and the self-subsisting attribute of divine speech, and that the latter is eternal and encompasses all knowledge.

Core Idea
Scriptures are sensory indicators of the eternal, self-subsisting speech.

This lesson covers the latter part of Al-Sawi's explanation, where he distinguishes between two meanings of 'speech of Allah': sensory speech (letters and sounds) and the self-subsisting attribute (eternal, not created). Sensory speech, like the Qur'an, indicates only part of the self-subsisting speech, which encompasses all necessary, impossible, and possible things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on homonymous usage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that 'kalam' is used for two things.
2
Define sensory speech
Identify that sensory speech consists of letters and sounds and indicates part of the self-subsisting speech.
Example: revealed scriptures.
3
Define self-subsisting speech
Note its properties: no letters/sound, no time, no division, eternal, not created.
It is the attribute subsisting in God.
4
Understand the scope
Recognize that scriptures only indicate some of the self-subsisting speech's meaning; only Allah encompasses all.
The self-subsisting speech details all necessary, impossible, and possible.

Divine Speech as a Self-Subsisting Attribute
Al-Sawi defines the fifth attribute of meanings — kalam — and explains its nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Allah's speech as an eternal, self-subsisting attribute that is not composed of letters or sound, and the refutation of the Mu'tazila view.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is a self-subsisting, eternal attribute—not letters or sound.

In this lesson, Al-Sawi defines the fifth divine attribute among the attributes of meanings: Speech (kalam). He emphasizes that it is a self-subsisting attribute (sifa nafsiyya), not composed of letters or sound, directly refuting the Mu'tazila who claimed Allah's speech is created. You'll explore the precise terminology and the logical distinction between letter and sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kalam
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully, noting the key terms: كلام, صفة نفسية, حرف, صوت.
Focus on the opening statement: 'صفة أزلية نفسية، ليست بحرف ولا صوت'.
2
Understand 'self-subsisting' (nafsiyya)
Notice why the author uses 'nafsiyya' specifically—to refute the Mu'tazila who denied an eternal speech subsisting in Allah's essence.
The term 'nafsiyya' means 'subsisting in the self/essence'.
3
Analyze the negation of letters and sound
Recognize that both letters and sounds are negated separately. Sound is more general; negating letters does not automatically negate sound, so both are mentioned.
The author provides a physical definition of sound to illustrate the generality.
4
Consider the figurative expression
Note the comment about 'He may command and intend'—this division is figurative because it applies to objects, not attributes.
This clarifies that command and intention are not parts of speech but related actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامس صفات المعاني (كلامه تعالى، وهو: صفة أزلية نفسية، ليست بحرف ولا صوت تدل على جميع المعلومات . المشهور، وهو قولهم : (فقد يأمر ويريد . . إلخ)؛ لما فيه من التَّجوز؛ فإنَّ التقسيم للمتعلّق وهو المأمور به والمراد، لا للأمر والإرادة. قوله : (نفسيَّة) : أي : قائمة بالنَّفس ؛ أي: الذات، وعبّر عنها بـ(نفسيَّة) دون سائر الصفات؛ ردّاً على المعتزلة القائلين: ليس الله كلام نفسي، بل معنى كونه متكلّماً خلق الكلام. قوله : (ليست بحرف ولا صوت الحرف أخصُّ من الصوت، ولمَّا كان لا يلزم من نفي الأخص نفي الأعم ؛ ذكر الأعم بعده، وإنَّما كان الصَّوت أعم من الحرف؛ لأنَّ الكيفية الحاصلة عند انضغاط الهواء وانحباسه بشيء صوت، سواء انحبس في مخرج من مخارج الحروف، ويقال له حرف وصوت أو في غير ذلك، ويقال للكيفية الحاصلة حينئذ : صوت فقط.
And the fifth of the attributes of meanings is (His speech, Exalted is He, and it is an eternal self-subsisting attribute, not a letter nor a sound, indicating all known things. The well-known (statement), which is their saying: 'For He may command and intend... etc.'; due to the figurativeness therein; for the division pertains to the object of application, which is the commanded thing and the intended thing, not to the command and the intention. His saying: (self-subsisting) i.e., subsisting in the self, i.e., the essence, and he expressed it as (self-subsisting) to the exclusion of other attributes; as a refutation of the Mu'tazila who say: Allah has no self-subsisting speech, rather the meaning of His being speaking is the creation of speech. His saying: (not a letter nor a sound) – the letter is more specific than the sound, and since negating the more specific does not necessitate negating the more general, he mentioned the more general after it. And indeed sound is more general than letter because the quality produced when air is compressed and confined by something is sound, whether it is confined in one of the points of articulation of letters – and it is called a letter and a sound – or otherwise, and the quality produced then is called sound only.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the fifth divine attribute among the attributes of meanings: Speech (kalam). The author begins by stating that Allah's speech is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute (sifa nafsiyya), not composed of letters or sound, and that it indicates all known things. He then addresses a common expression from the scholars: 'For He may command and intend...' – noting that this division is figurative because the division actually applies to the objects (the commanded thing and the intended thing), not to the attributes of command and intention themselves. Next, he explains why the attribute is called 'self-subsisting' (nafsiyya): it means it is subsisting in the self or essence, and this term is used specifically to refute the Mu'tazila who denied that Allah has an eternal, self-subsisting speech, claiming instead that His being 'speaking' means He creates speech. Finally, he clarifies the negation of 'letter' and 'sound': since letter is a subset of sound, negating 'letter' does not automatically negate 'sound', so the author explicitly negates both. He then provides a physical definition of sound as the quality produced by compressed air, and distinguishes between sound that is articulated as a letter and sound that is not, showing that the divine attribute transcends both.

Key Terms
كلام
صفة نفسية
حرف
صوت
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating letters from divine speech implies negating sounds entirely, but the author shows that both are negated separately.
Correction: The author explicitly negates both letters and sounds, clarifying that sound is more general; negating the specific (letter) does not automatically negate the general (sound), so both must be stated.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Sawi mention the expression 'فقد يأمر ويريد' (He may command and intend)?
A) To affirm that command and intention are distinct attributes.
B) To show that the division of speech into command and prohibition is figurative.
C) To prove that Allah's speech is composed of letters.
D) To refute the Mu'tazila on the creation of speech.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal, self-subsisting attribute, not composed of letters or sound, and it indicates all known things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah’s eternal speech is not letters or sound. Yet you recite the Quran with letters and sounds. Does your heart merely pass over the recitation, or do you strive to connect to the eternal meaning (ma'na) that is Allah's actual speech? What will you do in your next recitation to shift from focusing on the form to focusing on the inherent meaning?
?
The Mu'tazila denied Allah's self-subsisting speech because they could not conceive of speech without letters or sound. Are there any of Allah's attributes that you find difficult to accept because they don't match your created experience? Will you choose to submit to revelation, even when it defies analogy?
?

How does the vastness of this attribute affect your trust in Allah's knowledge of your struggles, your future, and your innermost thoughts? Will you turn to His eternal speech—the Quran—seeking guidance for areas of your life where you lack certainty?

The Two Meanings of Allah's Speech
Al-Sawi defines and contrasts the two meanings of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensible speech (kalam hissi) and self-subsisting speech (kalam nafsi) as attributes of Allah, and why the division of speech applies only to the indicated meanings.

Core Idea
Allah's speech has two meanings: created letters and an eternal attribute.

In Ash'ari theology, the term 'speech' is used homonymously for two realities: the created letters and sounds we hear in revelation, and the eternal, uncreated attribute that subsists in Allah's essence. Al-Sawi explains that the heavenly books indicate only part of that eternal speech, and that the division into command, prohibition, etc. applies to the meanings conveyed, not to the attribute itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two types of speech
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully, noting the definitions of 'sensible speech' and 'self-subsisting speech'.
Notice that 'customary truth' means the term is commonly understood in both senses.
2
Identify the attributes of each type
List the attributes: sensible speech has letters/sound, is created, and indicates part of the self-subsisting speech. Self-subsisting speech has no letters/sound, is eternal, and indicates the necessary, impossible, and possible in detail.
3
Understand the implication for heavenly books
Ask yourself: Why can't the heavenly books contain all of what the self-subsisting speech indicates? The passage explains that they indicate some things in detail and all things in summary.
