واعلم أنَّ كلام الله تعالى يطلق بالاشتراك على الحسي والنَّفسي، الذي هو الصفة القديمة، فهو حقيقة عرفيَّة في كلّ، فالحسي : ما كان بحرف وصوت ومدلوله بعض مدلول الكلام النَّفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى والنَّفسي : ما ليس بحرف ولا صوت، ولا يوصف بتقديم ولا تأخير، ولا تقسيم ولا بداية ولا نهاية، وهو قديم ليس بمخلوق، فالكتب السَّماوية دالة على بعض مدلول الكلام النَّفسي، ولا يحيط بكلِّ مدلوله إِلَّا هو ؛ لأنَّ مدلول الكلام النَّفسيّ الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات تفصيلاً . وأمَّا الكتب السماوية : فقد دلّت على بعض الواجبات تفصيلاً، وكلُّ الواجبات إجمالاً وكذا المستحيلات والجائزات وتكليم الله لموسى على الجبل كان بالكلام النَّفسيّ على التحقيق عند الأشاعرة وبعض الماتريدية، خلافاً للمعتزلة والبعض الآخر من الماتريدية، فتقسيم الكلام إلى أمر ونهي وخبر واستخبار ووعد ووعيد إنَّما هو لتلك المدلولات التي دلّ عليها الكلام الحسي . وأما الصِّفة القديمة : فيستحيل انقسامها كما علمت. أخرج الطبراني عن سعيد بن جبير عن ابن عباس قال: (أوحى الله إلى موسى إنِّي جعلت فيك عشرة آلاف سمع حتّى سمعت كلامي، وعشرة آلاف لسان حتَّى أجبتني) . وأخرج القضاعي : أنَّ الله ناجى موسى بمئة ألف كلمة وأربعين ألف كلمة، فأشرق وجهه بالنُّور لما جاء من عند ربه؛ ليعرف الناس صدق ما ادعاه، فما رآه أحد إِلَّا فكان عمي، الرَّائي إليه وجهه بثوب ممَّا يمسح عليه فيرد الله عليه بصره، فتبرقع؛ لئلا تذهب أبصار الناس عند رؤيته، وبقي البرقع على وجهه إلى أن مات وكان يسدُّ أذنيه بعد رجوعه من المناجاة مدة؛ لئلا يسمع كلام النَّاس فيموت من وحشة قبحه، وصار
And know that the speech of Allah, Exalted is He, is used by homonymy for the sensible and the self-subsisting, which is the eternal attribute. So it is a customary truth in each. So the sensible is that which consists of letters and sound, and its indication is some of the indication of the eternal self-subsisting speech that subsists in His essence, Exalted is He. And the self-subsisting is that which is not letters nor sound, and is not described with precedence or delay, nor division nor beginning nor end, and it is eternal, not created. So the heavenly books indicate some of the indication of the self-subsisting speech, and no one encompasses all its indication except Him, because the indication of the self-subsisting speech is the necessary, the impossible, and the possible in detail. As for the heavenly books, they have indicated some of the necessary things in detail, and all the necessary things in summary, and likewise the impossible and the possible. And Allah's speaking to Musa on the mountain was by the self-subsisting speech according to verification for the Ash'aris and some of the Maturidis, contrary to the Mu'tazila and some other Maturidis. So the division of speech into command, prohibition, report, inquiry, promise, and threat is only for those indicated meanings which the sensible speech indicates. As for the eternal attribute, its division is impossible as you have learned. Al-Tabarani narrated from Sa'id ibn Jubayr from Ibn 'Abbas who said: 'Allah revealed to Musa: I have placed in you ten thousand ears so that you hear My speech, and ten thousand tongues so that you answer Me.' And al-Quda'i narrated that Allah conversed with Musa with one hundred thousand and forty thousand words, so his face shone with light when he came from his Lord, so that people would know the truth of what he claimed. So no one saw him except that he became blind – the one who looked at his face would cover it with a garment, and he would wipe over it and Allah would return his sight to him. So he veiled himself, lest people's sight be taken away when seeing him, and the veil remained on his face until he died. And he used to block his ears after returning from the intimate conversation for a while, lest he hear people's speech and die from the ugliness of his grief. And he became…
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of the two meanings of 'Allah's speech' and a discussion of the nature of the divine address to Prophet Musa. The author first clarifies that the term 'Allah's speech' is used homonymously for two realities: the sensible speech (kalam hissi) that consists of letters and sound, and the self-subsisting eternal speech (kalam nafsi) which is the ancient attribute. Both are customary truths. The sensible speech indicates only part of what the self-subsisting speech indicates, because the self-subsisting speech encompasses all knowable things – the necessary, the impossible, and the possible in full detail. The heavenly books (Torah, Gospel, etc.) indicate some of these in detail and all of them in summary. Then the author addresses the event of Allah speaking to Musa on Mount Sinai: according to the verification of the Ash'aris and some Maturidis, this was by the self-subsisting speech, not by a created sound, contrary to the Mu'tazila and some Maturidis. He then explains that the division of speech into command, prohibition, etc. applies to the indicated meanings (madlulat) that are conveyed by sensible speech, not to the eternal attribute itself, which cannot be divided. Finally, he cites two hadith narrations. The first from Al-Tabarani: Allah gave Musa ten thousand ears to hear His speech and ten thousand tongues to respond. The second from Al-Quda'i: The number of words in the conversation was 140,000, and as a result Musa's face shone with light, causing blindness to those who looked at him directly. To protect people, he wore a veil. He also blocked his ears after returning from the encounter to avoid the jarring contrast of human speech. This supports the extraordinary nature of the divine communication.

Key Terms
كلام حسي
كلام نفسي
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the self-subsisting speech indicate?
A) Only the necessary things.
B) The necessary, impossible, and possible in detail.
C) Only the commands and prohibitions.
D) The words of the heavenly books.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is both a created sensible form and an eternal attribute; the latter encompasses all knowledge and cannot be divided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever pause to consider that the letters and sounds you hear are not the eternal speech itself but a created indication of it? How does that change your reverence for the words you recite?
?
The passage says that no one encompasses all the indication of the self-subsisting speech except Allah. Have you ever felt frustrated that you cannot fully comprehend Allah's knowledge? How does this limitation humble you in your quest for understanding?
?
Allah's speech is both a created form (the Quran) and an eternal attribute. Which aspect do you find more awe-inspiring, and why? Does this duality affect how you approach the Quran in your daily life?

The Encounter of Musa and the Extraordinary Signs
Al-Sawi narrates hadith about Musa's encounter with divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of Allah's speech to Musa and the miraculous signs that accompanied it, based on hadith narrations.

Core Idea
Musa heard Allah's eternal speech through extraordinary means.

The previous lesson established that Allah's speech to Musa was by the self-subsisting attribute. Now Al-Sawi narrates two hadith that describe the miraculous circumstances: Allah gave Musa ten thousand ears and tongues, and his face shone so brightly that it caused blindness. These stories illustrate the awe-inspiring nature of divine communication and confirm Musa's prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith narrations
Read the Arabic and translation of the two hadith, noting the specific gifts given to Musa and the effects on his appearance.
The first hadith mentions ten thousand ears and tongues; the second mentions the number of words and the shining face.
2
Understand the purpose of these signs
Reflect on why these extraordinary signs were given: to confirm the truth of Musa's prophecy and to demonstrate the majesty of the divine encounter.
The passage says 'so that people would know the truth of what he claimed.'
3
Connect to the doctrine of divine speech
Relate this to the Ash'ari position that Allah's speech is eternal and not a created sound. The hadith support the idea that Musa heard the eternal attribute directly.

The Eternal Attribute of Speech and Its Temporal Expressions
Sanusi distinguishes divine eternal speech from temporal expressions and states the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari distinction between God's eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi) and its temporal sensory expressions (kalam hissi), and why the eternal attribute cannot be divided into commands, prohibitions, etc.

Core Idea
God's eternal speech is one indivisible attribute; its divisions belong only to temporal expressions.

This passage from Sanusi lays the foundation for understanding a pivotal debate in Islamic theology: how can God speak without being composed of sounds and letters? The Ash'ari school answers by distinguishing between the eternal attribute of speech (kalam nafsi) and its temporal manifestations in revelation, such as the Qur'an and the address to Moses. As you read, notice how Sanusi clarifies that the familiar categories of command, prohibition, etc., apply only to the sensory expressions, not to the eternal attribute itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style; each sentence carries a key point.
2
Identify the two types of speech
Look for 'kalam nafsi' (internal/eternal) and 'kalam hissi' (sensory/temporal). Understand what each refers to.
The author uses 'al-kalam al-nafsi' and 'al-kalam al-hissi'.
3
Understand the controversy
Which schools hold that God's speech to Moses was the eternal attribute? Which oppose? Note the positions of Ash'aris, Maturidis, and Mu'tazila.
4
Grasp the implication of indivisibility
Why can't the eternal attribute be divided into command, prohibition, etc.? The author says it's impossible (yastaḥīlu inqisāmuhā).
This safeguards God's transcendence (tanzih).
5
Reflect on the big picture
How does this distinction resolve the apparent contradiction between God's eternal speech and the temporal, varied revealed texts?
This is a foundational move in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْكُتُبُ السَّمَاوِيَّةُ: فَقَدْ دَلَّتْ عَلَى بَعْضِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ تَفْصِيلاً، وَكُلُّ الْوَاجِبَاتِ إِجْمَالاً وَكَذَا الْمُسْتَحِيلاَت، وَالْجَائِزَاتُ. وَتَكْلِيمُ اللَّهِ لِمُوسَى عَلَى الْجَبَلِ كَانَ بِالْكَلَامِ النَّفْسِيِّ عَلَى التَّحْقِيقِ عِنْدَ الْأَشَاعِرَةِ وَبَعْضِ الْمَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ، خِلَافاً لِلْمُعْتَزِلَةِ وَالْبَعْضِ الْآخَرِ مِنَ الْمَاتُرِيدِيَّةِ. فَتَقْسِيمُ الْكَلَامِ إِلَى أَمْرٍ وَنَهْيٍ وَخَبَرٍ وَاسْتِخْبَارٍ وَوَعْدٍ وَوَعِيدٍ إِنَّمَا هُوَ لِتِلْكَ الْمَدْلُولَاتِ الَّتِي دَلَّ عَلَيْهَا الْكَلَامُ الْحِسِّيُّ. وَأَمَّا الصِّفَةُ الْقَدِيمَةُ: فَيَسْتَحِيلُ انْقِسَامُهَا كَمَا عَلِمْتَ.
As for the heavenly books, they have indicated some obligations in detail, and all obligations in summary, and likewise the impossibilities and the contingencies. And Allah’s speaking to Moses on the mountain was with the internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) according to the verification of the Ash‘aris and some Maturidis, contrary to the Mu‘tazila and the other some of the Maturidis. So the division of speech into command, prohibition, report, interrogation, promise and threat is only for those significations which the sensory speech (al-kalam al-hissi) indicated. As for the eternal attribute (al-sifa al-qadima), its division is impossible, as you have learned.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two aspects of divine speech: the eternal attribute (al-kalam al-nafsi) and its temporal, sensory expressions (al-kalam al-hissi). The author states that the heavenly books detail some obligations and summarize all, including impossibilities and contingencies. He then addresses a specific case: God's speech to Moses on Mount Sinai. According to the Ash‘aris and some Maturidis, that speech was the eternal, internal attribute, not a created sound. This opposes the Mu‘tazila and some other Maturidis, who held that God's speech to Moses was created. The author then explains that the well-known divisions of speech—command, prohibition, report, etc.—apply only to the sensory speech, i.e., the expressions that signify meanings. The eternal attribute itself, being one and indivisible, cannot be divided into these categories. This guards against the misconception that God's eternal speech is multiple or composed of parts.

Key Terms
كلام نفسي
كلام حسي
صفة قديمة
أشاعرة
ماتريدية
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternal speech is divisible into commands, prohibitions, reports, etc., or that His speech to Moses was a created sound.
Correction: The eternal attribute of speech is one and indivisible; divisions apply only to its temporal sensory expressions.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash‘aris, what kind of speech was God's address to Moses on the mountain?
A) Sensory speech (al-kalam al-hissi)
B) Internal, eternal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi)
C) A created voice and letters
D) Both sensory and internal speech

Key Takeaway
God's eternal speech is a single indivisible attribute; its temporal expressions may be categorized but the attribute itself is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you treat it as a collection of commands and prohibitions, or do you sense that you are engaging with a single, eternal attribute of Allah? How would your reverence change if you internalized this lesson's distinction?
?
The Ash'aris hold that God's speech to Musa was the very same eternal attribute, not a created sound. Do you believe that Allah can speak to you today through His eternal speech? What would it take for you to listen as Musa listened?
?
If the division of speech into types applies only to temporal expressions, then every command and promise in the Quran ultimately points to one indivisible reality. Are you living as if God's commands are fragments to be selectively obeyed, or as a coherent call from His eternal attribute?

The Divine Attributes of Hearing and Sight
Defines hearing and sight as divine attributes, clarifies terminology and scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of hearing and sight as divine attributes, their eternal and all-encompassing scope, and the significance of the author's terminology.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight encompass all existents, not just physical sounds and sights.

In this passage, the commentator defines two more divine attributes: hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). He explains why the author used a particular wording and clarifies that these attributes are eternal and apply to all existing things, not just what we normally consider audible or visible. This understanding is crucial for avoiding a mistaken limitation of God's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the commentator's style and the structure of the argument.
Pay attention to the commentary on 'al-ibṣār' and the footnote references.
2
Identify the two attributes and their description
Find where hearing and sight are defined as eternal attributes that disclose all existents. Note the phrase 'جميع الموجودات' (all existents).
This is the key doctrinal point.
3
Understand the terminology and the contrast
Read the explanation about the metaphorical use of 'effect' for 'cause' and the comment about the poem's meter. Then see how the view of al-Sanusi and al-Ash'ari differs from al-Sa'd.
This clarifies the scope of the attributes.

Commentary of al-Sawi on the explanation of al-Kharidat: Hearing and sight. (And) the sixth (attribute) is hearing, and the seventh is sight, meaning vision. He used the name of the effect and intended the cause metaphorically. That indicates his intent that the discussion is about the (essential) attributes, and likewise what comes about relation. If he had said 'then sight' it would be clearer. Hearing and sight are two eternal attributes by which all existents are disclosed. So if you know that, do not wonder at the statement of the scholars: 'Indeed Moses heard the speech with all his parts from all his directions.' With all. His saying: 'al-ibsār' with a kasra on the hamza, a verbal noun from abṣara. His saying: 'He used the name of the effect' is an empty unit on his saying: 'meaning'. His saying: 'indicates his intent' i.e., the one which is sight. And his saying: 'the discussion is about the attributes' meaning the essential attributes subsisting in the existence-Entity. His saying: 'if he had said then sight it would be clearer' i.e., with changing the structure of the poem, otherwise the meter would be lost. His saying: 'all existents' i.e., according to al-Sanusi and al-Ash'ari. Thus one does not restrict sight to visible things and hearing to audible things, contrary to al-Sa'd. (1) Umm al-Barahin (p.56). (2) See Sharh al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya (p.85).
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attributes of hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). It first notes that the author called the attribute 'al-ibṣār' (sight) instead of 'al-baṣar' as a metaphorical use of the name of the effect (the act of seeing) to refer to the cause (the attribute). This indicates that the discussion is about the essential attributes (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) that subsist in God's essence. The commentator suggests that if the author had said 'thumma al-baṣar' (then sight) it would have been clearer. Then hearing and sight are described as two eternal attributes through which all existents are disclosed. This is supported by the example of Prophet Moses hearing God's speech with his entire being, signifying that God's hearing and sight are not limited to particular objects. The passage further explains that 'al-ibṣār' is a verbal noun from 'abṣara', and justifies the author's phrasing by noting poetic meter constraints. Finally, it clarifies that according to al-Sanusi and al-Ash'ari, these attributes encompass all existents, not just visible or audible things, in contrast to al-Saʿd al-Taftazani who restricts them. The footnotes cite authoritative sources for these views.

The Divine Attributes of Hearing and Sight: Eternal and All-Encompassing
Al-Sawi defines hearing and sight as eternal attributes revealing all existents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of hearing and sight are eternal, disclose all existents, and are distinct from the attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing and sight disclose every existent, not just sounds and sights.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary explores the divine attributes of hearing and sight. It clarifies that these are eternal (azali) attributes by which all existents, not limited to audible or visible things, are disclosed. Al-Sawi also discusses the metaphorical usage of 'ibṣār' for 'baṣar' and the important distinction that this disclosure is different from that of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms like 'ibṣār' and 'baṣar'.
2
Identify the key claims
The passage makes several key claims: hearing and sight are eternal, they disclose all existents (not just sounds/images), and this disclosure differs from knowledge. Note the example of Moses hearing Allah's speech in full.
The Moses example illustrates the all-encompassing nature.
3
Reflect on the metaphor and distinction
Al-Sawi explains that using 'ibṣār' (effect) for 'baṣar' (cause) is metaphorical. Also, understand that the disclosure by hearing/sight is different from that by knowledge—a point of theological depth.
This distinction is important for understanding divine attributes.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) سادسها (السَّمعُ، و) سابعها (الإبصار)، يعني: البصر، فقد أطلق اسم المسبب وأراد السَّبب مجازاً، يدلُّ على مراده أنَّ الكلام في المعاني، وكذا ما يأتي في التعلّق، ولو قال : ثمَّ البصر .. لكان أوضح. والسمعُ والبصرُ: صفتان أزليَّتان ينكشف بهما جميع الموجودات. فإذا علمت ذلك فلا يستغرب قول العلماء: إن موسى سمع الكلام بجميع أجزائه من جميع جهاته.

And its sixth is hearing, and its seventh is sight, meaning: vision, for he used the name of the effect and intended the cause metaphorically. It indicates his intent that the discussion is about the attributes of meanings, and likewise what comes regarding relation. And if he had said 'then sight' it would be clearer. And hearing and sight are two eternal attributes by which all existents are disclosed. So if you know that, then do not find strange the scholars' statement: 'Indeed Moses heard the speech with all its parts from all its directions.' His statement: 'it indicates his intent' means: which is vision. And his statement: 'the discussion is about the attributes of meanings' means: about the attributes of meanings subsisting in the existential essence. His statement: 'and if he had said: then sight, it would be clearer' means: with a change in the meter of the line, otherwise the meter would be lost. The attributes of meanings, a complete disclosure. And the disclosure by them differs from disclosure by knowledge, just as disclosure by one of them differs from disclosure by the other.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine attributes of Hearing (as-sam‘) and Sight (al-ibṣār). It begins by clarifying that the author used the term 'ibṣār' (effect) to mean 'baṣar' (cause) metaphorically. The attributes are described as eternal (azliyyān) by which all existents are disclosed, not limited to audible or visible things. The example of Moses hearing Allah's speech in full is given to illustrate the all-encompassing nature of divine hearing. The commentary then glosses the word 'ibṣār' as a verbal noun, and explains that the discussion pertains to attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-ma‘ānī) subsisting in the essence. Finally, it asserts that the disclosure through hearing and sight is different in reality from disclosure through knowledge, and similarly each of these two attributes differs from the other in the manner of disclosure, even though we cannot fully comprehend that difference.

The Real Distinction Between the Attributes of Meanings and Refuting the Objection
Al-Sawi refutes the objection of tahsil al-hasil regarding the attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the real distinction between the attributes of meanings refutes the objection that knowledge, hearing, and sight would entail tahsil al-hasil or ignorance.

Core Idea
Attributes differ in reality even if we cannot perceive the difference.

Al-Sawi addresses a subtle objection: if hearing, sight, and knowledge all relate to every existent, what prevents them from being identical? He explains that the difference is real but hidden, thereby preserving both divine simplicity and the multiplicity of attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage concerning the objection that attributes would lead to tahsil al-hasil or ignorance. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the two impossibilities listed.
2
Understand the objection
The objection argues that if hearing, sight, and knowledge all relate to the same objects, they must be identical. If they were identical, one attribute already possessing that object would mean the others add nothing (tahsil al-hasil). If they are different, then some objects known by hearing/sight would be unknown to knowledge—impossible for God.
3
Grasp the refutation
Al-Sawi says the author uses 'differs' (yughāyir) to indicate a real difference in essence (haqīqa) and actual fact (nafs al-amr), even though we (humans) cannot perceive that difference. This refutes the objection because the attributes are not identical in reality.
The difference is affirmed despite our inability to discern it.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This shows that attributes can be distinct without implying composition or deficiency, as the distinction is real but not perceived by us. It upholds the doctrine of attributes of meanings.
This is a key point in defending Ash'ari theology against objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ فرَّع على صفات المعاني في الجملة، إذ التفريع إنَّما يظهر قوله : (يغاير) أي : في الحقيقة ونفس الأمر وإن كنا لا نطلع على ذلك، وبهذا اندفع ما أورد أنَّ العلم والسَّمع والبصر متعلقات بكلِّ موجود، فيلزم إما تحصيل الحاصل إن كان ما تعلَّق به أحدها تعلَّق به الباقي، أو خفاء بعض المعلومات عن العلم إن كان ما تعلَّق به السَّمع والبصر لم يتعلق به العلم، وكلا الأمرين محال. ودليل هذه الصفات الثَّلاثة نقلي من الكتاب والسُّنَّة والإجماع والتّواتر، قال تعالى: وَكَلَّمَ اللهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا (١) ، وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ البصير (٢) وأجمع أهل الأديان والعقلاء على أنَّه تعالى سميع بصير متكلّم، والمشتقُ يدلُّ على المشتق منه، خلافاً للمعتزلة النافين للمعاني، حيث قالوا : بذاته . . . وهكذا ، وإنّما كانت أدلّة هذه الثَّلاثة نقليَّة ؛ لأنَّ إيجاد العالم ليس متوقفاً عليها ؛ لأنَّ صفة العلم مغنية، فإن كان الغرض علمه محيط بحقائق الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، على ما هو عليه تفصيلاً في كلِّ جزئيَّة؛ فهو غني عن المؤكّد.
Then he branched off to the attributes of meanings in summary, for the branching is only apparent from his statement: (differs), meaning: in reality and in actual fact, even if we are not informed of that. And by this is repelled what was objected: that knowledge, hearing, and sight relate to every existent, so it would necessitate either the acquisition of what is already obtained if what one of them relates to is related to by the others, or some known things being hidden from knowledge if what hearing and sight relate to is not related to by knowledge, and both matters are impossible. And the proof of these three attributes is transmitted from the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, and recurrent reports. Allah Most High said: "And Allah spoke to Moses directly" (Quran 4:164) and "He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing" (Quran 42:11). And the people of religions and rational people have agreed that He is Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, and the derived term indicates the source from which it is derived, contrary to the Mu'tazila who negate the attributes of meanings, for they say: 'by His essence...' and so forth. And the proofs of these three are only transmitted because the existence of the world does not depend on them; because the attribute of knowledge suffices. For if the purpose is that His knowledge encompasses the realities of the necessary, possible, and impossible things as they are in detail in every particular, then He is independent of the confirmed.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage discusses the three attributes of meanings: hearing, sight, and speech. Al-Sawi explains that the author's branching (tafrī‘) is based on the difference in reality between these attributes and knowledge. He then addresses an objection: if all three attributes relate to every existent, why not say they are identical? The objection claims that if one attribute's object is also related to by the others, that would be 'acquisition of what is already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), and if hearing and sight relate to something beyond knowledge, knowledge would be deficient—both impossible. The answer is that the difference is real though unknown to us. The proof for these attributes is transmitted (naqlī) from Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and recurrent reports, not rational (ʿaqlī). Examples include Allah speaking to Moses and the verse declaring Him Hearing and Seeing. Consensus of religious people and rational beings affirms He is Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking; the derived terms 'Hearing' and 'Seeing' indicate the attribute. This refutes the Muʿtazila who deny such attributes, claiming God knows by His essence. The reason the proof is transmitted is that the world's creation does not require these attributes—knowledge alone suffices to encompass all things.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
تحصيل الحاصل
حقيقة
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if multiple attributes relate to the same objects, they must be identical, leading to a contradiction in divine attributes.
Correction: The passage affirms that the attributes are really distinct even if we cannot perceive the difference, so there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
What are the two impossible consequences that would follow if knowledge, hearing, and sight were not really distinct?
A) Tahsil al-hasil and deficiency of hearing
B) Tahsil al-hasil and hiddenness of some known things from knowledge
C) Hiddenness of hearing and sight from knowledge
D) Redundancy of knowledge and lack of hearing

Key Takeaway
The real distinction between the attributes of meanings, though imperceptible to us, refutes the objection that they would entail tahsil al-hasil or divine ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that Allah's attributes are truly distinct in reality, even though your mind cannot fully grasp how. Is there an area of your life where you demand complete understanding before you submit? Where is Allah asking you to trust His perfection even when your intellect struggles?
?
Al-Sawi refutes the objection by affirming a real distinction that we cannot perceive. When you face a doubt about Allah's attributes, do you humbly accept the teaching of the scholars, or do you insist on a rational explanation that satisfies you? Which response reflects true faith?
?
The objection, if accepted, would lead to attributing ignorance to Allah—a grave error. How seriously do you protect your belief in Allah's perfect attributes? Are there subtle doubts you harbor that you need to uproot? What will you do today to strengthen your conviction in what Allah has revealed about Himself?

The Transmitted Proofs for Hearing, Sight, and Speech and Why They Are Naqli
Al-Sawi provides transmitted proof for hearing, sight, and speech and explains their naqli basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the proofs for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are transmitted (naqli) from revelation, consensus, and language, and why rational proof is not required for these attributes.

Core Idea
These attributes are proven by revelation and consensus, not by reason.

Al-Sawi now presents the positive evidence: the three attributes are established by the Quran, Sunnah, consensus of religious communities, and even the linguistic principle that derived terms indicate their sources. He also explains that rational proof is unnecessary because creation does not depend on these attributes — knowledge alone suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on transmitted proofs
Read the Arabic passage that lists the evidences for hearing, sight, and speech, and the reason why they are naqli. Then read the translation.
Notice the four sources: Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and the language of derived terms.
2
Identify the transmitted evidences
The passage cites two Quranic verses: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' and 'He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing.' It also invokes consensus of religious people and rational beings, and the fact that derived terms (like Samī‘ and Baṣīr) indicate the attribute from which they are derived.
The consensus includes even non-Muslims according to the text.
3
Understand the refutation of Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila deny attributes of meanings, saying God knows by His essence. The passage counters by pointing out that the derived terms 'Hearing' and 'Seeing' imply attributes, otherwise they would be meaningless.
The Mu'tazila claim is 'by His essence' (bi-dhātihi).
4
Grasp why the proofs are naqli, not 'aqli
The passage states that the world's existence does not depend on these attributes; knowledge alone is sufficient for God to create and manage the universe. Therefore, rational proof is not necessary, and the attributes are affirmed solely through revelation and consensus.
This distinguishes these three attributes from attributes like knowledge, power, and will, which can be proven rationally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that all divine attributes must be proven rationally, but the passage shows that hearing, sight, and speech are proven only by revelation and consensus.
Correction: The passage clarifies that these attributes are not rationally necessary for creating the world; knowledge suffices, so they are affirmed solely through transmitted sources.

Checkpoint
Why are the proofs for hearing, sight, and speech transmitted rather than rational?
A) Because rational proofs are impossible for these attributes.
B) Because the existence of the world does not depend on these attributes; knowledge suffices.
C) Because the Quran only mentions them in transmitted form.
D) Because the Mu'tazila denied rational proofs.

Key Takeaway
The attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are affirmed solely through transmitted proofs, and rational proof is unnecessary because creation does not require these attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Proof of Hearing, Sight, and Speech is Transmitted
Refutes objection about overlapping attributes and asserts transmitted proof for hearing, sight, speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the proof for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech is transmitted rather than rational, and how the objection about overlapping attributes is refuted.

Core Idea
Transmitted proof establishes attributes that rational proof cannot.

This passage tackles a key objection about the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech—if they all relate to everything, why aren't they redundant? The author refutes this by explaining their distinct realities. He then clarifies that these attributes are proven through revelation, not reason, because they are not necessary for creation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection and refutation
Read the part where the author addresses the objection that if knowledge, hearing, and sight all relate to every existent, then either redundancy or ignorance follows. Notice how the refutation relies on the distinct nature of each attribute.
The author says these attributes differ 'in reality and actual fact' even if we do not perceive it.
2
Identify the transmitted proofs cited
Look for the phrase 'دليل هذه الصفات الثلاثة نقلي' and note the sources: Quran (4:164, 42:11), consensus of people of religion and reason.
Also note the mention of 'المشتق' and the Mu'tazila denial.
3
Grasp the reason why transmitted proof is needed
Understand the author's argument that the world does not depend on these attributes; knowledge suffices for creation. Hence, rational proof cannot necessitate them, only transmitted proof establishes them.
This highlights the distinction between attributes necessary for creation (like power) and those not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والانكشاف بهما يغاير الانكشاف بالعلم، كما أنَّ الانكشاف بإحداهما يغاير الانكشاف بالأخرى. ثمَّ فرَّع على صفات المعاني في الجملة، إذ التفريع إنَّما يظهر قوله : (يغاير) أي : في الحقيقة ونفس الأمر وإن كنا لا نطلع على ذلك، وبهذا اندفع ما أورد أنَّ العلم والسَّمع والبصر متعلقات بكلِّ موجود، فيلزم إما تحصيل الحاصل إن كان ما تعلَّق به أحدها تعلَّق به الباقي، أو خفاء بعض المعلومات عن العلم إن كان ما تعلَّق به السَّمع والبصر لم يتعلق به العلم، وكلا الأمرين محال. ودليل هذه الصفات الثَّلاثة نقلي من الكتاب والسُّنَّة والإجماع والتّواتر، قال تعالى: وَكَلَّمَ اللهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا، وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ البَصِيرُ. وأجمع أهل الأديان والعقلاء على أنَّه تعالى سميع بصير متكلّم، والمشتقُ يدلُّ على المشتق منه، خلافاً للمعتزلة النافين للمعاني، حيث قالوا بذاته... وهكذا، وإنّما كانت أدلّة هذه الثَّلاثة نقليَّة؛ لأنَّ إيجاد العالم ليس متوقفاً عليها؛ لأنَّ صفة العلم مغنية، فإن كان الغرض علمه محيط بحقائق الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، على ما تفصيلاً في كلِّ جزئيَّة؛ فهو غني عن المؤكّد.
And the disclosure by them differs from disclosure by knowledge, just as disclosure by one of them differs from disclosure by another. Then he branched onto the attributes of meanings in general, for the branching only appears in his saying '(yughāyiru)' i.e., in reality and actual fact even if we do not perceive that. And with this is repelled what was objected: that knowledge, hearing, and sight relate to every existent, so either it would necessitate obtaining the obtained if what one of them relates to is also related to by the others, or the concealment of some known things from knowledge if what hearing and sight relate to is not related to by knowledge, and both matters are impossible. And the proof of these three attributes is transmitted from the Book, the Sunnah, consensus, and mutawātir. He said, may He be exalted: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' (4:164), and 'He is the All-Hearing, the All-Seeing' (42:11). And the people of religions and the rational people have agreed that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking. And the derived term indicates the source term, contrary to the Mu'tazila who deny the attributes, for they said 'by His essence...' and so on. And the proofs of these three are only transmitted because the existence of the world does not depend on them; because the attribute of knowledge suffices. For if the purpose is that His knowledge encompasses the realities of the necessary, possible, and impossible, in detail for every particular, then He is free from needing reinforcement.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof structure followed by objection and refutation. The author begins by explaining that the mode of disclosure through hearing and sight differs from disclosure through knowledge, and each differs from the other. He then addresses an objection: if knowledge, hearing, and sight all relate to every existent, it would either be redundant or cause some unknowns. The answer is that they are distinct in reality though we cannot perceive the difference. He then asserts that the proof for the three attributes of hearing, sight, and speech is entirely transmitted (naqlī) from the Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and mutawātir reports. He cites verses and notes the consensus of religious communities and rational people. He contrasts this with the Mu'tazila who deny these attributes as distinct entities. The reason for relying on transmitted proof is that the creation of the world does not depend on these attributes; God's knowledge alone is sufficient to encompass all realities. Thus, these attributes are not needed for creation but are established by revelation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing, sight, and speech can be proven rationally from creation.
Correction: The author argues that the world does not depend on these attributes; knowledge alone suffices. Hence, only transmitted proof (revelation) can establish them.

Checkpoint

A) Rational proof from necessity
B) Transmitted proof from Quran, Sunnah, consensus, and mutawatir
C) Intuitive proof from self-evidence
D) Proof from creation

Key Takeaway
The attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are established solely through transmitted evidence, not rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You have likely never seen or heard God, yet this passage insists these attributes are proven by revelation. Is there an attribute of Allah you find hard to accept because it is beyond your experience? Will you submit to the transmitted proof even if your reason struggles?
?
If the Mu'tazila deny distinct attributes, and you know the evidence for them is from Quran and consensus, have you ever let intellectual doubt creep in about a clear Quranic description of Allah? What will you do the next time such a doubt arises?

Refutation of a Rational Proof for the Attributes
Objection-and-answer refuting a rational proof for attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogical reasoning from contingent beings to God is invalid in kalam, and why the rational proof for the attributes is rejected.

Core Idea
What is perfection for creatures may be impossible for God.

This lesson examines a proposed rational proof for the divine attributes and its refutation. The objector argues that without these attributes, God would have deficiencies. The answer shows that deficiency is observed in contingent things, but we cannot measure the Eternal by the contingent. This highlights a key principle: the perfection of creatures does not automatically apply to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer.
The objection uses a conditional: 'If He were not described... He would be described with their opposite.'
2
Identify the flawed analogy
The answer points out that deficiency is observed in contingent things, but we cannot compare the Eternal to the contingent. What is deficiency in creatures does not necessarily imply it is so for God.
The example of wife and child shows that something considered perfection for creatures is impossible for God.
3
Understand the principle
The key principle is: the perfection of the contingent does not entail perfection with respect to God. This is why the rational proof is weak.
This principle prevents arbitrary analogy from creation to the Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : إنَّه يمكن أن يكون دليلها عقليّاً، وتقديره أن تقول : لو لم يتّصف بها ؛ لاتّصف بضدّها ، وهو نقص، والنقص عليه محال. أجيب بأن النقص مشاهد في الحوادث، ولا يقاس القديم على الحادث؛ لأنَّ كمال الحادث لا يلزم أن يكون كمالاً في حق الله، ألا ترى الزَّوجة والولد فإنَّهما كمال في حقّ الحادث، مستحيل في حقّ الله، فضعف الدَّليل العقلي.
If you say: It is possible that their proof is rational, and its estimation is that you say: If He were not described with them, He would be described with their opposite, which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Him. The answer is that deficiency is observed in contingent things, and the Eternal is not measured by the contingent; because the perfection of the contingent does not necessarily entail that it is perfection with respect to God. Do you not see that wife and child are perfection for the contingent, but impossible for God? Thus the rational proof is weak.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector proposes a rational proof: if God lacked these attributes, He would have their opposites (e.g., deafness, blindness, muteness) which are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God—therefore He must have them. The author answers that this analogy from creatures to God fails because what is considered perfection for created beings (like having a spouse and children) is actually impossible for God. Similarly, the attributes in question are not necessarily perfections for God in the same way they are for creatures. Hence, the rational argument is weak and cannot be relied upon. This highlights the principle in kalam that one cannot arbitrarily analogize from the contingent to the Eternal.

Key Terms
دليل عقلي
ضد
نقص
قديم
حادث
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the rational proof is strong because avoiding opposites seems necessary.
Correction: The author shows that the classification of something as a perfection or deficiency depends on the being in question; what is perfection for creatures may be impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the rational proof that God must have these attributes to avoid deficiency?
A) Because deficiency is not impossible for God
B) Because what is perfection for creatures may not be perfection for God
C) Because the attributes are not necessary
D) Because the proof is from the Quran only

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning from creatures to God is invalid because what is perfect for the contingent may be impossible for the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a human virtue you deeply admire—generosity, patience, or justice. Have you ever unconsciously assumed that God possesses that virtue in exactly the same way you do? This lesson warns that what is perfect for a creature may be impossible for God. Will you commit to actively checking your conception of God against the revelation, rather than your own experience?
?
This passage uses the example of a spouse and children—blessings that are perfect for us but impossible for God. What other 'perfections' in your life (like leadership, creativity, or love) do you project onto God? How can you train yourself to remember that God's perfections are incomparable?
?
You may have felt confident in a theological argument only to realize it was based on a false analogy, as the rational proof here was weak. When you reason about God, are you willing to humble your intellect and accept that your categories of perfection may not apply? What would it cost you to let go of that intellectual pride?

Resolving the Circularity Objection in Proving God's Speech
Resolves circularity objection regarding proving God's speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the apparent circularity in using transmitted evidence to prove God's speech is resolved by distinguishing the modality of miracles and the nature of divine speech.

Core Idea
Miracles indicate truthfulness without requiring God to utter words.

One common objection to proving God's speech through revelation is that it seems circular: we need a miracle to verify the prophet, but the miracle itself presupposes that God speaks. This passage from Sanusi's commentator shows how this objection is answered by carefully separating what a miracle signifies from what it does not.

Guided Path
1
Understand the circularity objection
Read the objector's claim carefully: proving God's speech via transmitted evidence requires a truthful messenger, but miracles (which prove his truthfulness) depend on God being speaking. Identify each link in the chain.
Notice that the objector assumes the miracle is like God saying 'My servant is truthful.'
2
Grasp the answer: separate modality
The commentator replies that the modality (jiha) is separate: the miracle indicates truthfulness without implying that God actually spoke those words. It functions as a sign, not a speech act.
The term 'جهة منفكة' means the aspect is disconnected – miracle and speech are not necessarily linked.
3
Analyze the gesture analogy
The author compares the miracle to a gesture: a gesture conveys meaning without the gesturer being a speaker. Similarly, the miracle conveys divine attestation without God uttering words.
This clarifies that the miracle does not require prior verbal speech from God.
4
Distinguish internal from verbal speech
The speech being proven is internal speech (kalam nafsi), not verbal speech (kalam lafzi). The miracle does not depend on verbal speech, so there is no circularity.
Internal speech is the eternal attribute; verbal speech is temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : في الاستدلال بالنقل على صفة الكلام دور، وذلك لأنَّها لا تثبت إلَّا إذا ثبت صدق الرسول، ولا يثبت صدقه إلَّا بالمعجزة، وهي لا تثبت إلَّا إذا ثبت كون الباري متكلّماً؛ لأنَّ المعجزة تنزل منزلة قول الله : (صدق عبدي في كلِّ ما يبلغ عنِّي، وكونه متكلّماً يتوقف على إثبات الكلام له بالدليل الشرعي. أجيب بأنَّ الجهة منفكّة، وذلك لأنَّ معنى تنزيل المعجزة منزلة قول الله ... إلخ ، أنَّها تدلُّ على ما يدلُّ عليه القول من صدق الآتي بها، وليس معناه أنَّ فاعلها تكلَّم بتصديق من ظهرت على يديه، وهذا كما تقول : الإشارة تدلُّ وضعاً على ما يدلُّ عليه الكلام، وهل المشير المتكلّم ، أو أخرس؟ محتمل وليس في الإشارة ما يدلُّ على شيء. والكلام المستدل عليه هو النَّفسي لا اللفظي.
If you say: In using transmitted evidence for the attribute of speech there is circularity, because it is not established except if the messenger's truthfulness is established, and his truthfulness is not established except by a miracle, and a miracle is not established except if it is established that the Creator is speaking; because the miracle takes the place of God's saying: 'My servant is truthful in all that he conveys from Me', and His being speaking depends on proving speech for Him by legal evidence. The answer is that the modality is separate. Because the meaning of the miracle taking the place of God's saying ... etc., is that it indicates what the saying indicates of the truthfulness of the one who brings it, and it does not mean that its agent spoke to affirm the one on whose hands it appeared. And this is like you say: A gesture conventionally indicates what speech indicates, and whether the gesturer is speaking or mute is possible, and there is nothing in the gesture that indicates anything. And the speech that is sought to be proven is the internal speech, not the verbal.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit addressing a circularity argument. The objector claims that proving God's speech via transmitted evidence is circular: to prove God speaks, we need a truthful messenger; to prove the messenger truthful, we need a miracle; but a miracle itself presupposes God speaks because it is like God saying 'My servant is truthful.' Thus we need speech to prove speech. The author answers by distinguishing the modality (جهة منفكّة). The miracle does not require God's actual speech; it functions as a sign indicating truthfulness, just as a gesture can indicate meaning without the gesturer being a speaker. Moreover, the speech being proven is the internal speech (كلام نفسي), not verbal speech, so the miracle does not depend on a prior verbal speech act. This resolves the circularity.

Key Terms
دور
صدق الرسول
معجزة
تنزيل المعجزة منزلة القول
جهة منفكة
كلام نفسي
كلام لفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a miracle requires God to have spoken a specific command or verbal attestation.
Correction: The passage clarifies that a miracle only indicates divine attestation of the prophet's truthfulness, not that God uttered words. It is a sign, not a speech act.

Checkpoint
How does the author resolve the circularity in using transmitted evidence to prove God's speech?
A) By arguing that the miracle is God's direct speech
B) By distinguishing the modality of the miracle from actual speech and focusing on internal speech
C) By denying that miracles are needed
D) By relying on rational proof instead

Key Takeaway
The apparent circularity is resolved by recognizing that the miracle only proves the prophet's truthfulness, not that God has uttered a specific statement, and the speech proven is internal, not verbal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever demanded that God speak directly to you in order to believe, rather than accepting the miracles and signs He has given? How does this lesson challenge you to trust those signs more fully?
?
Think of a situation where you sought explicit verbal reassurance from someone instead of trusting their actions. How can the author's analogy of a gesture help you recognize the sufficiency of God's signs in your own life?
?
The objector saw circularity and demanded a clearer proof. Is there an area of your faith where you are stuck looking for a 'verbal command' from God instead of trusting the evidence He has already provided? What step will you take today to act on the signs He has given?

The First Four Divine Attributes
Clarifies that the 'four first' attributes are knowledge, life, power, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which four attributes are considered the primary divine attributes in the science of kalam.

Core Idea
The first four attributes are knowledge, life, power, and will.

Before delving into the various divine attributes, it is essential to know which ones are considered primary. This brief gloss from the commentary clarifies exactly that: the 'first four' refer to knowledge, life, power, and will. This identification sets the stage for deeper discussions on each attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic gloss and its English translation. Notice that the main text phrase 'upon the first four' is immediately explained.
The Arabic is concise but packs essential information.
2
Identify the four attributes
List the four attributes mentioned: knowledge (العلم), life (الحياة), power (القدرة), and will (الإرادة). Understand that these are traditionally the first attributes discussed in kalam.
This list is standard in Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( على الأربعة الأول) : أي : الَّتي هي العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة .
His saying: 'upon the first four' i.e., which are knowledge, life, power, and will.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is a brief gloss defining a phrase from the main text. The phrase 'على الأربعة الأول' is explained as referring to the four primary attributes of God: knowledge (علم), life (حياة), power (قدرة), and will (إرادة). These are traditionally the first attributes discussed in kalam works before moving to other attributes like hearing, sight, and speech. The gloss ensures the reader understands which attributes are being referenced.

The Weakness of Rational Proof for Divine Attributes
Al-Sawi answers an objection against rational proof for the four attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a rational argument for God's attributes based on deficiency is invalid due to the incommensurability between the Eternal and the contingent.

Core Idea
Comparing God to creation distorts rational proofs for His attributes.

Al-Sawi presents an objection against using rational proof for the four essential attributes. The objector argues that if God lacked them, He would have deficiencies, which is impossible. Al-Sawi's answer reveals a crucial flaw: such reasoning improperly equates God with contingent beings. This lesson dissects that exchange and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the objection and the response.
Notice the structure: 'If you say... The answer...'
2
Identify the objector's reasoning
The objector uses a conditional: if God lacked attributes, He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies. Since deficiency is impossible for God, He must have the attributes. Understand this logical chain.
This is a standard kalam argument by contradiction.
3
See how Al-Sawi counters it
Al-Sawi points out that deficiency is observed in contingent things, but the Eternal cannot be compared to the contingent. The key is the rejection of analogy between God and creation.
The phrase 'لا يقاس القديم على الحادث' is central.
4
Reflect on the example of spouse and offspring
Al-Sawi gives an example: having a spouse and children is perfection for a human but impossible for God. This illustrates that what is perfection for a contingent may be impossible for God.
This example makes the abstract point concrete.
5
Understand the broader principle
The rational proof is weak because it mistakenly applies a standard of perfection from the contingent realm to God. Divine attributes are known through revelation and rational proof that does not rely on such analogy.
This lesson guards against overreliance on weak rational arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : إنَّه يمكن أن يكون دليلها عقلياً، وتقديره أن تقول : لو لم يتّصف بها ؛ لاتّصف بضدّها ، وهو نقص، والنقص عليه محال. أجيب بأن النقص مشاهد في الحوادث، ولا يقاس القديم على الحادث؛ لأنَّ كمال الحادث لا يلزم أن يكون كمالاً في حق الله، ألا ترى الزَّوجة والولد فإنَّهما كمال في حقّ الحادث، مستحيل في حقّ الله، فضعف الدَّليل العقلي .
If you say: It is possible that its proof is rational, and its estimation is that you say: If He were not described with them, He would be described with their opposite, which is deficiency, and deficiency is impossible for Him. The answer: Deficiency is observed in contingent things, and the Eternal cannot be compared to the contingent; because the perfection of the contingent does not necessarily entail that it is perfection with respect to God. Do you not see that spouse and offspring are perfection with respect to the contingent, impossible with respect to God? Thus the rational proof is weak.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the rational proof for the four attributes (knowledge, life, power, will). The objector proposes a rational argument: if God were not described with these attributes, He would be described with their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, He must have these attributes. The answer refutes this by noting that deficiency is observed in contingent things, and the Eternal cannot be measured by the contingent. The perfection of a contingent thing does not imply it is perfection for God. The example of spouse and offspring illustrates this: they are perfections for a human but impossible for God. Hence, the rational proof is weak.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
نقص
كمال
دليل عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that a simple rational argument (like 'if God lacked attributes He would be deficient') is sufficient to prove divine attributes.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows that such an argument is weak because it improperly analogizes God to contingent beings. Divine attributes are not proven by such analogies.

Checkpoint
Why does Al-Sawi reject the rational proof for the four attributes?
A) Because the attributes cannot be proven by reason at all.
B) Because the rational argument draws a false analogy between God and contingent beings.
C) Because the objector's argument is self-contradictory.
D) Because the attributes are not real.

Key Takeaway
Rational arguments for divine attributes must avoid analogies from contingent perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Spouse and offspring are perfections for creatures yet impossible for God. What other 'perfections' in your life might you be tempted to attribute to God in a similar way? Commit today to letting go of one such assumption and instead accepting God's transcendence even when it challenges your comfort.

The Four Primary Attributes: Knowledge, Life, Power, and Will
Al-Sawi defines the phrase 'the four first' as the attributes knowledge, life, power, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which four attributes are referred to as 'the four first' and why they are considered primary divine attributes in this text.

Core Idea
The four first attributes are the foundation of divine theology: knowledge, life, power, and will.

In this brief note, Al-Sawi clarifies a crucial reference in the text: the phrase 'the four first' points to four specific divine attributes. These attributes are often considered the most fundamental in Islamic theology, as they are necessary for God's perfection. Let's examine them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then read the English translation. Notice how Al-Sawi directly explains the phrase.
The Arabic uses 'أي' meaning 'that is' to introduce the explanation.
2

From the translation, list the four attributes: knowledge, life, power, and will. Note that these are the same in Arabic: العلم, الحياة, القدرة, الإرادة.
Memorize the Arabic terms as they recur frequently in kalam.
3
Understand the label 'the four first'
Think about why the author calls them 'first' or 'primary'. These attributes are foundational because they are essential to God's nature and are prerequisites for other attributes.
For example, power and will together explain creation, while knowledge and life are necessary for a conscious agent.
4
Reflect on their importance
Consider how these four attributes form the basis for many theological arguments in kalam, such as proofs for God's existence or His capacity to act and create.
This understanding will help you when these attributes appear in later discussions.

Source Text

قوله : ( على الأربعة الأول) : أي : الَّتي هي العلم والحياة والقدرة والإرادة .
His saying: 'on the four first' means: which are knowledge, life, power, and will.

These are the four primary attributes of God: knowledge (العلم), life (الحياة), power (القدرة), and will (الإرادة). This is a straightforward gloss that helps the reader understand the reference in the preceding discussion.

Objection and Answer: Circularity in Scriptural Proof for Speech
Al-Sawi resolves an alleged circularity in proving God's speech through revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Sawi resolves the apparent circularity in proving God's attribute of speech through scriptural evidence.

Core Idea
The miracle is like a pointing gesture—it proves the messenger's truthfulness without presupposing God's speech.

In this passage, Al-Sawi addresses a common objection: that proving God's speech through revelation is circular. He presents the objection clearly and then gives a subtle answer based on the distinction between different aspects (جهة منفكّة). By the end, you'll see how the miracle functions as independent evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Start by reading the objection that Al-Sawi presents. Notice the logical chain: to prove God's speech, we need revelation; to trust revelation, we need a truthful messenger; to know the messenger is truthful, we need a miracle; but a miracle requires God to be speaking. This seems circular.
The objector assumes the miracle is like God's speech.
2
Understand the answer's key analogy
Al-Sawi answers by comparing the miracle to a pointing gesture. Just as a pointing gesture indicates what speech would indicate without the pointer being a speaker, the miracle indicates the messenger's truthfulness without God having spoken. This breaks the circle.
The Arabic phrase 'الجهة منفكّة' means the aspects are separate.
3
Note the conclusion about internal speech
Al-Sawi clarifies that the speech being proven is internal speech (الكلام النفسي), not uttered speech. This means the proof does not require that God has literally spoken in words; rather, the miracle points to the inner attribute of speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن قلت : في الاستدلال بالنقل على صفة الكلام دور، وذلك لأنَّها لا تثبت إلَّا إذا ثبت صدق الرسول، ولا يثبت صدقه إلَّا بالمعجزة، وهي لا تثبت إلَّا إذا ثبت كون الباري متكلّماً ؛ لأنَّ المعجزة تنزل منزلة قول الله : (صدق عبدي في كلِّ ما يبلغ عنِّي، وكونه متكلّماً يتوقف على إثبات الكلام له بالدليل الشرعي. أجيب بأنَّ الجهة منفكّة، وذلك لأنَّ معنى تنزيل المعجزة مَنْزِلة : قول الله ... إلخ ، أنَّها تدلُّ على ما يدلُّ عليه القول من صدق الآتي بها، وليس معناه أنَّ فاعلها تكلَّم بتصديق من ظهرت على يديه، وهذا كما تقول : الإشارة تدلُّ وضعاً على ما يدلُّ عليه الكلام، وهل المشير المتكلّم ، أو أخرس؟ محتمل وليس في الإشارة ما يدلُّ على شي والكلام المستدل عليه هو النَّفسي لا اللفظي.
If you say: In the scriptural proof for the attribute of speech there is circularity, because it is not established except by establishing the truthfulness of the messenger, and his truthfulness is not established except by the miracle, and it is not established except by establishing that the Creator is speaking; because the miracle is placed in the position of God's saying: 'My servant is truthful in everything he conveys from Me', and His being speaking depends on establishing speech for Him by scriptural evidence. The answer: The aspect is separate, because the meaning of placing the miracle in the position of God's saying... etc., is that it indicates what the statement indicates of the truthfulness of the one who brings it, and it does not mean that its agent spoke to affirm the one on whose hands it appears. This is like when you say: The pointing gesture indicates by convention what speech indicates, and whether the pointer is speaking or mute is possible, and the pointing gesture does not indicate anything. And the speech that is being proven is the internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), not the uttered speech (al-lafẓī).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the proof of God's attribute of speech through revelation. The objector argues that the proof is circular: to prove that God speaks, we need revelation; but to trust revelation, we need to know the messenger is truthful; to know the messenger is truthful, we need a miracle; but to know the miracle is from God and not magic, we need to know that God speaks (since the miracle is like God saying 'My servant is truthful'). Thus, it seems circular. The answer resolves this by explaining that the aspects are separate (الجهة منفكّة). The miracle serves as evidence of the messenger's truthfulness in a way analogous to a pointing gesture: a pointing gesture indicates the same meaning as speech but does not require that the pointer is speaking. Similarly, the miracle indicates the truthfulness of the messenger without presupposing that God has spoken in a literal sense. The speech that is being proven here is the internal, mental speech (الكلام النفسي), not the uttered speech (الكلام اللفظي). Thus, the argument is not circular because the miracle's evidential role is independent.

Key Terms
الكلام النفسي
الكلام اللفظي
معجزة
دور
جهة منفكّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may think that proving God's speech is circular because it requires revelation.
Correction: The passage shows that the miracle does not depend on God's speech being established beforehand; it functions as independent evidence like a pointing gesture.

Checkpoint
How does Al-Sawi resolve the circularity in proving God's speech?
A) By arguing that the miracle directly proves God's speech without needing the messenger.
B) By distinguishing between the role of the miracle and the speech being proven, showing that the miracle is like a pointing gesture.
C) By rejecting the need for a miracle and relying on rational proof.
D) By asserting that the circularity is only apparent and can be ignored.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi resolves the circularity by showing that the miracle functions like a pointing gesture, indicating truthfulness without presupposing God's speech, and the speech proven is internal speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that the evidence for God’s speech is circular, and how does Al-Sawi’s analogy of the pointing gesture free you from that doubt? What would it take for you to trust the gesture without demanding that God first speak audibly?
?
If the miracle is like a pointing gesture, what does that say about your responsibility to respond to it? Are you like the mute who cannot speak, or like one who understands the gesture and acts upon it? How will you respond today to the ‘pointing gestures’ in your life?
?
The distinction between internal and uttered speech means that God’s eternal speech is known only through revelation. Does that make you rely more on the Qur’an or less? What is one area of your life where you have treated the Qur’an as merely human words, and how does this lesson challenge you to see it as the internal speech of your Creator?

God as Free Agent and Refutation of Philosophers
Al-Sawi defines God as al-fa'il al-mukhtar and refutes the philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God as the free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar) and why the philosophical view of necessary action leads to false beliefs about the world and God's attributes.

Core Idea
God acts by will, not by nature or necessity.

Al-Sawi begins by defining God as the free agent, who acts according to His will. This is contrasted with the philosophers who claim God acts by necessity, which leads to serious errors. In this lesson, we explore this definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation to grasp Al-Sawi's argument.
Notice how Al-Sawi uses a Quranic verse to support his definition.
2
Identify the definition and contrast
Find the definition of God as al-fa'il al-mukhtar. Note the contrast with acting by nature or cause.
This contrast is central to the refutation of the philosophers.
3
Understand the consequences
Al-Sawi lists two consequences of the philosophical position: eternity of the world and denial of divine attributes. Reflect on why these follow from their view.
He labels this doctrine as false and clear disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفات المعاني
قوله : فهو (الإله أي المعبود بحق الفاعل المختارُ) أي : الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَختار (۱) ، لا أنَّه فاعل بالطبع أو بالعِلَّة، خلافاً للفلاسفة الملعونين، ولذا قالوا بقدم العالم؛ لأنَّه يلزم من قِدَم العِلَّة قِدَمُ المعلول، ونفوا عن الله تعالى صفاته الذاتية، وهو مذهب باطل وكفر صراح.
The attributes of meanings. His saying: So He is (the God, i.e., the worshipped by right, the free agent), i.e., He who if He wills, He does, and if He wills, He leaves. And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses (Quran 28:68). Not that He acts by nature or by cause, contrary to the cursed philosophers, and therefore they said the world is eternal because from the eternity of the cause follows the eternity of the effect, and they denied God's essential attributes, and it is a false doctrine and clear disbelief.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a definition and refutation. It begins by defining God as 'al-fa'il al-mukhtar' (the free agent), emphasizing that He acts according to His will, not by nature or cause. This contrasts with the philosophers who claim God acts by necessity. Al-Sawi then states that this philosophical position leads to two grave errors: claiming the world is eternal (since a necessary cause necessitates a necessary effect) and denying God's essential attributes (sifat dhatiyyah). He declares this doctrine false and clear kufr (disbelief). The Quranic verse is cited to support the concept of divine choice.

Key Terms
الإله
الفاعل المختار
الفلاسفة
قِدَم العالم
الصفات الذاتية
كفر صراح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's actions are necessary like a physical cause.
Correction: God acts by will, not by necessity; He is the free agent who chooses to act or not.

Checkpoint
What consequence does al-Sawi attribute to the philosophers' view that God acts by necessity?
A) It leads to affirming divine attributes.
B) It leads to the eternity of the world and denial of divine attributes.
C) It leads to belief in bodily resurrection.
D) It leads to belief in free will.

Key Takeaway
God is the free agent who acts by will, and denying this leads to false doctrines like the eternity of the world and denial of His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray, do you secretly believe that God must answer because of your effort or need? How does knowing that God is al-fa'il al-mukhtar—free to will as He chooses—change the posture of your heart when you ask of Him? This week, let every supplication be offered with genuine submission to His free choice, not with an expectation of entitlement.
?
Have you ever felt that God's actions toward you were mechanical—as if He rewards good deeds and punishes bad deeds without any personal will? Reflect on the truth that God's actions spring from His free will, not from any necessity. Will you let this truth turn your relationship with Him from one of transaction to one of humble reliance on His mercy and wisdom?
?
The philosophers denied God's free will and ended up denying His attributes. Is there any area of your life where you treat God as limited—bound by your expectations or your understanding of 'how things should work'? What would it mean for you to truly accept that God creates what He wills and chooses, even when it challenges your plans? This week, identify one situation where you have assumed God 'must' act in a certain way, and actively surrender that expectation to His free will.

The Seven Agreed-Upon Divine Attributes
Al-Sawi defines his scope: seven agreed attributes, omitting perception and entailed ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Sawi confines himself to seven divine attributes and why the attribute of perception is not included.

Core Idea
The seven attributes are agreed; perception is left open.

Al-Sawi introduces the seven attributes that all Sunni theologians agree upon. He explains why he excludes the attribute of perception and why he does not mention the entailed attributes. This lesson clarifies the scholarly consensus and the cautious approach toward disputed attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of Al-Sawi's statement. Notice his careful wording about agreement and omission.
The phrase 'فلذا' may be a variant; it likely means 'therefore'.
2
Understand the seven agreed attributes
Al-Sawi says these seven are agreed upon by the theologians ('القوم'). Reflect on why agreement matters in Islamic theology.
He does not list the seven here, but they are well-known: life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, sight, speech.
3
Examine the omission of perception
He deliberately does not add the attribute of perception (al-idrāk) because the truth is to stop (al-waqf) – i.e., suspend judgment. Understand that this reflects a divergence among scholars.
Perception is sometimes considered an eighth attribute by some, but Al-Sawi follows the safer position.
4
Note the exclusion of entailed attributes
He also does not mention the 'entailed attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) that necessarily accompany the seven attribute meanings. This indicates his focus is on the essential attributes themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلذا واعلم أنَّ هذه الصفات السبع هي السَّبع هي المتفق عليها بين القوم، اقتصرت عليها، ولم أزد ما زاد بعضهم من صفة الإدراك، ولأنَّ الحقّ فيها الوقف، ولم أذكر الصفات المعنوية اللازمة للسبع المعاني، وهي بهذا المعنى .
Therefore, know that these seven attributes are the seven agreed upon among the people (theologians). I confined myself to them and did not add what some added of the attribute of perception (al-idrāk), because the truth regarding it is to stop (al-waqf). And I did not mention the entailed attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) necessary for the seven (attribute) meanings, and they are in this sense.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a statement about the seven divine attributes. Al-Sawi declares that these seven are agreed upon by the theologians, so he limits himself to them. He does not include the additional attribute of perception (al-idrāk) that some added, because the correct position is to stop (al-waqf) – i.e., neither affirm nor deny it definitively. He also says he did not mention the 'entailed attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) that necessarily accompany the seven attribute meanings, implying his discussion follows a certain method.

Key Terms
الصفات السبع
صفة الإدراك
الوقف
الصفات المعنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Six
B) Seven
C) Eight
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Al-Sawi affirms seven agreed-upon divine attributes and refrains from adding the attribute of perception due to scholarly caution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Sawi omitted entailed attributes because they are secondary. Do you sometimes focus on deduced doctrines rather than the core fundamentals of faith? This week, will you review whether your beliefs are built on primary sources or on human inferences?
?
Intellectual humility means saying 'I don't know' when evidence is insufficient. In your pursuit of truth, do you feel pressured to have an opinion on every theological issue? Will you commit to stopping where Allah's revelation stops, trusting His wisdom over your need for complete answers?
