Two Opinions on Allah's Attribute of Perception
Al-Sawi analyzes two opinions on the attribute of perception and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major opinions regarding Allah's attribute of perception (al-idrāk) and how each opinion shapes the understanding of its attachments (taʿalluqāt).

Core Idea
Allah's perception is understood through two competing opinions about its unity and scope.

Al-Sawi unpacks the phrase 'from the attribute of perception' by presenting two major opinions among scholars. One sees perception as a single attribute, the other as three distinct perceptions. He then explains the different attachments that follow from each view. This passage reveals the complexity of discussing divine attributes while affirming that ultimately the full reality is known only to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for its structure. Notice the two opinions and their sub-cases.
The text has some incomplete sentences; focus on the main arguments.
2
Identify the two opinions
The first opinion holds that perception is a single attribute; the second that it is three distinct perceptions. Notice the sub-opinions within the first.
3
Analyze the attachments
See how each opinion leads to different attachments: three attachments if perception relates to all existents, two attachments (primordial and actualized) if it relates to specific sense-objects.
The term 'ṣalūḥī qadīm' means a potential eternal attachment, and 'tanjīzī ḥādith' means an actualized contingent attachment.
4
Reflect on the final note
The text ends with a comment about 'the truth is to stop' (al-ḥaqq al-waqf), suggesting that the best position is to withhold judgment on these details.
This reflects a cautious theological approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( من صفة الإدراك) : ظاهره أنها صفة واحدة، وهو أحد قولين، وعليه فقيل : متعلّقة بالموجودات ، وقيل : بالمشمومات والملموسات والمذوقات، والآخر أنَّها إدراكات ثلاثة كلُّ واحد متعلق بشيء خاص، فعلى أنَّه يتعلق بالموجودات يكون كالسمع والبصر، له ثلاث تعلقات، ولا يعلم المغايرة بينها إلا هو تعالى وعلى تعلقه بالأمور الثلاثة سواء قلنا : إنَّه واحد أو متعدّد؛ فله تعلقان : صلوحي قديم، وتنجيزي حادث، فتدبر. قوله : (ولأنَّ الحقَّ الوقف): الأظهر حذف الواو وجعله علة لعدم
His statement: 'From the attribute of perception (al-idrāk)': its apparent meaning is that it is a single attribute, and this is one of two opinions. According to it, it is said to pertain to existing things, and it is said to pertain to smells, touches, and tastes. The other opinion is that they are three perceptions, each related to a specific thing. So if it pertains to existing things, it is like hearing and sight, having three attachments, and no one knows the difference between them except Him, exalted. And if it pertains to the three things, whether we say it is one or multiple, it has two attachments: a primordial potential attachment (ṣalūḥī qadīm) and an actualized contingent attachment (tanjīzī ḥādith). So ponder. His statement: 'And because the truth is to stop (al-waqf)': apparently the waw is to be deleted and made a cause for not...
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is commentary on the phrase 'from the attribute of perception' from the original text. Al-Sawi explains that there are two opinions about this attribute. The first opinion holds that perception is a single attribute, and then two sub-opinions arise: either it pertains to all existents (like hearing and sight) or it pertains specifically to smells, touches, and tastes. The second opinion holds that perception consists of three distinct perceptions, each attached to a specific sense modality. He then details the attachments: if it pertains to existents, it has three attachments (like hearing and sight), but the difference between them is known only to Allah. If it pertains to the three specific things, whether one attribute or multiple, it has two attachments: one eternal (ṣalūḥī qadīm) and one temporal (tanjīzī ḥādith). The final comment on 'the truth is to stop' suggests that the best position is to withhold judgment. The text ends with an incomplete note about omitting the waw and making it a cause.

Key Terms
صفة الإدراك
تعلق
صلوحي قديم
تنجيزي حادث
الوقف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the attribute of perception is definitively one single attribute without dispute.
Correction: The passage shows there are two legitimate opinions: one considers it a single attribute, the other considers it three distinct perceptions. Scholars differ, and the matter is not settled.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The attribute of perception is subject to scholarly debate, with opinions differing on its unity and scope, and each view leads to distinct models of how this attribute attaches to its objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the scholarly disagreement on something as fundamental as Allah's perception affect your own certainty? Are you comfortable not having a definitive answer, or does it make you uneasy? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
When you face theological debates where the conclusion is 'the truth is to stop,' do you humbly accept the limit of human understanding, or do you feel a pull to push for a clear resolution? What practical step can you take today to surrender that area to Allah?
?
Consider the concept of 'primordial potential attachment'—the idea that Allah's perception of certain things existed in His knowledge from eternity. How does this perspective shape your belief about Allah's knowledge of you before you were born? Are you willing to trust that Allah has always known and perceived you perfectly, even when you don't understand His timing?

The Three Opinions on God's Attributes: Suspension, Affirmation, Negation
Al-Sawi discusses the three opinions on the three attributes and declares the correct stance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions regarding God's attributes of being knowing, living, and powerful, and why the correct stance is suspension (waqf) due to the scriptural nature of the proof.

Core Idea
The three attributes are not additional entities but expressions of meanings; yet the safe stance is to suspend judgment.

Al-Sawi engages a nuanced debate about three specific divine attributes: God's being knowing, living, and powerful. He presents three opinions: affirmation, negation, and suspension, and argues that suspension is the soundest because the evidence is scriptural and no explicit text affirms them. This lesson unpacks the arguments and clarifies the Ash'ari position that these attributes are mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the flow: first the Ash'ari position, then the declaration that the truth is suspension, then the three opinions, then the technical explanation of 'i'tibari'.
Pay attention to the phrase 'الحق الوقف' which immediately follows the mention of Ash'ari's view.
2
Identify the three opinions
Find where the text lists three opinions: suspension (waqf), affirmation based on rational proof, and negation based on transmitted proof. Note which one is deemed the soundest.
The text says 'وهذا أحد أقوال ثلاثة هو أصحها' — so suspension is the soundest.
3
Understand the reasoning behind each opinion
For each opinion, note the basis: suspension because proof is naqli but no text; affirmation because attributes are perfections proven rationally; negation because no text and affirming without scriptural evidence implies deficiency.
The third opinion mentions that sense attributes like smell/taste involve conditioning, which is impossible for God.
4
Grasp the Ash'ari concept of 'i'tibari'
Read the commentary section explaining that 'not having external existence' means these attributes are mental considerations real in themselves, not subsistent entities. Example: power is existential; 'being powerful' is a necessary attribute but, per Ash'ari, only in the mind.
This clarifies the Ash'ari position without falling into the extremes of affirming separate entities or denying attributes altogether.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه تعالى عالماً، وكونه حياً، وكونه تعالى قادراً ... إلخ ؛ لأنَّ الحقِّ ما ذهب إليه إمامنا إمامُ أهل السُّنَّة أبو الحسن الأشعري رضي الله تعالى عنه من أنَّها ليست زائدة على المعاني، بل هي عبارة عن قيام المعاني بالذات، لا أنّ لها ثبوتاً في الخارج عن الذهن، بناء على نفي الحال وأنَّه لا واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم. الزيادة، وإنما كان الحقُّ الوقف ؛ لأنَّ دليل الصفات الثلاثة نقلي، ولم يرد سمع بإثباتها، وهذا أحد أقوال ثلاثة هو أصحها والثَّاني : إثباتها بناء على أنَّ إثبات الصفات الثلاثة بالدليل العقلي، وهي من جملة الكمالات. والثَّالث : نفيها بناء على أنَّ إثباتها بالدَّليل السمعي، ولم يرد في الإدراك نصّ، وأيضاً إثباتها بدون نقل يوهم النقص ؛ لأنَّ الشَّم والذوق واللمس تفيد التكيف والاتّصال، وهو محال عليه تعالى. قوله : (لا أنَّ لها ثبوتاً في الخارج أي: بحيث تكون قائمة بالذات، فلا ينافي أنَّ هذا الأمر اعتباري متحقق في نفسه، بقطع النظر عن اعتبار المعتبر، فالقدرة مثلاً : صفة قائمة بالذات وجوديَّة يصح أن ترى، وكونه قادراً على غير قول الأشعري صفة قائمة بالذات لازمة للقدرة، ثابتة في الخارج لا ترى ،وهكذا ، وعلى كلام الأشعري صفة اعتباريَّة لها ثبوت في الذهن فقط .
His being, exalted is He, knowing, and His being living, and His being, exalted is He, powerful... etc.; because the truth is what our imam, the imam of the Sunnis, Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, may Allah be pleased with him, held: that they are not additional to the meanings, but rather they are expressions of the subsistence of the meanings in the essence, not that they have existence externally from the mind, based on the negation of the state (al-ḥāl) and that there is no intermediate between existence and non-existence. The addition, and the truth is suspension (al-waqf); because the proof for the three attributes is transmitted (naqlī), and no scriptural evidence has come for their affirmation. This is one of three opinions, the soundest of them. The second: affirming them based on the proof of the three attributes by rational evidence, and they are among the perfections. The third: negating them based on the proof of them by transmitted evidence, and no text has come regarding perception; also affirming them without transmission suggests deficiency because smell, taste, and touch involve conditioning and connection, which is impossible for Him, exalted is He. His saying: (not that they have existence externally) meaning: such that they subsist in the essence; this does not contradict that this thing is a mental consideration (i‘tibārī) real in itself, regardless of the consideration of the considerer. So power, for example, is an attribute subsisting in the essence, existential, which can be seen; and His being powerful, according to other than al-Ash'ari's statement, is an attribute subsisting in the essence, necessary for power, established externally but not seen, and so on. And according to al-Ash'ari's statement, it is an attributive consideration having existence only in the mind.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage discusses the debate around the three attributes of God: His being knowing, living, and powerful. The author states that the correct view is that of Imam al-Ash'ari: these attributes are not additional to the meanings (i.e., knowledge, life, power) but are expressions of the subsistence of those meanings in the divine essence. They do not have external existence separate from the mind, based on the negation of the intermediate state (ḥāl) and that there is no third category between existence and non-existence. Then the author notes that the correct stance is actually suspension (waqf) because the proof for these three attributes is scriptural (naqlī), and no scripture has come to affirm them. He lists three opinions: (1) suspension, which is the soundest; (2) affirmation based on rational proof that they are perfections; (3) negation based on scriptural proof and because affirming without scriptural evidence suggests deficiency (since senses involve conditioning, impossible for God). The commentary then explains the phrase 'not that they have existence externally': it means they are not subsistent in the essence as separate entities, but they are mental considerations real in themselves. For example, power is an existential attribute that can be seen (according to some), while 'being powerful' is an attribute necessary for power, established externally but not seen. According to al-Ash'ari, it is a mental consideration only.

Misconception: The attributes of knowing, living, and powerful are separate, independently existing entities added to the divine essence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that according to al-Ash'ari, these attributes are not additional entities; they are expressions of the subsistence of the meanings (knowledge, life, power) in the essence, and have only mental existence (i'tibari).

Key Takeaway
The soundest opinion regarding God's attributes of knowing, living, and powerful is to suspend judgment, as the proof is scriptural and no explicit text affirms them.

Will you adopt a stance of intellectual humility (waqf) in your own faith journey, rather than overstepping scriptural bounds?
?

?

Definition of Ta‘alluq and Its Rational Necessity
Al-Sawi defines ta‘alluq and states its rational necessity with emphasis on perpetuity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of ta‘alluq (relation) for the attributes of meanings, its rational necessity, and the importance of affirming such relations perpetually.

Core Idea
Ta‘alluq is the attribute's necessary connection to its object, a perpetual requirement of its perfection.

Al-Sawi has just explained the attributes of meanings themselves. Now he turns to their relations (ta‘alluqāt). A relation is what connects each attribute to its specific object—knowledge to the known, will to the willed, power to the powerful. This relation is not optional; it is rationally necessary and perpetual. The passage also warns against a misunderstanding that could lead to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's flow.
Notice the compact style; terms like ḥatman and dawāman are emphatic.
2
Identify the definition and examples
Find the definition of ta‘alluq: it is the attribute's requiring an additional matter beyond its subsistence in the essence. Then note the three examples: knowledge, will, and power.
The examples show how each attribute needs an object (malūm, murād, maqdūr).
3
Understand the necessity and perpetuity
The author states it is rationally necessary (wājib) and emphasizes perpetuity with ḥatman and dawāman. Reflect on why this is needed: without perpetual relation, the attribute would be incomplete.
The term ḥatman emphasizes obligation, and dawāman emphasizes continuity.
4
Note the warning about states (aḥwāl)
Al-Sawi mentions a theological nuance: on the view affirming states, the attribute-of-meaning (al-ma‘nawiyya) does not have relations because relation itself is a state. The author recommends enumeration for clarity and to avoid disbelief.
This is a technical point for advanced students; do not let it confuse the core definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من بيان صفات المعاني شرع في بيان تعلقها . تعريف التعلق والتعلُّق: اقتضاء الصفة أمراً زائداً على قيامها بالذات؛ كاقتضاء العلم معلوماً ينكشف به واقتضاء الإرادة مراداً يتخصص بها، واقتضاء القدرة مقدوراً، وهكذا. فقال: (وواجب) عقلاً (تعليقُ ذي) أي : هذه الصفاتِ) أي : صفات المعاني ( حَتْماً ) أي : لزوماً ، (دواماً) أي: على سبيل الدوام ،والاستمرار وهذا من زيادة التأكيد ؛ واعلم أنَّه على القول بإثبات الأحوال فليس للمعنوية تعلقات كالمعاني؛ لأنَّ التعلُّق حال، وحينئذ يلزم وصف الحال بالحال، وكان المناسب للشارح الا الله أن يعدها كما عدها السَّنوسي واللقاني(۱)؛ لأجل الإيضاح والتّعليم، ولأنَّ تركها ربَّما يوقع العوام في نفي نسبتها إلى الله تعالى وهو كفر . قوله: (وهذا من زيادة التَّأكيد :أي: قوله : (دواماً)، فحتماً توكيد لمعنى الوجوب، و(دواماً) زيادة تأكيد . قوله : (تصحح) : أي : توجب.
When he finished explaining the attributes of meanings, he began explaining their relation (ta‘alluq). Definition of ta‘alluq: Ta‘alluq is the attribute's requiring an additional matter beyond its subsistence in the essence; such as knowledge requiring a known object that becomes disclosed by it, and will requiring a willed object that becomes specified by it, and power requiring an object of power, and so on. So he said: (And it is rationally necessary (the attaching of that which) i.e., these attributes (i.e., attributes of meanings) (necessarily) i.e., perpetually (continuously) i.e., in the manner of perpetuity and continuity, and this is of increased emphasis. Know that according to the view affirming the states (aḥwāl), the attribute-of-meaning does not have relations like the attributes-of-meaning; because the relation is a state, and then it would entail describing a state by a state. The appropriate thing for the commentator - may Allah have mercy on him - would be to enumerate them as al-Sanūsī and al-Laqānī enumerated them, for clarification and teaching, and because leaving them might cause the common people to negate their attribution to Allah Almighty, which is disbelief. His saying: (And this is of increased emphasis) i.e., his saying: (continuously); so (necessarily) emphasizes the meaning of obligation, and (continuously) is an additional emphasis. His saying: (renders correct) i.e., necessitates.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage defines ta‘alluq (relation) of the attributes of meanings. After explaining the attributes themselves, the author now explains their relations. Ta‘alluq is the attribute's requiring something additional beyond its subsistence in the essence, such as knowledge requiring a known object, will requiring a willed object, power requiring an object of power. The author states that it is rationally necessary (wājib) for each attribute of meaning to be attached (mu‘allaq) to something necessarily and perpetually. The terms 'ḥatman' (necessarily) and 'dawāman' (continuously) are emphases. Then the author notes that according to the view that affirms states (aḥwāl), the attribute-of-meaning (al-ma‘nawiyya) does not have relations like the meanings themselves, because the relation itself is a state, leading to describing a state by a state, which is problematic. The commentator suggests that it would be better to enumerate these attributes (like al-Sanūsī and al-Laqānī did) for clarity and to prevent the common people from negating their attribution to God, which would be disbelief. Finally, he explains that 'dawāman' is an additional emphasis on top of 'ḥatman'.

Correction: Rather, it is a necessary relation of the attribute's perfection; the relation does not imply any change in the divine essence.

Key Takeaway
Ta‘alluq is the rational, perpetual requirement that each attribute of meaning be connected to its specific object.

Why Enumerate Attributes Despite the Ahwal Problem?
Author explains why the commentator enumerates ma‘nawiyya attributes despite the issue of ahwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator enumerates the ma‘nawiyya attributes despite a technical issue with the theory of ahwal.

Core Idea
Clarity and preventing disbelief outweigh abstract theological precision.

In this passage, the commentator acknowledges a subtle problem with the theory of states (ahwal) as it applies to the ma‘nawiyya attributes. Rather than omitting them, he chooses to list them. Why? The passage explains that the commentator prioritizes educational benefit and the grave danger of laypeople denying these attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the technical problem
Read the passage to find the issue with describing a state by a state. Understand why the ma‘nawiyya attributes would not have relations like the ma‘ani attributes under the theory of ahwal.
The key phrase is 'wasf al-hal bi al-hal'.
2
Observe the commentator's choice
Note that despite the problem, the commentator enumerates the attributes just as Sanusi and Laqani did. Ask yourself what might motivate this decision.
3
Extract the two reasons
The passage gives two explicit reasons: for clarification and teaching, and because omitting them could lead to disbelief. Identify these in the text.
The word 'لأجل الإيضاح والتعليم' indicates the first reason, and 'لأن تركها ربما يوقع العوام...' indicates the second.
4
Reflect on the prioritization
Consider the balance between technical consistency and practical teaching. Why is preventing disbelief more important than avoiding a subtle logical issue?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنَّه على القول بإثبات الأحوال فليس للمعنوية تعلقات كالمعاني؛ لأنَّ التعلُّق حال، وحينئذ يلزم وصف الحال بالحال، وكان المناسب للشارح إلا أن يعدها كما عدها السنوسي واللقاني؛ لأجل الإيضاح والتعليم، ولأنَّ تركها ربَّما يوقع العوام في نفي نسبتها إلى الله تعالى وهو كفر.
And know that according to the view affirming al-ahwal (states), the ma‘nawiyya attributes do not have relations like the ma‘ani attributes; because ta‘alluq is a state, and then it would require describing a state by a state. And it would have been appropriate for the commentator except that he enumerated them as Sanusi and Laqani enumerated them, for the sake of clarification and teaching, and because neglecting them might lead the common people to deny their ascription to Allah the Exalted, which is disbelief.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage addresses a subtle theological issue. The author points out that if one holds the Ash‘ari theory of al-ahwal (states), then the ma‘nawiyya attributes (derived attributes like 'being knowing') would not have ta‘alluq in the same way as the ma‘ani attributes (real attributes like knowledge). Why? Because ta‘alluq itself is a state (hal), and attributing a state to a state would be describing a state by a state (wasf al-hal bi al-hal), which is problematic. Despite this, the commentator (al-Sawi) chose to enumerate the ma‘nawiyya attributes just as Sanusi and Laqani did, for two reasons: first, for clarity and teaching purposes, and second, because omitting them might lead laypeople to deny that these attributes are ascribed to Allah, which would be disbelief. So the commentator prioritizes educational benefit over technical precision.

Correction: Denying these attributes is disbelief (kufr), as stated in the passage: 'which is disbelief.'

Classification of Attributes Regarding Ta‘alluq
Author classifies attributes into four divisions based on ta‘alluq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourfold classification of divine attributes based on whether they have ta‘alluq (relation), and specifically how life differs from knowledge and speech.

Core Idea
Attributes are divided into those without ta‘alluq (life) and those with ta‘alluq (knowledge, speech, etc.).

This passage from Hashiyat al-Sawi presents a foundational classification in Islamic theology: divine attributes are categorized by whether they have a relation (ta‘alluq) to something else. The author explains that life is unique in having no ta‘alluq, while other attributes like knowledge and speech relate to their objects. Let's explore this division.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author comments on the phrase 'tusahhih' and sets up the classification. Read the opening lines carefully.
2
Learn the four divisions
Identify the four divisions: one without ta‘alluq (life) and three with ta‘alluq, the first of which includes knowledge and speech.
3
Focus on life
Life is defined as an attribute that enables perception but does not require any object beyond its locus.
4
Focus on knowledge and speech
Knowledge and speech are said to relate to all divisions of rational judgment. Note the description of speech as transcendent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (تصحح) : أي : توجب. لأنَّ الواجب النقلى شأنه ذلك، (ما عدا الحياة) بالجر، فما زائدة، وعدا حرف جرّ، فيجب على كلِّ مكلَّف أن يعتقد ذلك. وحاصله: أنَّ هذه الصفات بالنسبة للتّعلُّق وعدمه أربعة أقسام: - قسم منها لا يتعلق بشيء، وهو الحياة، إذ هي صفة تُصحح لمن قامت به الإدراك، من غير أن تطلب أمراً زائداً على قيامها بمحلّها. - وقسم يتعلق، وهو ثلاثة أقسام: القسم الأول من الصفات التي لها تعلق الأول منها: ما يتعلق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي، وهو صفتان: العلم والكلام، وإليه أشار بقوله: (فالعِلمُ جَزْماً) معمول لقوله: (تعلَّقا) قدم عليه، (والكلام السّامي) أي: العالي المرتفع القدر، المنزه عن الحروف والأصوات، والتقديم والتّأخير، والسُّكوتِ واللحن والإعراب، وغير ذلك مما يتّصف.
His saying: 'tusahhih' means it necessitates. Because the transmitted obligation is such that, except life, in the genitive, so 'ma' is extra and '‘ada' is a preposition, so it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person to believe that. And its summary: that these attributes, with respect to having ta‘alluq or not, are four divisions: - One division does not have ta‘alluq with anything, and that is life, for it is an attribute that makes its possessor capable of perception, without demanding an extra thing beyond its subsisting in its locus. - And a division that has ta‘alluq, and it is three divisions: the first of the attributes that have ta‘alluq is that which relates to all divisions of rational judgment, and it is two attributes: knowledge and speech, and to it he alluded by his saying: 'So knowledge is a resolution' is an object of his saying: 'it related' preceded upon it, 'and speech is sublime' i.e., lofty, elevated in rank, transcendent from letters and sounds, and from precedence and delay, and silence, and grammatical mistakes, and i‘rab, and other than that with which it is described.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of attributes based on whether they have ta‘alluq (relation) or not. The author first comments on the phrase 'tusahhih' (it makes valid), explaining it means 'it necessitates' (tujib). He then notes that the transmitted obligation applies to everything except life, which is in the genitive due to the preposition '‘ada'. Then he gives the summary: attributes are four divisions regarding ta‘alluq. First division: attributes without any ta‘alluq, which is life. Life is an attribute that enables its possessor to perceive, but it does not demand any extra thing beyond itself. Second division: attributes that have ta‘alluq, and these are further subdivided into three types. The first of these three types is that which relates to all divisions of rational judgment, and it comprises two attributes: knowledge and speech. The author then alludes to the source text which says 'fa al-‘ilmu jazman' (so knowledge is a resolution) which is the object of the verb 'ta‘allaqa' (it related), and 'al-kalam al-sami' (speech is sublime), which is transcendent over letters, sounds, precedence, delay, silence, grammatical error, i‘rab, etc.

Misconception: Life relates to something like perception or existence
Correction: Life only enables perception but does not have a relation (ta‘alluq) to any object; it is self-sufficient in its locus.

Key Takeaway

Life as an Intellectual Condition for Perception
Author defines life as an intellectual condition for perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of life as an intellectual condition (shart 'aqli) for perception, including what it entails and what it does not entail.

Core Idea
Life makes perception possible, but does not cause it.

In this passage, the author clarifies the relationship between the divine attribute of life and the attribute of perception. He defines life as an intellectual condition, a key concept in kalam for understanding how one attribute can be a prerequisite for another without being its cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Note the definition of life and the conditional relationship.
2
Identify the three parts of the definition
The passage states that life is an intellectual condition: absence of life necessarily implies absence of perception; presence of life does not imply presence or absence of perception.
This is the core logical structure.
3
Understand 'shart 'aqli'
An intellectual condition is a rational prerequisite, not a physical cause. It is a necessary but not sufficient condition.
This distinguishes it from a physical cause like heat causing boiling.
4
Apply to divine and created life
The text says this definition applies to life whether eternal or originated, indicating the same logical relationship holds.
The eternal life of Allah is discussed elsewhere.
5
Reflect on significance
This clarifies that perception does not come from life itself but from Allah's will; life merely makes it possible.
Avoids implying that life automatically produces perception.

Source Text

وقوله : (الإدراك) : أي : الاتصاف به أزلاً وأبداً، فهي شرط عقلي يلزم من عدمها عدم الإدراك، ولا يلزم من وجودها وجود الإدراك ولا عدمه، وهذا تعريف للحياة من حيث هي قديمة أو حادثة، وتقدم تعريف القديمة في الشَّرح. قوله : (معمول) : أي : لقوله: (جزماً). قوله: (والتقديم والتأخير ) : أراد به لازمه ، وهو التقدم والتأخر ؛ لأنَّه هو الذي من صفات الكلام.

Commentary

The author explains that 'al-idrak' (perception) means being described with it eternally and forever. Life is a necessary condition for perception, but not a sufficient cause; it only makes perception possible.

?

Understanding Technical Linguistic Terms in Kalam
Author clarifies two technical terms from the source text.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the terms 'ma'mul' and 'al-taqdim wa ta'khir' as used in the source, and their intended references.

Core Idea
Technical terms in kalam often refer to their logical implications, not literal meanings.

In this brief passage, the author clarifies two terms from the main text: 'ma'mul' (governed) and 'al-taqdim wa ta'khir' (giving precedence and delay). These clarifications are essential for correctly interpreting the grammatical and theological context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the two clarifications carefully. Note how the author explains each term by pointing to its necessary consequence.
2
Understand 'ma'mul'
The author states that 'ma'mul' is the object (ma'mul) of the word 'jazman' (as a resolution). This is a grammatical point: the word in the source text that is governed by 'jazman'.
This clarifies the syntactic relationship.
3
Understand 'al-taqdim wa al-ta'khir'
The author says that the intended meaning of 'al-taqdim wa al-ta'khir' is its necessary consequence, which is 'al-taqaddum wa al-ta'akhkhur' (precedence and delay), because that is one of the attributes of speech.
The phrase is used metaphorically to indicate logical priority, not temporal ordering.
4
Reflect on method
Notice that the author explains terms by referring to what they imply (lazim), a common practice in kalam to avoid ambiguity.

The Four Categories of Divine Attributes Regarding Relation
Al-Sawi classifies divine attributes into four categories based on their relation to objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine attributes are classified based on their relation to objects, and why life is uniquely non-relating while knowledge and speech relate to all rational judgments.

Core Idea
Life qualifies without relating; knowledge and speech relate to everything possible, impossible, and necessary.

Al-Sawi introduces a key classification of divine attributes from the perspective of 'relation' (ta'alluq). This helps us understand how each attribute functions. He begins by stating that this classification is obligatory belief, then enumerates the four categories, focusing on life and the first relating category. This lesson unpacks that classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice how Al-Sawi structures his classification.
The passage begins with a summary statement about the four divisions.
2
Identify the four categories
Find the four divisions mentioned. The first is life (non-relating). Then note that three relate, with the first of these being knowledge and speech.
Only the first relating category is detailed; the other two are implied but not named here.
3
Understand why life is non-relating
Life is defined as an attribute that qualifies the subject for perception without requiring any external object. This makes it unique among the attributes.
Al-Sawi emphasizes that life is a rational condition: its absence entails absence of perception, but its presence does not guarantee perception.
4
Understand knowledge and speech
These two attributes relate to all three categories of rational judgment: necessary, impossible, and possible. Their relation is definite (jazman). Also note the description of speech as 'exalted' (al-sami).
This shows the scope of divine knowledge and speech — they encompass everything conceivable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنَّ الواجب النقلى شأنه ذلك، (ما عدا الحياة) بالجر، فما زائدة، وعدا حرف جرّ ، فيجب على كلِّ مكلَّف أن يعتقد ذلك . وحاصله : أنَّ هذه الصفات بالنسبة للتّعلُّق وعدمه أربعة أقسام : - قسم منها لا يتعلق بشيء، وهو الحياة، إذ هي صفة تُصحح لمن قامت به الإدراك، من غير أن تطلب أمراً زائداً على قيامها بمحلّها . - وقسم يتعلق، وهو ثلاثة أقسام: القسم الأول من الصفات التي لها تعلق الأول منها : ما يتعلق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي، وهو صفتان: العلم والكلام، وإليه أشار بقوله : (فالعِلمُ جَزْماً) معمول لقوله : (تعلَّقا) قدم عليه، (والكلام السّامي) أي : العالي المرتفع القدر، المنزه عن الحروف والأصوات، والتقديم والتّأخير، والسُّكوتِ واللحن والإعراب، وغير ذلك مما يتّصف به كلام الحوادث (تعلَّقا ) أي : إنَّ هاتين الصفتين تعلقا جزماً ؛ أي: مجزوماً به (بسائر ) أي : بجميع جُزئيات الأقسام) أي : أقسام الحكم العقليّ الثلاثة الواجب والمستحيل والجائز.
قوله : (الإدراك) : أي : الاتصاف به أزلاً وأبداً، فهي شرط عقلي يلزم من عدمها عدم الإدراك، ولا يلزم من وجودها وجود الإدراك ولا عدمه، وهذا تعريف للحياة من حيث هي قديمة أو حادثة، وتقدم تعريف القديمة في الشَّرح .
Because the transmitted obligation is of that nature, (except life) in the genitive, so 'ma' is extra, and 'ada' is a particle of exception. So it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person to believe that. Its summary: These attributes, with respect to relation and its absence, are four divisions: - One division does not relate to anything, and it is life, for it is an attribute that qualifies the one it subsists in for perception, without requiring anything beyond its subsistence in its subject. - And a division that relates, and it is three divisions: The first division of the attributes that have relation: that which relates to all categories of rational judgment, and it is two attributes: knowledge and speech. And he pointed to it by his saying: (So knowledge [is] definitely) as an object of his saying: (related) preceding it, (and the exalted speech) meaning: the high, exalted in rank, transcendent above letters and sounds, and precedence and delay, and silence and grammatical error and inflection, and other than that which characterizes the speech of contingents. (related) meaning: these two attributes are related definitely, i.e., with certainty, (to all) i.e., to all particulars of the categories, i.e., the three categories of rational judgment: the necessary, the impossible, and the possible.
His saying: (perception) i.e., being described with it eternally and perpetually, so it is an intellectual condition: the absence of perception necessarily follows from its absence, and from its existence neither the existence nor the absence of perception necessarily follows. This is a definition of life in terms of being eternal or originated, and the definition of the eternal has preceded in the commentary.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage classifies the divine attributes (الصفات) from the perspective of their relation (التعلق) to objects. The author begins by stating that it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person (مكلَّف) to believe this classification. The summary (حاصله) states that the attributes are of four categories with respect to relation: (1) a category that does not relate to anything, which is life (الحياة). Life is defined as an attribute that qualifies its subject for perception (الإدراك) without requiring anything beyond its subsistence in that subject. Then (2) a category that relates, and this category itself is divided into three further divisions. The first of those three divisions is that which relates to all three categories of rational judgment (الحكم العقلي: الواجب، المستحيل، الجائز), and it consists of two attributes: knowledge (العلم) and speech (الكلام). The author quotes the matn: 'So knowledge definitely related, and the exalted speech.' He explains that speech is 'exalted' because it is transcendent above letters, sounds, sequence, silence, grammatical features, etc., unlike created speech. The relation of these two attributes is 'definite' (جزماً) i.e., certain. They relate to all particulars of the three rational judgments. The passage then shifts to a gloss on the word 'perception' (الإدراك) in the definition of life: perception is being described with it eternally and perpetually. Life is a rational (intellectual) condition: if life is absent, perception is necessarily absent, but if life is present, perception may or may not be present. This definition applies to both eternal (قديمة) and originated (حادثة) life, and the definition of eternal life has been given earlier in the commentary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Life is an attribute that relates to objects, similar to knowledge or will.
Correction: Life does not relate to anything external; it only qualifies the subject for perception. It is a precondition for perception, not an act of relating.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes are classified into four categories based on relation; life stands alone as non-relating, while knowledge and speech relate to all three rational judgments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's life does not relate to anything—it is absolutely self-sufficient. Is your worship of Allah based on what He gives you, or on who He is in Himself? What would change in your prayer if you truly believed He needs nothing from you?
?
The passage teaches that Allah's speech transcends letters and sounds. When you recite the Qur'an, do you engage with it as a created book, or do you seek the uncreated reality behind the words? How can you cultivate awe for the divine speech itself, beyond the familiar Arabic text?
?
Allah's knowledge relates to every possible, impossible, and necessary thing—nothing escapes Him. Think of a single sin you committed in secret that you thought no one knew. Will you confess it to Allah today and live transparently before His all-encompassing knowledge?

Knowledge and Speech: Relating to Rational Judgments
Al-Sawi explains the distinct relations of knowledge and speech to the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attributes of knowledge and speech relate to all three rational judgments with distinct modalities of disclosure and indication.

Core Idea
Knowledge discloses truth; speech indicates meaning—both relate to all rational judgments but in different ways.

Having established the three rational judgments, we now examine how two key divine attributes—knowledge and speech—relate to them. Both are inherently relational, but their modes differ: knowledge discloses reality, while speech conveys meaning. This passage clarifies these modes and the implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relational nature
Both knowledge and speech require an object beyond themselves: knowledge requires a known object, speech requires a meaning to indicate.
This is the foundation for the rest of the lesson.
2
Distinguish the two modalities
Knowledge relates by disclosure (انكشاف), speech by indication (دلالة). Recognize this key difference.
3
Study knowledge's relation in detail
Knowledge relates to all universals and particulars eternally, without reflection, and is not described as necessary or acquired.
Notice that knowledge has a single, effective, eternal relation.
4
Analyze speech's multiplicity
Speech is one in essence but multiple in relations, leading to command, prohibition, report, and interrogation.
The multiplicity is due to relations, not the essence of speech itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (معمول) : أي : لقوله: (جزماً).
قوله: (والتقديم والتأخير ) : أراد به لازمه ، وهو التقدم والتأخر ؛ لأنَّه هو الذي من صفات الكلام.
- أما كونهما متعلّقين ؛ فلأنَّهما طلبا أمراً زائداً على قيامهما بمحلّهما ، إذ العلم يقتضى معلوماً ينكشف به، والكلام يقتضي معنى يدل عليه .
- وأما تعلُّقهما بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي : فظاهر، إلا أنَّ تعلقهما مختلف، فتعلُّق العلم تعلُّق انكشاف وتعلُّق الكلام تعلُّقُ دلالة كما فهم مما ذكرته لك .
- تعلق العلم : فالعلم يتعلّق بجميع الكلِّيَّات والجزئيات، أزلاً وأبداً ، بلا تأمل واستدلال، ولا سبب من الأسباب، فلا يوصف بالضروري ولا بالنظري، وله تعلق واحد تنجيزي قديم.
- تعلقات الكلام : والكلام يدلُّ على ما ذُكر دلالة مستمرة بلا انقطاع، أزلاً وأبداً، فهو تعالی به آمر ناءٍ مُخبر، فهو في نفسه واحد، وتكثُره إنَّما هو بتكثر التَّعلقات؛ كالعلم والقدرة ، ولذا قسموه إلى أمر ونهي، وخبر واستخبار . - فمن حيث اقتضاؤه فعلاً أو تركاً يسمّى أمراً ونهياً
قوله : ( ولذا قسموه) : أي : من حيث التعلقات.
قوله : (يسمى أمرا ونهيا) : لفّ ونشر مرتب .
His saying: (operand) i.e., to his saying: (definitely).
His saying: (and precedence and delay) he intended by it its necessary concomitant, which is priority and posteriority, because that is among the attributes of speech.
- As for their being related: it is because they demand something additional to their subsistence in their subject, for knowledge requires a known object that is disclosed by it, and speech requires a meaning that it indicates.
- As for their relation to all categories of rational judgment: it is apparent, except that their relation is different: the relation of knowledge is a relation of disclosure, and the relation of speech is a relation of indication, as understood from what I mentioned to you.
- The relation of knowledge: knowledge relates to all universals and particulars, eternally and perpetually, without reflection and reasoning, and without any cause, so it is not described as necessary or acquired, and it has a single, effective, eternal relation.
- The relations of speech: speech indicates what was mentioned with continuous indication without interruption, eternally and perpetually. So He, exalted, is by it a commander, a prohibitor, an informant. So it is in itself one, and its multiplicity is only due to the multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Therefore, they divided it into command, prohibition, report, and interrogation. So in terms of its requiring an action or abandonment, it is called command and prohibition.
His saying: (therefore they divided it) i.e., in terms of relations.
His saying: (it is called command and prohibition) is an ordered [لفّ ونشر مرتب].
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the two attributes mentioned in the previous division: knowledge (العلم) and speech (الكلام). It begins with glosses on the commentary: 'معمول' refers back to 'جزماً', and 'التقديم والتأخير' in the description of speech is interpreted as its necessary concomitant, priority and posteriority, which are attributes of speech (i.e., speech can be prior or posterior in the sense of commands, etc.).

The main argument: Both knowledge and speech are relational in that they require something beyond their subsistence in God. Knowledge requires a known object (معلوم) that is disclosed by it (ينكشف به). Speech requires a meaning (معنى) that it indicates (يدل عليه). Their relation to all three rational judgments (واجب، مستحيل، جائز) is apparent; however, their modality differs: knowledge relates by disclosure (انكشاف), speech relates by indication (دلالة).

Then the author details each:
- Knowledge: It relates to all universals and particulars, eternally (أزلاً وأبداً) without reflection or reasoning, and without any cause. Therefore, it is not described as necessary (ضروري) or acquired (نظري). It has a single, effective (تنجيزي), eternal (قديم) relation.
- Speech: It indicates continuously without interruption, eternally. Through it, God commands, prohibits, and informs. Speech itself is one, but its multiplicity arises from the multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Thus, theologians divided it into command (أمر), prohibition (نهي), report (خبر), and interrogation (استخبار). From the perspective of requiring an action or abandonment, it is called command and prohibition. The final gloss notes that the division is an ordered [لفّ ونشر مرتب], meaning the terms are presented in a woven-and-unfolded pattern.

الكلام

الانكشاف
الدلالة
العلوم الكلية والجزئية
الضروري
النظري
الأمر
النهي
الخبر
الاستخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that knowledge and speech have the same type of relation to rational judgments.
Correction: The author emphasizes that they differ: knowledge discloses, speech indicates.

Checkpoint
How does speech relate to rational judgments according to Al-Sawi?
A) By disclosure
B) By indication
C) By creation
D) By necessity

Key Takeaway
Knowledge and speech both relate to all rational judgments, but knowledge discloses while speech indicates, and speech's multiplicity arises from its relations, not its essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge discloses every thought you have ever had—without reflection or reasoning. Are you living today as if every hidden motive is fully exposed? What one habit will you change because He already knows it?
?
God's speech commands and prohibits. You claim to believe His speech is one, yet you selectively obey. Which of His commands do you habitually ignore, and what will you do this week to obey it from the heart?
?
If speech's multiplicity is only in its relations, not its essence, then every word of God—whether command or promise—carries equal weight. Which of His promises do you doubt most, and how will you act on that promise today to prove your trust?

Knowledge and Speech Relate to All Rational Judgments
The author explains the relation of knowledge and speech to all rational judgments and details the relation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attributes of knowledge and speech relate to the three rational judgments, and the distinct modes of relation—disclosure for knowledge and indication for speech.

Core Idea
Knowledge discloses, speech indicates—both encompass all rational judgments.

The author now turns to how specific divine attributes relate to the categories of necessary, impossible, and contingent. He explains that both knowledge and speech are related to all these divisions, but in fundamentally different ways. Understanding this distinction is key to properly conceptualizing God's attributes without anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author's structure: he first states a general claim, then explains the reason, then differentiates the two attributes, and finally elaborates on knowledge.
Pay attention to the terms انكشاف (disclosure) and دلالة (indication).
2
Identify the two attributes and their modes
The passage discusses knowledge (علم) and speech (كلام). For each, identify how they relate to the rational judgments: knowledge by disclosure, speech by indication.
3
Examine the special nature of divine knowledge
The author elaborates that divine knowledge covers all universals and particulars, eternally and forever, without reflection or inference. He concludes it is not described as necessary or acquired.
Contrast this with human knowledge which is acquired through reasoning.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters: it affirms God's omniscience and His direct, timeless awareness of everything, while speech indicates His will and commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلق الصفات كلام الحوادث (تعلَّقا) أي: إنَّ هاتين الصفتين تعلقا جزماً؛ أي: مجزوماً به (بسائر) أي: بجميع جُزئيات الأقسام) أي: أقسام الحكم العقليّ الثلاثة الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. - أما كونهما متعلّقين؛ فلأنَّهما طلبا أمراً زائداً على قيامهما بمحلّهما، إذ العلم يقتضى معلوماً ينكشف به، والكلام يقتضي معنى يدل عليه. - وأما تعلُّقهما بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي: فظاهر، إلا أنَّ تعلقهما مختلف، فتعلُّق العلم تعلُّق انكشاف وتعلُّق الكلام تعلُّقُ دلالة كما فهم مما ذكرته لك. - تعلق العلم: فالعلم يتعلّق بجميع الكلِّيَّات والجزئيات، أزلاً وأبداً، بلا تأمل واستدلال، ولا سبب من الأسباب، فلا يوصف بالضروري ولا بالنظري.
The relation of attributes: The speech of events (they relate) i.e., these two attributes relate definitely; that is, with certainty (to all) i.e., to all particulars of the divisions) i.e., the divisions of the three rational judgments: the necessary, the impossible, and the contingent. As for their being related: because they require an additional matter beyond their subsistence in their locus, since knowledge requires a known object that becomes disclosed through it, and speech requires a meaning that it indicates. As for their relation to all divisions of the rational judgment: it is evident, except that their relation differs: the relation of knowledge is a relation of disclosure, and the relation of speech is a relation of indication, as understood from what I have mentioned to you. The relation of knowledge: knowledge relates to all universals and particulars, eternally and forever, without reflection or inference, nor any cause, so it is not described as necessary or acquired.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of how the attributes of knowledge (علم) and speech (كلام) relate to the three rational judgments (الحكم العقلي): necessary (واجب), impossible (مستحيل), and contingent (جائز). The author begins by stating that both attributes are related to all particulars of these judgments in a definite manner. He then provides the reason for their being related: each attribute requires an additional object beyond its own existence in the divine essence—knowledge requires a known object that becomes disclosed, and speech requires a meaning that it indicates. The relation of knowledge is one of disclosure (انكشاف), meaning it unveils or reveals its object, while the relation of speech is one of indication (دلالة), meaning it points to or signifies a meaning. The author then elaborates on the relation of knowledge: it encompasses all universals and particulars, in eternity (azalan) and perpetuity (abadan), without needing reflection (ta'mmul) or inference (istidlal), nor any cause. Therefore, knowledge is not described as necessary (daruri) or acquired (nazari), because it is direct and immediate, not dependent on reasoning or external causes.

الحكم العقلي
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
انكشاف
دلالة
ضروري
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge and speech relate in the same way to the rational judgments.
Correction: Knowledge relates by disclosure (unveiling of the object), while speech relates by indication (pointing to a meaning). They are distinct modes.

Checkpoint
According to the author, how does the relation of knowledge differ from the relation of speech?
A) Knowledge relates by indication, speech by disclosure.
B) Knowledge relates by disclosure, speech by indication.
C) Both relate by disclosure.
D) Both relate by indication.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge discloses all things directly and timelessly, while divine speech indicates meanings—both encompass every rational judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's knowledge encompasses all particulars, even your hidden thoughts and intentions. Are you living as if every moment is fully disclosed before Him? What will you do today to align your inner life with that reality?
?
The author says divine speech indicates meaning. When you read the Qur'an, do you stop at the surface or actively seek the deeper indication? What specific verse will you ponder today to move from mere reading to true understanding?
?
Since divine knowledge requires no reflection or inference, how often do you rely on your own reasoning instead of seeking direct disclosure from God through His revelation? What step will you take this week to prioritize His disclosure over your own deductions?

The Division of Divine Speech into Four Types
Al-Sanusī divides divine speech into four types based on relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine speech, though one attribute, is divided into multiple types based on its relations to actions and states.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one, but its relations create multiplicity in meaning.

In this passage, al-Sanusī explains that God's speech is a single eternal attribute, yet scholars divide it into command, prohibition, news, and interrogation. This division does not imply multiplicity in the essence of speech, but rather arises from the different relations it bears to actions and propositions. Understanding this clarifies how divine speech can address various situations without change in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and notice the unity of speech
The opening lines emphasize that speech indicates continuously, without interruption, eternally. This reinforces the oneness of the attribute.
The phrase 'فهو في نفسه واحد' is key.
2
Identify the basis of division into four types
The author states that multiplicity is due to multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Then he lists the divisions.
Focus on the two pairs: command/prohibition relate to requiring action or abstention, news/interrogation relate to affirmation/negation.
3
Distinguish between the two principles of division
The first division (command/prohibition) is based on the effect on the addressee's action; the second (news) is based on truth value. Not all four are equally discussed here; interrogation is mentioned but not elaborated.
The commentary notes that the author only details news further, but the division is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقات الكلام: والكلام يدلُّ على ما ذُكر دلالة مستمرة بلا انقطاع، أزلاً وأبداً، فهو تعالی به آمر ناءٍ مُخبر، فهو في نفسه واحد، وتكثُره إنَّما هو بتكثر التَّعلقات؛ كالعلم والقدرة، ولذا قسموه إلى أمر ونهي، وخبر واستخبار. - فمن حيث اقتضاؤه فعلاً أو تركاً يسمّى أمراً ونهياً. قوله: (ولذا قسموه): أي: من حيث التعلقات. قوله: (يسمى أمرا ونهيا): لفّ ونشر مرتب. - ومن حيث تعلقه بثبوت أمر لأمر، أو نفيه عنه، يسمى خبراً. وهل يشترط في تسميته بذلك كالخطاب وجود مخاطبين بالفعل أو لا؟ خلاف، وينبني عليه الخلاف في الأحكام، هل هي حادثة أو قديمة باعتبار تنزيل من سيوجد منزلة الموجود اكتفاء بوجود المأمور في علم الآمر. وله تعلقات ثلاثة: - تنجيزي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات التي سيوجد منها وما لا يوجد. - وصلوحي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الأمر والنهي قبل وجود المخاطبين. - وتنجيزي حادث عند وجودهم. قوله: (وهل يشترط ... إلخ): المعتمد أنَّه لا يشترط، وعليه فالأحكام قديمة. قوله: (وتنجيزي حادث عند وجودهم: هذا مبني على أنَّه لا يشترط في الخطاب وجود المخاطبين بالفعل.
The relations of speech: Speech indicates what was mentioned with continuous indication without interruption, eternally and forever. So He, exalted, is by it commander, prohibitor, informer. It is one in itself, and its multiplicity is only through the multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Therefore they divided it into command, prohibition, news, and interrogation. So from the aspect of its requiring an action or abandonment, it is called command and prohibition. His saying: (And thus they divided it): i.e., from the aspect of relations. His saying: (it is called command and prohibition): it is an orderly wrapping and spreading. And from the aspect of its relation to the affirmation of something for something, or its negation from it, it is called news. And is it condition for calling it that, like address, that there be addressees in actuality or not? There is disagreement, and upon it depends the disagreement regarding the rulings: are they created or eternal? by considering that which will exist as if it were existing, relying on the existence of the commanded thing in the knowledge of the commander. And it has three relations: an eternal executive relation (tanzizī qadīm) by virtue of its indication of the necessary, impossible, and contingent things that will exist and those that will not exist. And an eternal potential relation (ṣulūḥī qadīm) by virtue of its indication of command and prohibition before the existence of addressees. And a temporally originated executive relation (tanzizī ḥādith) at their existence. His saying: (And is it condition... etc.): The relied-upon view is that it is not a condition, and accordingly the rulings are eternal. His saying: (And a temporally originated executive relation at their existence): this is based on the view that condition in the address is not the actual existence of addressees.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage explains the relation of speech (كلام) as an attribute of God. The author states that speech indicates continuously without interruption, eternally and forever. God is described as commander, prohibitor, and informer through this one attribute. The multiplicity of speech (e.g., command, prohibition, news) is not due to multiplicity in the attribute itself but due to the multiplicity of its relations (تعلقات). Thus, scholars divided it into command (أمر), prohibition (نهي), news (خبر), and interrogation (استخبار). Specifically, when speech requires an action or abandonment, it is called command or prohibition. When it relates to affirming or negating something, it is called news. Then the author introduces a debate: is it necessary for speech (as address) to have addressees actually existing? Some say yes, others no. This debate affects whether the rulings (الأحكام) derived from speech are created (حادثة) or eternal (قديمة). The view that does not require actual addressees considers the rulings eternal, based on God's knowledge of future commands. The author then lists three types of relations of speech: 1) An eternal executive relation (تنجيزي قديم) indicating necessary, impossible, and contingent things that will exist or not exist. 2) An eternal potential relation (صلوحي قديم) indicating command and prohibition before the existence of addressees. 3) A temporally originated executive relation (تنجيزي حادث) that occurs when addressees actually exist. The margin note (قوله) clarifies that the relied-upon view is that the condition of actual addressees is not required, so the rulings are eternal. The third type is based on the view that does not require actual addressees.

Key Terms
تعلقات الكلام
أمر
نهي
خبر
استخبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is multiple in essence because it includes commands, prohibitions, etc.
Correction: Divine speech is one in essence; multiplicity is only in its relations to created things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for calling speech 'command' or 'prohibition'?
A) Its relation to affirmation or negation.
B) Its requirement of action or abstention.
C) Its need for addressees.
D) Its temporal occurrence.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is one eternal attribute, and its division into categories reflects different relations, not essential multiplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear commands and prohibitions from Allah, do you treat them as coming from one unchanging source, or do you see them as disconnected demands? How does realizing that they are all relations of one eternal speech change your obedience?
?
Your own speech often contradicts itself. How does the perfect unity of Allah's speech challenge you to be more consistent in your words?
?
If Allah's speech is one but has multiple relations, what does that imply about how you should approach the Qur'an? Do you fragment its messages or seek its unified intent?

The Debate on Addressees and the Three Relations of Speech
Al-Sanusī discusses the condition of addressees and derives three relations of speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate on whether divine speech requires actual addressees, and how this leads to distinguishing three types of relations: eternal executive, eternal potential, and temporally originated executive.

Core Idea
The existence of addressees affects whether rulings are considered eternal or created.

This passage addresses a key debate in kalām: Does God's speech, when considered as address (khiṭāb), require actually existing addressees? The answer determines whether the rulings (aḥkām) are eternal or temporal. Al-Sanusī then categorizes the relations of speech into three kinds based on this debate, providing a nuanced framework that preserves divine speech's eternity while accounting for temporal commands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debated condition
Read the conditional question: is the existence of actual addressees a condition for calling speech an address? Note the dependency: if yes, rulings are created; if no, they are eternal.
The author favors the view that does not require actual addressees.
2
Recognize the three relations
The passage lists three relations: eternal executive, eternal potential, and temporally originated executive. Understand the difference: executive relates to actual effects (like informing about what will exist), potential relates to capacity for command/prohibition before addressees exist, and temporally originated occurs when addressees exist.
The third is based on the view that actual addressees are not necessary.
3
Connect the debate to the three relations
The third relation (temporally originated executive) is built on the view that addressees are not required, allowing speech to be considered executive even when addressees are not yet present. The eternal potential relation covers the period before addressees.
The margin note clarifies the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلقات الكلام: والكلام يدلُّ على ما ذُكر دلالة مستمرة بلا انقطاع، أزلاً وأبداً، فهو تعالی به آمر ناءٍ مُخبر، فهو في نفسه واحد، وتكثُره إنَّما هو بتكثر التَّعلقات؛ كالعلم والقدرة، ولذا قسموه إلى أمر ونهي، وخبر واستخبار. - فمن حيث اقتضاؤه فعلاً أو تركاً يسمّى أمراً ونهياً. قوله: (ولذا قسموه): أي: من حيث التعلقات. قوله: (يسمى أمرا ونهيا): لفّ ونشر مرتب. - ومن حيث تعلقه بثبوت أمر لأمر، أو نفيه عنه، يسمى خبراً. وهل يشترط في تسميته بذلك كالخطاب وجود مخاطبين بالفعل أو لا؟ خلاف، وينبني عليه الخلاف في الأحكام، هل هي حادثة أو قديمة باعتبار تنزيل من سيوجد منزلة الموجود اكتفاء بوجود المأمور في علم الآمر. وله تعلقات ثلاثة: - تنجيزي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الواجبات والمستحيلات والجائزات التي سيوجد منها وما لا يوجد. - وصلوحي قديم باعتبار دلالته على الأمر والنهي قبل وجود المخاطبين. - وتنجيزي حادث عند وجودهم. قوله: (وهل يشترط ... إلخ): المعتمد أنَّه لا يشترط، وعليه فالأحكام قديمة. قوله: (وتنجيزي حادث عند وجودهم: هذا مبني على أنَّه لا يشترط في الخطاب وجود المخاطبين بالفعل.
The relations of speech: Speech indicates what was mentioned with continuous indication without interruption, eternally and forever. So He, exalted, is by it commander, prohibitor, informer. It is one in itself, and its multiplicity is only through the multiplicity of relations, like knowledge and power. Therefore they divided it into command, prohibition, news, and interrogation. So from the aspect of its requiring an action or abandonment, it is called command and prohibition. His saying: (And thus they divided it): i.e., from the aspect of relations. His saying: (it is called command and prohibition): it is an orderly wrapping and spreading. And from the aspect of its relation to the affirmation of something for something, or its negation from it, it is called news. And is it condition for calling it that, like address, that there be addressees in actuality or not? There is disagreement, and upon it depends the disagreement regarding the rulings: are they created or eternal? by considering that which will exist as if it were existing, relying on the existence of the commanded thing in the knowledge of the commander. And it has three relations: an eternal executive relation (tanzizī qadīm) by virtue of its indication of the necessary, impossible, and contingent things that will exist and those that will not exist. And an eternal potential relation (ṣulūḥī qadīm) by virtue of its indication of command and prohibition before the existence of addressees. And a temporally originated executive relation (tanzizī ḥādith) at their existence. His saying: (And is it condition... etc.): The relied-upon view is that it is not a condition, and accordingly the rulings are eternal. His saying: (And a temporally originated executive relation at their existence): this is based on the view that condition in the address is not the actual existence of addressees.

God is described as commander, prohibitor, and informer through this one attribute. The multiplicity of speech (e.g., command, prohibition, news) is not due to multiplicity in the attribute itself but due to the multiplicity of its relations (تعلقات). ?
If God's commands are eternal and do not depend on your existence, how does that change the urgency with which you obey a command first given centuries ago? Will you treat it as addressed to you today?
?
The 'temporally originated executive relation' means that the same eternal command becomes effective in your lifetime. When you hear a command from God, do you react as if it has always been directed at you, or do you find reasons to delay? What would your response need to look like to reflect that truth?
?
You believe God's speech is eternal, yet you often act as if His commands are optional until circumstances force you. What one command are you currently postponing, and what would it mean to treat it as already binding upon you from eternity?

Divine Attributes

Second Category: Power and Will – Refuting the Mu'tazila
Defines second category of attributes and refutes Mu'tazila on human actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's power and will encompass all contingent things, including human voluntary actions, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that humans independently create their own actions.

Core Idea
God's power and will extend to every contingent thing – including what we do.

This lesson addresses a central point of dispute between Sunni Islam and the Mu'tazila: the scope of God's power and will over human actions. The author affirms that both attributes relate to all contingent things, and he refutes the Mu'tazilite view that humans independently create their own voluntary actions, especially sins. The commentary unpacks the argument and its implications for taqwa.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: first a definition, then a polemic against the Mu'tazila.
The passage uses the matn's verse as a springboard for refutation.
2
Identify the two attributes and their object
The passage identifies power and will as the two attributes related to contingent things (mumkinat). Note that they do not relate to necessary or impossible things.
This is a key technical point in kalam.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claimed that God's power does not cover human voluntary actions and that humans create their own acts. The passage refutes this as a corrupt doctrine and links belief in God's universal power to taqwa.
The commentator stresses that rejecting this view makes one not a person of taqwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : ما يتعلق الممكنات، وهو صفتان أيضاً، بجميع القدرة والإرادة، وإليه أشار بقوله : وقدرة إرادة تعلقا بالممكنات)، لا بالواجبات ولا بالمستحيلات. وأشار بقوله : (كلّها ( يا أخا التقى) أي : يا أيُّها الملازم على التَّقوى، للردّ على المعتزلة القائلين بأنَّ قدرته تعالى لا تتعلَّق بأفعال العبد الاختيارية ، بل العبد مستقل بخَلْق فعله الاختياري، وإنَّ بعض أنَّ أفعاله الاختيارية كالمعاصي ليست بإرادة الله تعالى، بناءً على الإرادة تستلزم الأمر، أو هي عينه ولا ريب في أنَّه مذهب فاسد. ومن ثَمَّ أشرتُ بقولي: (أخا التقى إلى أنَّ من لم يعتقد ما قلنا فليس بتقي. قوله : (للرَّدِّ على المعتزلة) : وتقدَّم له بسط الرَّدِّ عليهم
The second category: that which relates to contingent things (mumkinat), and it is also two attributes: power and will in their entirety. And to it he (i.e., the author of the matn) alluded by his saying: 'And power and will relate to contingent things,' not to the necessary nor to the impossible. And he alluded by his saying: 'all of them, O brother of taqwa,' i.e., O you who adhere to taqwa, to refute the Mu'tazila who say that His power (Exalted is He) does not relate to the voluntary actions of the servant, but rather the servant is independent in creating his own voluntary action, and that some that his voluntary actions like sins are not by the will of God, based on the view that will necessitates command, or it is identical to it, and there is no doubt that this is a corrupt doctrine. And therefore I alluded by my saying: 'brother of taqwa' to the fact that whoever does not believe what we said is not a person of taqwa. His saying: (to refute the Mu'tazila): And the detailed refutation of them has preceded.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a polemic. It begins by defining the second category of divine attributes: those that relate to contingent things (mumkinat). This category comprises two attributes: absolute power (qudra) and will (irada). The author of the matn (Sanusi) indicates that these two attributes relate to all contingent things, not to necessary things (wajibat) nor impossible things (mustahilat). The commentator then explains that the phrase 'all of them' in the matn is used to refute the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila wrongly claimed that God's power does not relate to human voluntary actions; rather, humans independently create their own voluntary actions. Some Mu'tazila also held that sins are not willed by God, based on a flawed argument that will necessitates divine command or is identical to it. The commentator firmly declares this doctrine corrupt (fasid). He then states that his use of the address 'brother of taqwa' is meant to imply that whoever does not affirm that God's power and will encompass all contingent things, including human actions, is not a person of taqwa (God-consciousness). The commentator adds that a detailed refutation of the Mu'tazila has already been presented earlier in the work. This passage firmly upholds Sunni doctrine on divine omnipotence and will against Mu'tazilite rationalism.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
الممكنات
المعتزلة
أفعال العبد الاختيارية
التقوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila claim that God's power does not relate to human voluntary actions; humans independently create their own actions, and sins are not willed by God because will would necessitate command.
Correction: The passage affirms that God's power and will encompass all contingent things, including human voluntary actions and sins. The Mu'tazilite view is corrupt; denying this makes one lack taqwa.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila claim about God's power and human actions?
A) God's power relates to all human actions
B) God's power does not relate to voluntary human actions; humans create them independently
C) God's power relates only to involuntary actions
D) God's power is identical to His will

Key Takeaway
God's power and will encompass all contingent things, including human voluntary actions, affirming Sunni doctrine against Mu'tazilite rationalism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that it happened within God's will, or do you secretly think you are acting independently of His power? How does that belief change the way you seek forgiveness and rely on Him?
?
The commentator says that not affirming that God's power and will cover all contingent things makes you not a person of taqwa. Examine your own heart: are you fully convinced of God's absolute sovereignty over every aspect of your life, including your failures? If not, what would it take for you to surrender that reservation?
?
Consider one area of your life where you struggle to submit to God's will — perhaps a repeated sin, a habit, or a fear. Are you willing to acknowledge that even that struggle is under God's control, and that He alone can change it? What does that mean for your next step of faith?

The Two Eternal Relations of the Divine Will
Explains the two types of relation of the divine will: eternal actual and eternal potential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two eternal relations of the divine will: the actual specification that determines what will happen, and the potential specification that covers what is not.

Core Idea
The divine will has two eternal relations: one specifies what will be, the other covers what is not.

This lesson explores the nature of the divine will in Islamic theology. While both power and will relate to contingent things, the will has a unique role: it 'specifies' each thing with its particular existence. The passage distinguishes between two eternal relations: one that actually determines what will happen, and another that is potential. Understanding this distinction clarifies how God's eternal will relates to both what occurs and what does not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the technical terms and how the author defines the will's relation.
The passage is from a commentary on al-Kharida.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms 'takhsis' (specification), 'tanjizi' (actual eternal), and 'suluhi' (potential eternal). Recognize that 'tadhkira' is not present but these are central.
3
Distinguish the two relations
Note that the will has two eternal relations: tanjizi refers to the eternal specification of things exactly as they will come to be; suluhi refers to the will's relation to possibilities that never occur.
The passage only describes tanjizi in detail.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why it matters that the specification is eternal. This guards against the misconception that God's will changes over time.
Connect to the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعلق الإرادة : وهما وإن تعلقا بالممكن إلا أنَّ تعلُّق الإرادة به تعلُّق مخصوص إذ هي صفة تُخصِّص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، ولها تعلقان قديمان، تنجيزي وصلوحي : - فتخصيصها في الأزل الأشياء على الوجه الذي ستوجد عليه فيما لا يزال تنجيزي قديم . قوله : (ولها) أي : للإرادة
The relation of the will: And even though both (power and will) relate to the contingent, the relation of the will to it is a specific relation, for it is an attribute that specifies the contingent with some of what is possible for it. And it has two eternal relations: actual eternal (tanjizi) and potential eternal (suluhi). So its specification in pre-eternity of things in the manner they will come to exist in what is yet to come is an eternal actual (tanjizi qadim). His saying: (And it has) i.e., for the will.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory definition of the relation of the divine attribute of will (irada). It first notes that although both power and will relate to contingent things, the relation of will is special. Will is described as the attribute that 'specifies' (takhsis) a contingent thing with one of its possible states. The passage then states that the will has two eternal (qadim) relations: one called 'actual eternal' (tanjizi qadim) and the other 'potential eternal' (suluhi qadim). The actual eternal relation refers to the will's specification in pre-eternity of things exactly as they will come to exist in the future. The potential relation (not detailed here) presumably refers to the will's relation to possibilities that never occur. The commentary simply clarifies that 'it has' refers to the will. This passage refines the understanding of divine will by distinguishing its eternal aspect from temporal manifestation, affirming that God's will is eternal and its specifications are determined from eternity.

Key Terms

الإرادة
تخصيص
تنجيزي
صلوحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the will's specification occurs in time, as if God decides things as they happen.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the specification itself is eternal (tanjizi qadim), not temporal, even though the actualized things are temporal.

Checkpoint

A) It creates them
B) It specifies them with some of what is possible for them
C) It is identical to power
D) It is temporal

Key Takeaway
The divine will has two eternal relations: an actual one that specifies what will be from eternity, and a potential one that covers what is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The potential relation reminds you that God's will covers every 'what if' that never happened. Think of a worry you've carried about a future that didn't come. How does knowing that God's eternal will already encompasses both what is and what is not change how you face uncertainty?
?
You believe in an unchanging divine will—but does your daily life reflect trust in that reality? When you make plans, do you anchor them in the awareness that God's eternal specification has already determined their outcome? What would it look like to truly live that belief?
