Power and Will: Attributes That Encompass All Possibles
Definition of the second attribute category and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's power and will extend to all possible things, and why the Mu'tazilite denial of this is a corrupt doctrine.

Core Idea
Allah's power and will cover every possible thing, not just some.

This lesson continues the classification of divine attributes. Having covered the first category (attributes related to the divine essence), we now turn to the second category: attributes that relate to possible created things. The author identifies two such attributes—power and will—and insists that they apply to all possibles, a point he underscores by addressing the reader as 'possessor of piety.' This address is a subtle refutation of the Mu'tazila, who restrict God's power and will regarding human actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second category of attributes
Read the Arabic passage and find where the author introduces the second category. Note that it consists of two attributes: power and will.
2
Understand the scope: all possibles, not necessary or impossible
The passage explicitly states that these attributes relate to 'all possibles,' excluding the necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil). Reflect on why that is logically required.
3
Decode the phrase 'ya akha al-tuqa'
The author explains that this address is to one who adheres to piety, and it serves to refute the Mu'tazila. Understand what the Mu'tazila claim and why it is deemed corrupt.
4
Contrast with the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazila say God's power does not extend to human voluntary acts and that some acts are not willed by God. Compare this to the author's assertion that all possibles are subject to God's power and will.
This relates to the issue of free will and divine predestination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني : ما يتعلق الممكنات، وهو صفتان أيضاً، القدرة والإرادة، وإليه أشار بقوله : وقدرة إرادة تعلقا بالممكنات كلها يا أخا التقى، لا بالواجبات ولا بالمستحيلات. وأشار بقوله : (كلّها يا أخا التقى) أي : يا أيُّها الملازم على التَّقوى، للردّ على المعتزلة القائلين بأنَّ قدرته تعالى لا تتعلَّق بأفعال العبد الاختيارية، بل العبد مستقل بخَلْق فعله الاختياري، وإنَّ بعض أنَّ أفعاله الاختيارية كالمعاصي ليست بإرادة الله تعالى، بناءً على الإرادة تستلزم الأمر، أو هي عينه، ولا ريب في أنَّه مذهب فاسد. ومن ثَمَّ أشرتُ بقولي: (أخا التقى) إلى أنَّ من لم يعتقد ما قلنا فليس بتقي. قوله : (للرَّدِّ على المعتزلة) : وتقدَّم له بسط الرَّدِّ عليهم.
The second category: what relates to possibles, and it is two attributes as well: power and will. And he pointed to it by his saying: 'Power and will relate to possibles all of them, O possessor of piety,' not to the necessary things nor to the impossibles. And he indicated by his saying: 'all of them, O possessor of piety,' meaning: O you who adhere to piety, to refute the Mu'tazila who say that His power, exalted is He, does not relate to the voluntary acts of the servant, rather the servant is independent in creating his voluntary act, and that some of his voluntary acts like sins are not by the will of God, based on the premise that will necessitates command or is identical to it, and there is no doubt that this is a corrupt doctrine. Hence I indicated by my saying 'O possessor of piety' that whoever does not believe what we have said is not pious. His statement: 'to refute the Mu'tazila': and previously his refutation of them has been elaborated.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage defines the second category of divine attributes that relate to the possible (al-mumkināt), namely power (qudra) and will (irāda). The author quotes the poem to affirm that these two attributes relate to all possibles, not to the necessary (wājib) or impossible (mustaḥīl). He then explains the phrase 'yā akhā al-tuqā' (O possessor of piety) as an address to one who adheres to piety, and states that this is to refute the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila claim that God's power does not relate to the voluntary acts of the human servant; rather, the servant independently creates his own voluntary acts, and some acts like sins are not willed by God. Their argument is based on the idea that will entails command or is identical to it. The author declares this doctrine corrupt and implies that anyone who does not believe the correct view is not truly pious. A marginal note adds that the refutation of the Mu'tazila was detailed earlier.

Key Terms

الإرادة
المعتزلة
أخا التقى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power does not extend to human voluntary acts; humans independently create their own actions and some acts (like sins) are not willed by God.
Correction: God's power and will encompass all possibles, including all human voluntary acts. No act occurs outside His power or will; the Mu'tazilite view is corrupt.

Checkpoint
According to the author, which attributes belong to the second category that relate to possibles?
A) Knowledge and life
B) Power and will
C) Hearing and sight
D) Speech and will

Key Takeaway
Allah's power and will encompass every possible thing, and it is a sign of piety to affirm this against the Mu'tazilite restriction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you do, including your sins, happens only because Allah willed it? How does this change your view of your own failures and your need for His forgiveness?
?
The author links piety (taqwā) to affirming that Allah's power and will cover all possibles. If you have ever thought that your choices are outside Allah's control, what does that reveal about the depth of your taqwā? Will you repent and correct this belief today?
?
How would your daily du'ā change if you fully internalized that Allah’s power and will encompass every possible thing—including the circumstances you are facing right now? What would you ask Him for that you have been hesitant to ask?

The Will's Two Eternal Relations
Defines the will's specifying attribute and enumerates its two eternal relations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two eternal relations of the divine will — tanzizi (actualizing) and suluhi (potential) — and how they relate to possibles.

Core Idea
The will's eternal relation is twofold: actualizing and potential.

This lesson introduces the fundamental distinction in how the divine will relates to possible things. The will is a specifying attribute, and it has two eternal aspects: one that specifies things as they will be, and another that allows for them to be otherwise. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the balance between divine decree and possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining the will's specifying role
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how the author first defines the will as an attribute that specifies a possible with one of its permissible states.
This definition is the foundation for the two relations.
2
Identify the two relations
The passage explicitly states two relations: tanzizi and suluhi. Find the description of each and note that both are described as eternal (qadim).
3
Contrast tanzizi and suluhi
Tanzizi is the actual specification of things as they will exist, while suluhi is the potential for the thing to be otherwise. Both are eternal, but one is actual, the other potential.
This distinction prevents the misconception that God's will only has one mode.
4
Reflect on why both are necessary
Ask yourself why theologians needed to posit two relations. Consider how this preserves both divine decree and the contingency of creation.
This connects to broader issues in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهما وإن تعلقا بالممكن إلا أنَّ تعلُّق الإرادة به تعلُّق مخصوص، إذ هي صفة تُخصِّص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، ولها تعلقان: تنجيزي وصلوحي: فتخصيصها في الأزل الأشياء على الوجه الذي ستوجد عليه فيما لا يزال تنجيزي قديم. وصلوحُها لأن يكون على خلاف ما هو عليه صلوحي قديم. قيل : ولها تعلق ثالث، تنجيزي حادث، وهو: تخصيصها الشَّي بالفعل وقت وجوده على وفق التخصيص الأزليّ. قوله : (قيل : ولها تعلق ثالث تنجيزي حادث): إن قلت: إنَّ فيه تحصيل حاصل فما الحكمة في هذا التَّعلُّق؟ أجيب بأنَّ حكمته إظهاره للملائكة.
And although they relate to the possible, the relation of will to it is a specific relation, for it is an attribute that specifies the possible with part of what is possible for it. And it has two relations: actualizing (tanzizi) and potential (suluhi): its specification in pre-eternity of things in the manner they will come to exist in the future is an eternal actualizing. And its suitability for being contrary to what it is is an eternal potential. It is said: it has a third relation, a temporal actualizing, which is its specification of the thing by actual existence at the time of its existence in accordance with the eternal specification. His statement: 'It is said: it has a third relation, a temporal actualizing': If you say: that is a tautology, so what is the wisdom in this relation? It is answered: Its wisdom is to manifest it to the angels.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage examines the relation of the divine will (irāda) to possibles. The will is a specifying attribute that picks out one of the many possible states for a thing. It has two eternal relations: tanzīzī (actualizing) and ṣulūḥī (potential). The tanzīzī relation is the eternal specification of things exactly as they will occur in the future; the ṣulūḥī relation is the eternal potential for the thing to be other than it is. A third, temporal, tanzīzī relation is mentioned by some: the actual specification at the time of existence, which matches the eternal decree. The author then addresses an objection: if the temporal specification is the same as the eternal one, it is a tautology. The answer is that the wisdom of this third relation is to manifest the divine decree to the angels, who witness the actual occurrence.

Key Terms
تنجيزي
صلوحي
قديم
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The will's relation to possibles is singular and does not include potentiality.
Correction: The will has both an actualizing and a potential relation, both eternal, affirming both decree and contingency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the will do to possibles?
A) Creates them
B) Specifies them with part of what is possible
C) Necessitates them
D) Negates them

Key Takeaway
The divine will has two eternal relations to possibles: an actualizing specification of everything as it will occur, and a potential to have specified differently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will has eternally specified every detail of your life, what does that mean for how you view your past mistakes? Can you release regret knowing that each moment was part of His precise choice?
?
The potential relation shows that things could have been otherwise. Does this truth increase your gratitude for the specific circumstances God has chosen for you? How can you actively thank Him for the path you are on?
?
Do you live as if God's will is still undecided about your future, or do you trust that His actualizing relation has already determined it with wisdom? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to that eternal specification today?

The Third Temporal Relation of the Will
Presents a third temporal relation of the will and answers the objection of tautology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third, temporal relation of the divine will and the wisdom behind it, as well as the objection that it seems redundant.

Core Idea
The temporal manifestation of the will confirms the eternal decree and is witnessed by angels.

After establishing the two eternal relations, some scholars propose a third relation: a temporal actualizing that occurs at the moment of each thing's existence. This lesson explores this additional relation, the objection that it is a tautology, and the answer that it serves to manifest the decree to the angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage introducing the third relation
Read the Arabic passage. Note that it begins with 'قيل' (It is said), indicating this is a view held by some.
The third relation is described as tanzizi hadith (temporal actualizing).
2
Understand the objection of tautology
The passage raises an objection: if the temporal specification is identical to the eternal one, it is a tautology (tahsilu hasil). Reflect on why this might be seen as redundant.
3
Grasp the wisdom: manifestation to angels
The answer is that the wisdom of this third relation is to manifest the eternal decree to the angels, who witness the actual occurrence of events. This does not change the decree but makes it observable.
This preserves the necessity of the decree while providing a purpose for the temporal specification.
4
Reflect on why the third relation is not redundant
Consider how the manifestation to created beings justifies an additional aspect of the will's relation. The eternal decree itself is not affected, but its display serves a purpose.
This shows theological nuance in reconciling divine transcendence with creation's perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهما وإن تعلقا بالممكن إلا أنَّ تعلُّق الإرادة به تعلُّق مخصوص، إذ هي صفة تُخصِّص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، ولها تعلقان: تنجيزي وصلوحي: فتخصيصها في الأزل الأشياء على الوجه الذي ستوجد عليه فيما لا يزال تنجيزي قديم. وصلوحُها لأن يكون على خلاف ما هو عليه صلوحي قديم. قيل : ولها تعلق ثالث، تنجيزي حادث، وهو: تخصيصها الشَّي بالفعل وقت وجوده على وفق التخصيص الأزليّ. قوله : (قيل : ولها تعلق ثالث تنجيزي حادث): إن قلت: إنَّ فيه تحصيل حاصل فما الحكمة في هذا التَّعلُّق؟ أجيب بأنَّ حكمته إظهاره للملائكة.
And although they relate to the possible, the relation of will to it is a specific relation, for it is an attribute that specifies the possible with part of what is possible for it. And it has two relations: actualizing (tanzizi) and potential (suluhi): its specification in pre-eternity of things in the manner they will come to exist in the future is an eternal actualizing. And its suitability for being contrary to what it is is an eternal potential. It is said: it has a third relation, a temporal actualizing, which is its specification of the thing by actual existence at the time of its existence in accordance with the eternal specification. His statement: 'It is said: it has a third relation, a temporal actualizing': If you say: that is a tautology, so what is the wisdom in this relation? It is answered: Its wisdom is to manifest it to the angels.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage examines the relation of the divine will (irāda) to possibles. The will is a specifying attribute that picks out one of the many possible states for a thing. It has two eternal relations: tanzīzī (actualizing) and ṣulūḥī (potential). The tanzīzī relation is the eternal specification of things exactly as they will occur in the future; the ṣulūḥī relation is the eternal potential for the thing to be other than it is. A third, temporal, tanzīzī relation is mentioned by some: the actual specification at the time of existence, which matches the eternal decree. The author then addresses an objection: if the temporal specification is the same as the eternal one, it is a tautology. The answer is that the wisdom of this third relation is to manifest the divine decree to the angels, who witness the actual occurrence.

Misconception: The third temporal relation is redundant and serves no purpose.
Correction: It is not redundant; it manifests the eternal decree to created beings like angels.

?

Power's Two Relations and the Ordering of Attributes
Explains power's two relations and the ordering of power, will, and knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two relations of divine power—eternal potential and temporal actualizing—and the logical dependence of power on will, and will on knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine power depends on will, which depends on knowledge; its action has an eternal potential and temporal actualization.

This passage delves into how divine power relates to creation. It distinguishes between a timeless capacity and the actual act of bringing things into existence, all in accordance with will and knowledge. The author then clarifies the hierarchical order among these attributes, a key point in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations of power
Read the first part of the passage and note the distinction between the eternal potential relation (ṣulūḥī qadīm) and the temporal actualizing relation (tanzīzī ḥādith). Power relates to possibles either by bringing into existence or annihilating.
The temporal relation is identified with the attributes of acts like creation and provision.
2
Understand the attributes of acts
The passage lists creation, provision, giving life, and causing death as examples of the temporal relation. These are called attributes of acts (ṣifāt al-af‘āl).
3
Trace the ordering of power, will, and knowledge
The author states that the relations of power, will, and knowledge are ordered. Note the logical dependence: power depends on will, and will depends on knowledge.
This is a logical order, not temporal priority.
4
Apply the ordering to divine action
Follow the examples: what God knows will be, He wills it and brings it about; what He knows will not be, He does not will it, so it does not exist even if commanded (like the faith of a persistent disbeliever).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا تعلُّق القدرة به فتَعلُّق إيجاد أو إعدام على طبق الإرادة، ولها تعلقان : صلوحي قديم، وتنجيزي حادث، وهذا التَّعلُّق الحادث هو المعبّر عنه بالخلق والرّزق والإحياء والإماتة، المسماة عندنا بصفات الأفعال، وسيأتي له زيادة إيضاح في قسم الجائز. واعلم : أنَّ تعلق القدرة والإرادة والعلم مترتِّب، فتَعلُّق القدرة تابع لتعلُّق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلُّق العلم، فلا يُوجد شيئاً أو يعدمه إلا إذا أراده، ولا يُريده إلا إذا علمه، فما علم أنه يكون أراد كونه، ثمَّ أبرزه على طبق الإرادة وما عَلِم أنَّه لا يكون فلم يرد كونه، فلم يوجد وإن أمر به؛ كالإيمان ممَّن عَلِم الله أنَّه يستمر على الكفر حتَّى الموت. وإنَّما لم تتعلَّق القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل؛ لأنَّهما لما... قوله : (مترتب) : أي : في التَّعُقُل فقط بالنظر لتعلق القدرة الحادثة مع...
As for the relation of power to it: it is a relation of bringing into existence or annihilating in accordance with will. And it has two relations: eternal potential and temporal actualizing. And this temporal relation is what is expressed as creation, provision, giving life, and causing death, which are called by us the attributes of acts, and further clarification will come in the section on the contingent. Know that the relations of power, will, and knowledge are ordered: the relation of power is dependent on the relation of will, and the relation of will is dependent on the relation of knowledge. So He does not bring anything into existence or annihilate it unless He wills it, and He does not will it unless He knows it. So what He knows will be, He wills its existence, then He brings it forth in accordance with will. And what He knows will not be, He does not will its existence, so it does not exist even if He commands it; like faith from one whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death. And the reason that power and will do not relate to the necessary and impossible is because they are ... His statement: 'ordered' means in intellect only in consideration of the temporal power with ...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains the relation of divine power (qudra) to possibles. Power's relation is either bringing into existence (ījād) or annihilating (i‘dām) in accordance with the will. Power has two relations: an eternal potential (ṣulūḥī qadīm) and a temporal actualizing (tanzīzī ḥādith). The temporal relation is referred to as the attributes of acts (ṣifāt al-af‘āl): creation, provision, giving life, and causing death. The author then states that the relations of power, will, and knowledge are ordered (mutarattib): power's relation depends on will, will on knowledge. God does not create or annihilate unless He wills, and He does not will unless He knows. What He knows will occur, He wills its existence and brings it about; what He knows will not occur, He does not will its existence, so it does not exist even if He commands it—like the faith of someone whom God knows will die a disbeliever. The text begins to explain why power and will do not relate to the necessary (wājib) and impossible (mustaḥīl), but cuts off. A marginal note clarifies that 'ordered' (mutarattib) refers to intellectual order only, considering the temporal power with...

Misconception: The ordering among power, will, and knowledge implies temporal sequence or that one attribute acts before the other.
Correction: The author notes that the ordering is 'in intellect only' (fī al-ta‘aqqul faqaṭ), meaning it is a logical dependence, not a temporal priority. All attributes are eternal, but the act of power follows the decree of will and knowledge in the order of explanation.

Attachments of Divine Power
Sawi defines the attachments of power and explains the wisdom of the third temporal attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two attachments of divine power (eternal potentiality and temporal actualization) and the wisdom behind the third temporal attachment.

Core Idea
Divine power's temporal attachment is not redundant—it manifests God's decree to angels.

In this passage, Sawi clarifies the nature of divine power's attachments. He explains that power has an eternal potentiality and a temporal actualization, and addresses a key objection: why bother with a third attachment if it only realizes what is already decreed? The answer reveals a profound wisdom about creation's purpose.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sawi defines the attachments sequentially.
Pay attention to the phrases 'صلوحي قديم' and 'تنجيزي حادث'.
2
Identify the two (or three) attachments
The passage first mentions an eternal potentiality (ṣalūḥī qadīm) and then reports a third temporal attachment (tanjīzī ḥādith). It later says power has two attachments: the eternal potentiality and the temporal actualization.
Clarify that the 'third' attachment is the same as the temporal actualization, but the passage counts differently.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection is that the third attachment seems to realize something already realized (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). The response gives the wisdom: manifesting it to the angels.
This shows the theological purpose of temporal events: they are not redundant but have a cosmic audience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصلوحُها لأن يكون على خلاف ما هو عليه صلوحي قديم. قيل : ولها تعلق ثالث، تنجيزي حادث، وهو: تخصيصها الشَّي بالفعل وقت وجوده على وفق التخصيص الأزليّ. تعلق القدرة : وأمَّا تعلُّق القدرة به : فتَعلُّق إيجاد أو إعدام على طبق الإرادة، ولها تعلقان : صلوحي قديم، وتنجيزي حادث، وهذا التعلُّق الحادث هو المعبّر عنه بالخلق والرّزق والإحياء والإماتة، المسماة عندنا وسيأتي له زيادة إيضاح في قسم بصفات الأفعال، فهي - الجائز . قوله : (قيل : ولها تعلق ثالث تنجيزي حادث): إن قلت: إنَّ فيه تحصيل حاصل فما الحكمة في هذا التَّعلُّق؟ جيب بأنَّ حكمته إظهاره للملائكة .
And its potentiality to be contrary to what it is is an eternal potentiality. It was said: And it has a third attachment, a temporal actualizer, which is: its specifying the thing with the act at the time of its existence according to the eternal specification. The attachment of power: As for the attachment of power to it: it is an attachment of bringing into existence or annihilating in accordance with will, and it has two attachments: an eternal potentiality and a temporal actualization. And this temporal attachment is what is expressed as creation, provision, giving life, and causing death, named among us, and later there will come further clarification in the section on attributes of acts. So they are the possible. His statement: (It was said: It has a third temporal actualizing attachment): If you say: In it there is a realization of something already realized, so what is the wisdom in this attachment? The answer: Its wisdom is to manifest it to the angels.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the attachments of divine power (qudra) and addresses an objection about a third temporal attachment. It begins by explaining that the potentiality of power to produce something different from what currently exists is an eternal potentiality (salūḥī qadīm). Then it reports a statement (qīla) that power has a third attachment, a temporal actualization (tanjīzī ḥādith), which consists of specifying the thing with the act at the moment of its existence in accordance with the eternal specification. Next, it formally defines the attachment of power: it is an attachment of bringing into existence (ījād) or annihilating (iʿdām) in accordance with the divine will. Power has two attachments: an eternal potentiality (salūḥī qadīm) and a temporal actualization (tanjīzī ḥādith). This temporal attachment is what is referred to as creation (khalq), provision (rizq), giving life (iḥyāʾ), and causing death (imāta). The passage notes that further clarification will come later in the section on attributes of acts. The phrase 'fa-hiya al-jāʾiz' likely means that these acts are possible (jāʾiz) relative to God's power. The marginal gloss (qawluhu) then expands on the third attachment: an objector might say that this attachment is a realization of something already realized (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) and ask for its wisdom. The answer is that its wisdom is to manifest these acts to the angels, i.e., to show them the realization of God's eternal decree in time.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third temporal attachment of divine power is redundant because it merely realizes what is already eternally decreed.
Correction: The passage teaches that this attachment is not redundant; its wisdom is to manifest God's eternal decree to the angels, giving it a purpose beyond mere realization.

Checkpoint

The Order of Power, Will, and Knowledge
Sawi explains the sequential dependence of power on will and will on knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sequential dependence of God's power on His will, and His will on His knowledge, and how this ordering is understood as primarily intellectual rather than temporal.

Core Idea
God's power follows His will, and His will follows His knowledge — an ordered relationship of logical dependence.

This lesson explores a subtle point in Islamic theology regarding the relationship among God's attributes of power, will, and knowledge. Al-Sawi explains how their attachments to created things are ordered, and clarifies that this ordering is primarily an intellectual necessity, not a temporal sequence. Understanding this distinction prevents misconceptions about divine action.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: قدرة, إرادة, علم, and the concept of ترتب (ordering).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the chain of dependence
The passage states that the attachment of power depends on will, and will on knowledge. Locate this chain and understand the examples given (e.g., faith of the disbeliever).
3
Distinguish intellectual vs external ordering
The marginal gloss explains that the ordering is intellectual when considering temporal attributes, but external when considering the eternal aspect. Reflect on the difference and why it matters.
This prevents thinking God's actions are temporally ordered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ تعلق القدرة والإرادة والعلم مترتِّب، فتَعلُّق القدرة تابع لتعلُّق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلُّق العلم، فلا يُوجد شيئاً أو يعدمه إلا إذا أراده، ولا يُريده إلا إذا علمه، فما علم أنه يكون أراد كونه ، ثمَّ أبرزه على طبق الإرادة وما عَلِم أنَّه لا يكون فلم يرد كونه، فلم يوجد وإن أمر به ؛ كالإيمان ممَّن عَلِم الله أنَّه يستمر على الكفر حتَّى الموت. قوله : (مترتب) : أي : في التَّعقُل فقط بالنظر لتعلق القدرة الحادثة مع تعلق الإرادة التنجيزي الحادث، ولتعلُّق الإرادة القديم مع تعلق العلم، وأمَّا بالنَّظر إلى تعلّق القدرة الحادث مع تعلق الإرادة التنجيزي القديم، وكذا تعلق الإرادة التنجيزي الحادث مع تعلق العلم؛ فهو ترتب خارجي، كترتب الحادث على القديم في الخارج.
And know that the attachments of power, will, and knowledge are ordered. For the attachment of power is dependent on the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is dependent on the attachment of knowledge. So He does not bring something into existence or annihilate it unless He willed it, and He does not will it unless He knew it. So what He knows that it will be, He willed its existence, then He brings it forth in accordance with will. And what He knows that it will not be, He did not will its existence, so it does not exist even if He commands it; like faith from one whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death. His statement: (ordered) means: in intellection only, considering the attachment of the temporal power with the attachment of the temporal actualizing will, and the attachment of the eternal will with the attachment of knowledge. However, considering the attachment of the temporal power with the attachment of the eternal actualizing will, and likewise the attachment of the temporal actualizing will with the attachment of knowledge, it is an external ordering, like the ordering of the temporal event upon the eternal in external existence.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage explains the ordered relationship (tarattub) among God's attributes of power (qudra), will (irāda), and knowledge (ʿilm) in terms of their attachments to objects. It states that the attachment of power is dependent on the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is dependent on the attachment of knowledge. This means that God only creates or destroys something if He wills it, and He only wills it if He knows it. Examples are given: what God knows will happen, He wills and then brings into existence; what He knows will not happen, He does not will, so it does not occur even if He commands it, like the faith of someone He knows will die as an unbeliever. The marginal gloss clarifies that this ordering is primarily intellectual (fī al-taʿaqqul faqaṭ) when considering the temporal power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) with the temporal actualizing will (al-irāda al-tanjīziyya al-ḥāditha), and the eternal will with knowledge. However, when considering the temporal power with the eternal actualizing will, or the temporal actualizing will with knowledge, the ordering is external (khārijī), like the temporal event following the eternal decree in external existence.

Key Terms

القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
التعلق التنجيزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the order of power, will, and knowledge is always a temporal sequence in God's action.
Correction: The ordering is primarily intellectual (logical dependence) when considering temporal attributes; it is external (like cause and effect) only in specific contexts, such as temporal power with eternal will.

Checkpoint

B) When considering temporal power with eternal actualizing will
C) When considering temporal actualizing will with knowledge
D) In all cases

Key Takeaway
The attachments of God's power, will, and knowledge are ordered by logical dependence, not by time; intellectual order applies to temporal aspects, while external order applies between temporal and eternal aspects.

Why Divine Power and Will Do Not Attach to the Necessary and Impossible
Al-Sawi proves that power and will do not attach to the necessary and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power and will cannot apply to the necessary existent (wājib) and the impossible (mustaḥīl), based on the nature of effect and the logical consequences of such application.

Core Idea
God's power cannot create what already exists or what cannot exist.

This passage from Al-Sawi's commentary explains a crucial limitation of divine attributes: power and will are attributes of effect, requiring the effect to come into existence after non-existence. Since the necessary existent cannot not exist, and the impossible cannot exist, they fall outside the scope of these attributes. The argument uses two absurd possibilities: realizing what is already realized (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) and reversing realities (qalb al-ḥaqāʾiq). Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of effect
Read the passage's opening: power and will are attributes of effect, and an effect must come into existence after non-existence. This is the key premise.
This premise makes clear that what never had non-existence (the necessary) or never can have existence (the impossible) cannot be an effect.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice the two categories: the necessary (wājib) which does not accept non-existence, and the impossible (mustaḥīl) which does not accept existence. Both are beyond the scope of power and will.
3
Examine the two absurd consequences
The gloss breaks down two cases: if power/will attached to bringing the necessary into existence (already exists) → taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil; if attached to annihilating the necessary or creating the impossible → qalb al-ḥaqāʾiq. Both are incoherent.
These consequences show why attachment is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما لم تتعلَّق القدرة والإرادة بالواجب والمستحيل ؛ لأنَّهما لما كانا صفتي تأثير، ومن لازم الأثر وجوده بعد عدم، لزم أنَّ ما لم يقبل العدم أصلاً، وهو الواجب، وما لم يقبل الوجود أصلاً، وهو المستحيل، لم يصح أن يكون أثراً لهما وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل وقلب الحقائق بصيرورة الواجب أو المستحيل جائزاً، وهو تهافت لا يعقل؛ فالكمال المطلق في عدم تعلُّقهما بالواجب والمستحيل لما علمت والنَّقصُ الذي ما بعده نَقصُ تعلُّقهما بهما المؤدِّي ذلك إلى إعدامهما أنفسهما وإعدام الذات العليَّة وإيجاد الشّريك والعجز تمسك به بعض أهل الاختلال. قوله : ( وإلا لزام تحصيل الحاصل ... إلخ) : أي : إن تعلقت بإيجاد الواجب، أو بإعدام المستحيل. وقوله : ( وقلب الحقائق) : أي : إن تعلقت بإعدام الواجب، أو بإيجاد المستحيل .
And power and will did not attach to the necessary and the impossible because since they are both attributes of effect, and from the necessary consequences of an effect is its existence after non-existence, it follows that what does not accept non-existence at all, which is the necessary, and what does not accept existence at all, which is the impossible, cannot be an effect for them. Otherwise it would necessitate the realization of what is already realized and the reversal of realities, by the necessary or the impossible becoming possible, which is an incoherence not understood. So absolute perfection lies in their not attaching to the necessary and the impossible, as you have learned. And the deficiency beyond which there is no deficiency is their attaching to them, leading to the annihilation of themselves, the annihilation of the Supreme Essence, the bringing into existence of a partner, and impotence and ignorance, which some people of deviation have adhered to. His statement: (And otherwise it would necessitate the realization of what is already realized, etc.) means: if it attaches to bringing the necessary into existence or annihilating the impossible. And his statement: (and the reversal of realities) means: if it attaches to annihilating the necessary or bringing the impossible into existence.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage gives the proof for why divine power (qudra) and will (irāda) do not attach to the necessary existent (wājib) or the impossible (mustaḥīl). The argument: power and will are attributes of effect (ṣifatā taʾthīr), and an effect necessarily exists after non-existence. The necessary existent cannot admit non-existence, and the impossible cannot admit existence. Therefore, they cannot be effects of power and will. If power or will were to attach to them, it would lead to either taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil (realizing what is already realized, e.g., creating the necessary which already exists) or qalb al-ḥaqāʾiq (reversal of realities, e.g., annihilating the necessary, which is impossible). Such consequences are incoherent. Thus, absolute perfection (al-kamāl al-muṭlaq) requires that power and will not attach to the necessary and impossible. Attaching them would lead to absurdities like annihilating God's essence or creating a partner. Some misguided people have adhered to these absurd views. The marginal gloss clarifies the two cases: taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil occurs if power attaches to bringing the necessary into existence (which already exists) or annihilating the impossible (which does not exist); qalb al-ḥaqāʾiq occurs if it attaches to annihilating the necessary or bringing the impossible into existence.

Key Terms
الواجب
المستحيل
القدرة
الإرادة
تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's omnipotence means He can do absolutely anything, including making the necessary existent non-existent or the impossible possible.
Correction: This passage shows that logical impossibilities (like bringing about that which is already realized or reversing logical categories) are not objects of divine power. Omnipotence applies only to possible things.

Checkpoint
What does 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' refer to in the context of this passage?
A) Creating the impossible
B) Annihilating the necessary
C) Bringing the necessary into existence (which already exists)
D) Bringing the impossible into existence

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will are limited to the realm of possible things; they cannot apply to the necessary or the impossible, as such application would entail logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says absolute perfection lies in not attaching to the necessary and impossible. How does this affect your view of God's attributes? Are you tempted to think of God as 'able to do anything' without considering logical coherence? What does this lesson demand you change in your understanding of God's power and will today?

Hearing and Sight as Divine Attributes of Disclosure
Sawi introduces hearing and sight as divine attributes that encompass all existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that hearing and sight are divine attributes that encompass all existents through an attachment of disclosure.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight disclose all existents without physical organs.

In this lesson, we encounter the third category of divine attributes that have attachment: hearing and sight. Ahmad al-Sawi explains that these two attributes attach to all existents, not just some, with a unique attachment called 'attachment of disclosure' (ta'alluq inkishāf). This helps us understand that God's knowledge of all things is direct and immediate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting that it is incomplete. Then read the literal translation to understand the core statements.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have the beginning of the point.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that hearing and sight are two attributes that attach 'to all existents' (bi-jamīʿ al-mawjūdāt) with an attachment of disclosure. This means they encompass everything.
This is a key distinction from human senses which are limited.
3
Understand 'attachment of disclosure'
The term ta'alluq inkishāf indicates that God's hearing and sight are not like ours; they are a direct disclosure of all things to Him without physical means.
This concept avoids anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the incomplete text
Notice that the passage cuts off at 'to all...' (bikulli). The full statement would likely complete the idea that hearing and sight attach to all existents whatsoever.
The commentary continues beyond this missing part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من القسم الثالث من الصفات التي لها تعلق والقسم الثالث ما يتعلق صفتان أيضاً، بجميع الموجودات، وهو السمع والبصر، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( واجزم) أيُّها المكلَّف (بأنَّ سمعه) تعالى ( والبصرا) الألف للإطلاق، (تعلقا) معاً تعلُّق انكشاف، (بكلِّ
From the third category of attributes that have attachment. And the third category: there are two attributes that attach as well, to all existents, and they are hearing and sight. And to this he pointed by his saying: (And be certain) O obligated one (that His hearing) exalted (and sight) — the alif is for absolute form — (attached) together an attachment of disclosure (to all ...)
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the third category of divine attributes that have attachments: hearing (samʿ) and sight (baṣar). It states that these two attributes also attach to all existents (bi-jamīʿ al-mawjūdāt). The author (the original text) is quoted as saying: 'And be certain, O obligated one, that His hearing and sight attached together an attachment of disclosure (taʿalluq inkishāf) to all...' The passage is incomplete, ending at 'bikulli' (to all). The marginal note explains that the alif after 'basarā' is for absolute form (al-alf li-l-iṭlāq). This lesson introduces the idea that hearing and sight are attributes of God that encompass everything in a way that discloses them to Him.

Key Terms

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's hearing and sight are similar to human senses, requiring ears and eyes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine hearing and sight are an 'attachment of disclosure' — a direct knowledge of all existents without physical organs.

Why Divine Power and Will Cannot Affect the Necessary or Impossible
Al-Sawi argues why divine power and will cannot relate to the Necessary or Impossible being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of power and will cannot relate to the Necessary Being or the Impossible, based on the logical nature of effects.

Core Idea
Divine power only applies to the contingent, not the necessary or impossible.

This passage from Al-Sawi provides a rigorous argument that God's power and will are not unlimited in the sense of being able to do anything whatsoever. Rather, they are limited to the realm of the contingent. The argument is based on the definition of an effect as something that comes into existence after non-existence. Since the Necessary Being (God) cannot cease to exist and the Impossible cannot come to exist, these attributes cannot produce an effect on them. This clarifies an important aspect of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the first part: 'They are two attributes of effect, and from the necessary concomitant of an effect is its existence after non-existence.' This means power and will produce effects that come into being from non-being.
This is the foundational logical rule.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage identifies 'that which does not accept non-existence at all' (the Necessary) and 'that which does not accept existence at all' (the Impossible). These cannot be objects of these attributes.
The Necessary is eternal, the Impossible never exists.
3
Follow the logical consequence
If these attributes were to relate to the Necessary or Impossible, it would lead to absurdities like 'the acquisition of the already obtained' and 'the inversion of realities' (e.g., making the Necessary possible).
These are logical contradictions.
4
Recognize the conclusion and warning
The author states that it is absolute perfection for the attributes not to relate to them, and any claim otherwise leads to deficiency and even heresy. He condemns those who hold this view.
This safeguards divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانا صفتي تأثير، ومن لازم الأثر وجوده بعد عدم، لزم أنَّ ما لم يقبل العدم أصلاً، وهو الواجب، وما لم يقبل الوجود أصلاً، وهو المستحيل، لم يصح أن يكون أثراً لهما وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل وقلب الحقائق بصيرورة الواجب أو المستحيل جائزاً، وهو تهافت لا يعقل؛ فالكمال المطلق في عدم تعلُّقهما بالواجب والمستحيل لما علمت والنَّقصُ الذي ما بعده نَقصُ تعلُّقهما بهما المؤدِّي ذلك إلى إعدامهما أنفسهما وإعدام الذات العليَّة وإيجاد الشّريك والعجز تمسك به بعض أهل الاختلال. والجهل، نعوذ بالله الضَّلال الذي تمسك من
They are two attributes of effect, and from the necessary concomitant of an effect is its existence after non-existence. So it follows that that which does not accept non-existence at all—which is the Necessary—and that which does not accept existence at all—which is the Impossible—cannot be an effect of them. Otherwise it would necessitate the acquisition of the already obtained and the inversion of realities by making the Necessary or the Impossible possible, and that is an incoherence that is not rational. So absolute perfection lies in their not relating to the Necessary and the Impossible as you have learned, and the deficiency beyond which there is no deficiency is their relating to them, leading to the annihilation of themselves and the annihilation of the supreme Essence and the bringing into existence of a partner and impotence. Some of those afflicted with disorder have clung to this. And ignorance—we seek refuge in Allah from the misguidance which clings from.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the divine attributes of power and will (referred to as 'two attributes of effect') cannot relate to the Necessary Being (God) or to the Impossible. The argument: these attributes produce an effect, which by definition comes into existence after non-existence. Therefore, they require a subject that can receive existence after non-existence, i.e., a contingent being. The Necessary Being cannot cease to exist, and the Impossible cannot come to exist. If these attributes were to relate to them, it would lead to absurdities such as 'the acquisition of the already obtained' (making an existent again) or 'the inversion of realities' (making the Necessary become possible). This is incoherent. The author then states that it is absolute perfection for these attributes not to relate to the Necessary or Impossible, and it is the greatest deficiency for them to so relate, as that would imply the destruction of God and the existence of a partner. He notes that some misguided people have held this erroneous view, and he condemns such ignorance.

Key Terms
صفتي تأثير
الواجب
المستحيل
تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's power is absolutely unlimited and can make the impossible possible (e.g., create another god or change His own nature).
Correction: The passage shows that power and will are defined as producing existence after non-existence, so they cannot apply to what is eternal (Necessary) or non-existent (Impossible). Doing so would entail logical contradictions and undermine divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
Why can divine power and will not affect the Necessary or the Impossible?
A) Because the Necessary and Impossible do not exist.
B) Because power and will produce existence after non-existence, and the Necessary cannot cease to exist and the Impossible cannot come to exist.
C) Because God is above any attributes.
D) Because power and will are contingent themselves.

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will are limited to contingent beings; they cannot relate to the Necessary or Impossible, preserving God's transcendence and logical consistency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself wishing that God would overrule the logical necessities of your life—like the inevitability of death, the consequences of sin, or the fixed laws of nature? How does this lesson challenge that wish?
?
When you ask Allah for something impossible by His normal laws, do you stop to reflect that true perfection lies in His not granting it? How does knowing this change what you ask for and how you trust His wisdom?
?
This passage says that if divine power could affect the impossible, it would 'annihilate the supreme Essence.' In what subtle ways might your own thoughts about God's power actually be reducing His majesty rather than magnifying it? What will you consciously correct?

Divine Hearing and Sight: Nature and Relation
Al-Sawi defines divine hearing and sight and their relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's hearing and sight are two distinct attributes that fully disclose all existents, differing from knowledge and from each other.

Core Idea
Allah’s hearing and sight reveal everything, but their disclosure is distinct from knowledge.

This lesson introduces the third category of divine attributes: Hearing (sam‘) and Sight (baṣar). Al-Sawi explains that these two attributes relate to all existents—both eternal and contingent—through a special relation of disclosure (inkishāf). He also clarifies how this disclosure differs from that of knowledge (ʿilm) and how each attribute’s disclosure differs from the other.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the terms 'tā‘alluq inkishāf' and 'al-inkishāf bi-l-ʿilm'.
The opening phrase 'al-qism al-thālith' sets the context.
2
Identify the two attributes
Note that hearing and sight are treated as a pair. The author says they relate 'bi-jamīʿ al-mawjūdāt' (to all existents). Contrast this with human senses.
The phrase 'tā‘alluqā ma‘an tā‘alluq inkishāf' is key.
3
Understand disclosure versus knowledge
The passage states that disclosure via hearing/sight differs from disclosure via knowledge. Ask yourself: why might this distinction matter for understanding divine attributes?
This distinction preserves the uniqueness of each attribute.
4
Note the scope of their objects
The objects of hearing and sight are more specific than the object of knowledge. Knowledge encompasses everything, while hearing and sight cover all but with a different mode.
This refines the 'comprehensiveness' claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثالث من الصفات التي لها تعلق صفتان أيضاً، بجميع الموجودات، وهو السمع والبصر، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( واجزم) أيُّها المكلَّف (بأنَّ سمعه) تعالى ( والبصرا) الألف للإطلاق، (تعلقا) معاً تعلُّق انكشاف، (بكلِّ موجود يُرى بالبناء للمجهول ؛ أي: يعلم؛ أي: معلوم له تعالى قديماً كان كذاته وصفاته، أو حادثاً كذوات المخلوقين وصفاتهم. والانكشاف بهما يغاير الانكشاف بالعلم، وكذا الانكشاف بكلِّ منهما يغاير الانكشاف بالأخرى. ومتعلقهما أخصُّ من متعلَّق العلم، فيسمع ويرى سبحانه الذوات والصفات، كانت من قبيل الأصوات أو من غيرها، فسمعه وبصره تعالى يخالفان سمعنا وبصرنا في التَّعلُّق؛ لأنَّ سمعنا إنَّما يتعلَّق عادةً ببعض الموجودات، وهي الأصوات، بشرط عدم البعد جداً، وبصرنا إِنَّما يتعلَّق عادةً ببعض الموجودات، وهي الأجسام وألوانها، في جهة مخصوصة على وجه مخصوص . قوله : (لأن سمعنا إنَّما يتعلَّق عادة ... إلخ) : أي : ومن غير العادة قد يتعلق سمعنا بغير الأصوات؛ كسماع موسى لكلام الله القديم الذي ليس بحرف ولا صوت، وكسماعنا كلام ربِّ العالمين في الجنّة. قوله : (وهي الأصوات): الضَّمير لبعض الموجودات، وأنث الضَّمير لاكتساب المضاف التأنيث من المضاف إليه . قوله : (وبصرنا إنَّما يتعلَّق عادة ببعض الموجودات) : بشرط المقابلة واتصال الأشعة، وقد تخرق العادة كما في رؤية وجه الله الكريم. قوله : (وهي الأجسام جمع جسم، وهو ما تركب من جوهرين فردين فأكثر، وهو المتحيّز القابل للقسمة، وقضيَّته أنَّ الجوهر الفرد لا يرى، وهو كذلك بحسب العادة، وما ذكره الشَّرح من أنَّ المرئي هو ...
The third category of attributes that have relation: also two attributes, to all existents, which are Hearing and Sight. And to it he indicated by his saying: 'And be certain, O responsible one, that His Hearing and Sight—the alif is for release—are related together with a relation of disclosure, to every existing thing which is seen (built for the passive, i.e., known, i.e., known to Him, whether eternal like His Essence and attributes, or contingent like the essences of creatures and their attributes). And the disclosure by them is different from disclosure by knowledge, and likewise disclosure by each of them is different from disclosure by the other. And their objects are more specific than the object of knowledge; so He hears and sees, glory be to Him, essences and attributes, whether they be of the type of sounds or otherwise. So His Hearing and Sight, exalted is He, differ from our hearing and sight in relation, because our hearing is usually related only to some existents, namely sounds, provided they are not too distant, and our sight is usually related only to some existents, namely bodies and their colors, in a specific direction and in a specific manner.' His saying: 'Because our hearing is usually related... etc.' means: And outside the ordinary course our hearing may relate to other than sounds, like Moses' hearing of the eternal speech of God which is not a letter or sound, and like our hearing of the speech of the Lord of the worlds in Paradise. His saying: 'Which are sounds': the pronoun refers to 'some existents', and the pronoun was feminized due to the annexed term acquiring femininity from the addendum. His saying: 'And our sight is usually related to some existents' means: with the condition of facing and the connection of rays, and it may break the ordinary course as in the vision of the Noble Face of God. His saying: 'Which are bodies': the plural of body, which is that which is composed of two or more individual atoms, and it is that which occupies space and is divisible, and its implication is that the individual atom is not seen, and that is in accordance with the ordinary course, and what the commentary mentioned that the visible is...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the third category of divine attributes: Hearing (sam‘) and Sight (baṣar). The author explains that these two attributes relate to all existents (both eternal and contingent) and that their mode of ‘disclosure’ (inkishāf) differs from that of knowledge (ʿilm) and from each other. Their objects are more specific than the object of knowledge. The author then emphasizes that Allah’s hearing and sight are not like human senses: human hearing is usually restricted to sounds at moderate distances, and human sight to bodies and colors under specific conditions (direction, facing, connection of rays). However, exceptions occur by divine grace, such as Moses hearing God’s eternal speech (non-phonetic) and the believers seeing God’s face in Paradise, or the breaking of the ordinary course in miracles. The passage also includes a grammatical note on the feminine pronoun in ‘al-aṣwāt’ and a definition of ‘body’ (jism) as composed of atoms, implying that the individual atom is not seen in the ordinary course. The text concludes with a reference to the commentary's view on the visible, though it cuts off.

السمع
البصر
تعلُّق انكشاف
الانكشاف بالعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the objects of hearing and sight compare to the object of knowledge?
A) They are identical.
B) They are more general.
C) They are more specific.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Allah’s hearing and sight are comprehensive attributes that disclose all existents, yet their mode of disclosure is distinct from knowledge and from each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's hearing discloses every word you speak, even the ones you whisper to yourself, which conversation today do you most need to repent of? Will you seek His forgiveness now, knowing He heard it all and still invites you to turn back?
?
The passage says Allah's sight discloses every existent, including your actions, thoughts, and intentions. What is one thing you are doing in secret that you would be ashamed to have disclosed? How can you use this awareness to change your behavior starting this hour?
?
Knowing that Allah's hearing and sight are distinct from His knowledge means His awareness of you is intimate and multi-dimensional. Does this truth make you want to hide from Him or run to Him? What is one step you can take today to live as though He sees and hears every moment?

Divine Hearing vs Human Senses and Miraculous Exceptions
Al-Sawi contrasts Allah's hearing and sight with human senses and discusses exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Allah's hearing and sight differ from human senses and that human senses can perceive beyond their ordinary scope by divine power.

Core Idea
Allah hears and sees everything without limit, while human senses are confined to sounds and bodies under normal conditions.

Al-Sawi now contrasts divine hearing and sight with human senses. Human hearing is usually only for sounds that are not too distant, and human sight only for bodies and colors under specific conditions (facing, connected rays). However, by divine intervention, exceptions occur—like Moses hearing God’s eternal speech (without letters or sound) and the vision of God in Paradise. The passage also defines 'body' and notes that the individual atom is not seen in the ordinary course.

Guided Path
1
Read the contrast passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the phrase 'yukhālifān sam‘anā wa baṣaranā fī al-ta‘alluq' – they differ in relation.
The word 'ta‘alluq' is key; it's about the mode of relation, not the sense itself.
2
List human sense limitations
Identify the conditions for human hearing and sight: hearing usually requires sounds not too distant; sight requires facing, connected rays, and applies to bodies and colors.
The author uses '‘ādatan' (usually) to indicate the ordinary course.
3
Study the exceptions
Moses heard God's speech which is not a sound; believers will hear the speech of God in Paradise. Sight can break its ordinary course, like seeing God's face.
These exceptions show that human senses can perceive beyond their normal limits by divine power.
4
Understand the definition of body
The passage defines 'body' (jism) as composed of two or more individual atoms, occupying space and divisible. This implies the individual atom is not seen in the ordinary course.
This relates to the limits of human sight: it cannot see atoms.

?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Allah's Hearing and Sight: Nature and Scope
Explains the disclosure by hearing and sight and their difference from human senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah’s hearing and sight are not limited like human senses, and encompass all existents regardless of category.

Core Idea
Allah’s hearing and sight are unlimited—they perceive all essences and attributes, not just sounds or visible objects.

This passage clarifies the divine attributes of hearing and sight by contrasting them with human sensory experience. It explains that the disclosure through these attributes is unique, and their objects are more specific than knowledge but still universal in scope. Understanding this distinction prevents anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own wording set the foundation.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how the passage distinguishes disclosure (inkishāf) through hearing and sight from disclosure through knowledge. Also note that disclosure through hearing differs from disclosure through sight.
This establishes that each attribute provides a unique type of awareness.
3
Analyze the objects of hearing and sight
The passage states that the objects (mutāʿallaq) of hearing and sight are more specific than the object of knowledge, yet they encompass all essences and attributes, whether sounds or not.
This shows that Allah's hearing and sight are not limited to physical phenomena.
4
Contrast with human senses
Compare the description of human hearing and sight: they are normally restricted to sounds (with conditions) and bodies/colors (with direction and manner). This highlights the vast difference from Allah's attributes.
This guards against anthropomorphism (tashbīh).
5
Summarize the doctrine
Conclude that Allah's hearing and sight are perfect, unrestricted, and encompass all existents in a manner befitting His majesty.
This reinforces tawhid and divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
موجود يُرى بالبناء للمجهول ؛ أي: يعلم؛ أي: معلوم له تعالى قديماً كان كذاته وصفاته، أو حادثاً كذوات المخلوقين وصفاتهم. والانكشاف بهما يغاير الانكشاف بالعلم، وكذا الانكشاف بكلِّ منهما يغاير الانكشاف بالأخرى. ومتعلقهما أخصُّ من متعلَّق العلم، فيسمع ويرى سبحانه الذوات والصفات، كانت من قبيل الأصوات أو من غيرها، فسمعه وبصره تعالى يخالفان سمعنا وبصرنا في التَّعلُّق؛ لأنَّ سمعنا إنَّما يتعلَّق عادةً ببعض الموجودات، وهي الأصوات، بشرط الأصوات، بشرط عدم البعد جداً، وبصرنا إِنَّما يتعلَّق عادةً ببعض الموجودات، وهي الأجسام وألوانها، في جهة مخصوصة على وجه مخصوص .
Existent that is seen (in the passive voice) i.e., known; i.e., known to Him, Exalted, whether eternal like His Essence and attributes, or contingent like the essences of creatures and their attributes. And the disclosure (inkishāf) by them (hearing and sight) differs from disclosure by knowledge, and likewise the disclosure by each of them differs from disclosure by the other. And their objects (mutāʿallaq) are more specific than the object of knowledge, so He, glorified, hears and sees essences and attributes, whether they are of the category of sounds or other than them. So His hearing and His sight, Exalted, differ from our hearing and our sight in relation (taʿalluq); because our hearing normally relates only to some existents, namely sounds, with the condition of not being too distant, and our sight normally relates only to some existents, namely bodies and their colors, in a specific direction and in a specific manner.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage defines the objects of Allah's hearing and sight and contrasts them with human senses. It begins by explaining that the phrase 'existent that is seen' refers to what is known to Allah, whether eternal or contingent. It then states that the disclosure (inkishāf) achieved through hearing and sight is different from that achieved through knowledge, and that each of hearing and sight discloses differently from each other. The objects of hearing and sight are more specific than the object of knowledge. Allah hears and sees essences and attributes, regardless of whether they are sounds or not. The passage then highlights the difference in relation (taʿalluq): human hearing normally perceives only sounds with the condition of not being too distant, and human sight normally perceives only bodies and colors in a specific direction and manner. Thus, Allah's hearing and sight are not limited in these ways.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking Allah's hearing and sight are like human senses, limited to sounds and visible objects.
Correction: Allah's hearing and sight encompass all existents, regardless of type, without the constraints of distance, direction, or physical conditions.

Checkpoint

C) They are limited to sounds and bodies.

Key Takeaway

Exceptions to Human Senses and the Ashʿarī View on Visibility
Commentary on exceptions and visibility, defining body, stating Ash'ari view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that while human senses normally have limitations, Allah can create exceptions, and that the Ash'ari school teaches that both bodies and colors are visible.

Core Idea
Normal sense limits can be broken; both bodies and colors are visible.

This passage from the commentary on Sanusi's work clarifies two important points: first, that human senses are not absolutely bound by their usual objects—Allah can enable perception beyond normal means; second, that the visible world includes both bodies and colors, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Let's examine how the commentator develops these ideas step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand Exceptions to Hearing
Read how hearing can perceive non-sounds through divine exception, citing Moses and Paradise.
Moses heard Allah's eternal speech, which is not letter or sound.
2
Understand Exceptions to Sight
Note that sight normally requires facing and connection of rays, but can breach usual conditions, as in seeing Allah.
3
Define Body and Individual Atom
Learn the definition of jism (body) as composed of two or more jawhar fard (individual atoms), occupying space and divisible.
The individual atom is not seen normally.
4
Grasp the Ash'ari Position on Visibility
The passage states that according to Ahl al-Sunna, the visible includes both bodies and colors, unlike the Mu'tazila who limit visibility to colors.
This is a key sectarian difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لأن سمعنا إنَّما يتعلَّق عادة ... إلخ) : أي : ومن غير العادة قد يتعلق سمعنا بغير الأصوات؛ كسماع موسى لكلام الله القديم الذي ليس بحرف ولا صوت، وكسماعنا كلام ربِّ العالمين في الجنّة. قوله : (وهي الأصوات): الضَّمير لبعض الموجودات، وأنث الضَّمير لاكتساب المضاف التأنيث من المضاف إليه . قوله : (وبصرنا إنَّما يتعلَّق عادة ببعض الموجودات) : بشرط المقابلة واتصال الأشعة، وقد تخرق العادة كما في رؤية وجه الله الكريم. قوله : (وهي الأجسام جمع جسم، وهو ما تركب من جوهرين فردين فأكثر، وهو المتحيّز القابل للقسمة، وقضيَّته أنَّ الجوهر الفرد لا يرى، وهو كذلك بحسب العادة، وما ذكره الشَّرح من أنَّ المرئي هو الأجسام والألوان معاً لا الألوان فقط هو مذهب أهل السُّنَّة، خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين : المرئي الألوان فقط
His saying: (Because our hearing normally relates only... etc.): i.e., and outside of the usual, our hearing may relate to other than sounds, such as the hearing of Moses (Mūsā) for the eternal speech of Allah which is neither letter nor sound, and such as our hearing the speech of the Lord of the Worlds in Paradise. His saying: (namely sounds): the pronoun refers to 'some existents', and the pronoun was feminine due to the annexed noun acquiring femininity from the annexed-to noun. His saying: (And our sight normally relates only to some existents): with the condition of facing (muqābala) and the connection of rays (ittiṣāl al-ashʿaʿ), and it may breach the usual as in seeing the noble face of Allah. His saying: (namely bodies): the plural of jism, which is that which is composed of two or more individual atoms (jawhar fard), and it is that which occupies space (mutaḥayyiz) and is divisible, and its implication is that the individual atom (jawhar fard) is not seen, and that is according to the usual, and what the commentary (sharḥ) mentioned that the visible (marʾī) is bodies and colors together, not colors alone, is the school of Ahl al-Sunna, contrary to the Muʿtazila who say: the visible is only colors.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage provides commentary on the earlier statement about human senses. It begins by explaining exceptions: sometimes human hearing can perceive non-sounds, as when Moses heard Allah's speech (which is not composed of letters or sounds), and the believers in Paradise will hear Allah's speech. Then it comments on the pronoun reference. Next, it notes that human sight normally requires facing and connection of rays, but exceptions occur as in seeing Allah in the afterlife. Then it defines 'bodies' (ajsām) as composed of individual atoms (jawhar fard), occupying space, and divisible. It deduces that the individual atom is not visible in the usual course of things. Finally, it states the Ashʿarī position that both bodies and colors are visible, in contrast to the Muʿtazila who restrict visibility to colors.

الجسم
الجوهر الفرد
المرئي
أهل السنة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human senses are always limited and cannot perceive beyond their normal objects.
Correction: Allah can create exceptions (kharq al-ʿāda), as in Moses hearing Allah’s speech and believers seeing Allah.

Checkpoint

A) Only colors are visible.
B) Only bodies are visible.
C) Both bodies and colors are visible.
D) Neither bodies nor colors are visible.

Key Takeaway
In Ash'ari theology, the visible includes both bodies and colors, and Allah can suspend the normal laws governing sense perception.

The Eternal Nature of Allah's Hearing and Sight
Sanusi explains the essential difference between divine and human hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of hearing and sight are eternal and transcend physical organs, unlike human senses which are created and subsist in specific body parts.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing and sight are eternal; ours are created and physical.

Sanusi emphasizes that Allah's attributes are fundamentally different from human faculties. While our hearing and sight depend on physical organs like the eye and ear, Allah's hearing and sight subsist in His Essence directly. This passage clarifies this difference and also touches on the philosophical understanding of sensory faculties.

Guided Path
1
Contrast divine and human attributes
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi states that Allah's hearing and sight are eternal and subsist in His Essence, while human hearing and sight are created and subsist in specific substrates.
Notice the use of 'qadīm' (eternal) and 'ḥādith' (created).
2
Identify physical substrates for sight and hearing
The passage describes that human sight subsists in the pupil or optic nerves, and human hearing subsists in the ear canal. Find these descriptions and note the anatomical terms used.
These are based on the philosophers' views, not necessarily empirical science.
3
Understand the implications for knowledge and transcendence
Sanusi states that human senses cause knowledge, unlike Allah's attributes, and that Allah is transcendent above physicality. Reflect on why this distinction matters for tanzih.
Also note the mention of Ahl al-Sunna's view on visibility of bodies and colors as a related point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما أنَّهما يخالفان سمعنا وبصرنا أيضاً في الذات، فهما صفتان قدیمتان قائمتان بذاته تعالى، وأمَّا سمعنا وبصرنا فحادثان قائمان بمحل مخصوص : - فبصرنا قائم بإنسان العين أو هو : قوَّة مودعة في العصبتين المجوفتين اللتين يتلاقيان ثم يفترقان، كما هو مذهب الحكماء. - وسمعنا قائم بالصِّماخ ؛ أي: ثقب الأذن، أو هو : قوَّةٌ قائمة بالعصب المفروش في مقعر الصماخ. والله تعالى منزّه عن ذلك، وسمعنا وبصرنا من أسباب علومنا بخلاف سمعه وبصره تعالى . الأجسام والألوان معاً لا الألوان فقط هو مذهب أهل السُّنَّة، خلافاً للمعتزلة القائلين : المرئي الألوان فقط ، قوله : (بإنسان (العين) أي : النُّقطة الصغيرة التي في وسط السواد. قوله : ( مودعة ) : أي : كائنة ومستقرة . قوله : (اللَّتين يتلاقيان) أي: ويتقاطعان تقاطعاً صليبياً، وقيل: يتلاقيان ثم يرجعان ؛ كالدالين المقلوبتين ظهر إحديهما في ظهر الأخرى، فقول الشارح: (ثم يفترقان) مرور على القول الثاني، وهذان القولان للفلاسفة . قوله : (من أسباب علومنا أي فإذا رأينا أو سمعنا شيئاً؛ نعلم بسبب ذلك معاني تقوم بعقولنا
And just as they (hearing and sight) differ from our hearing and our sight also in essence (dhāt), for they (His attributes) are two eternal attributes subsisting in His Essence, Exalted. As for our hearing and our sight, they are two created (ḥādithān) attributes subsisting in a specific substrate (maḥall makhṣūṣ). So our sight subsists in the pupil of the eye (insān al-ʿayn) or it is a power deposited in the two hollow optic nerves that meet then diverge, according to the school of the philosophers (ḥukamāʾ). And our hearing subsists in the ear canal (ṣimākh); i.e., the opening of the ear, or it is a power subsisting in the nerve spread in the concavity of the ear canal. And Allah, Exalted, is transcendent above that. And our hearing and our sight are causes (asbāb) of our knowledge, unlike His hearing and His sight, Exalted. Bodies and colors together, not colors only, is the school of Ahl al-Sunna, contrary to the Muʿtazila who say: the visible is only colors. His saying: (by the pupil of the eye) i.e., the small dot in the middle of the black. His saying: (deposited) i.e., existing and settled. His saying: (the two that meet) i.e., and they intersect in a cross-like intersection, and it is said: they meet then return like two inverted dāls with the back of one against the back of the other; so the commentator's saying: (then they diverge) is based on the second opinion, and these two opinions are of the philosophers. His saying: (from the causes of our knowledge) i.e., so when we see or hear something, we know by that meanings that subsist in our minds.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion by highlighting the difference in essence (dhāt) between Allah's attributes and human senses. It states that Allah's hearing and sight are eternal attributes subsisting in His Essence, while human hearing and sight are created and subsist in specific physical substrates. It then describes the physical location of human sight: either in the pupil or as a power in the optic nerves, according to philosophers. Similarly, human hearing is located in the ear canal or as a power in a nerve. Allah is transcendent above these physical limitations. The passage also notes that human senses are causes of our knowledge, unlike Allah's attributes which are not causes in the same way. It then repeats the Ashʿarī position on visibility, again contrasting with the Muʿtazila. Finally, it provides lexical and anatomical explanations of terms: the pupil (insān al-ʿayn), the word 'deposited' (mūdaʿa), the description of the optic nerves meeting and diverging, and the phrase 'causes of our knowledge' (asbāb ʿulūminā).

Correction: Allah's attributes are eternal and not dependent on physical organs; He is transcendent.

Hearing and Sight: Eternal Attributes beyond Human Senses
Contrasts divine hearing/sight with human senses in essence and locus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes of hearing and sight are eternal and transcend physical organs, unlike human senses which are contingent and depend on bodily structures.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing and sight are eternal; ours are fleeting and tied to nerves and pupils.

The author begins by establishing a sharp contrast between divine and human senses. While our hearing and sight are created and depend on specific physical organs, Allah's hearing and sight are eternal attributes that subsist in His essence without any bodily limitation. This foundational distinction protects the principle of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the detailed anatomical descriptions.
2
Identify the two key contrasts
Look for the contrasts: eternal vs contingent, and subsisting in essence vs subsisting in a specific locus.
These are the main structural elements.
3
Reflect on the anatomical details
Why does the author describe the human eye and ear in such detail? Consider how this emphasizes divine transcendence.
The author uses philosophical knowledge to highlight physical dependence.
4
Conclude with the functional difference
Notice that human senses are causes of knowledge, while divine senses are not. This further distinguishes them.
